Effect of Absolute Prohibition on Legal Acts: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Hanafi vs Shafi'i dispute on absolute prohibition of legal acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the effect of absolute prohibition on legal acts and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the principle of reality and metaphor.

Core Idea
Absolute prohibition of legal acts renders them sinful but does not invalidate their legal structure.

This passage tackles a foundational dispute in usul al-fiqh: when the Lawgiver prohibits a legal act (like a sale or fast), does the act become completely void, or does it retain some legal validity? The Hanafi school argues that the act remains legally valid (مشروع) but becomes ugly (قبيح) due to an external attribute; Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the act becomes inherently evil and therefore invalid. The debate hinges on how we understand the 'reality' of prohibition and the completeness of its effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of prohibition
Distinguish between prohibition of physical acts (e.g., murder) and legal acts (e.g., sale). Note that the first type universally indicates inherent ugliness.
2
State the Hanafi position on prohibition of legal acts
The act remains legally valid (مشروع) but is prohibited due to an external attribute, so it is sinful but not void.
3
State the Shafi'i position and its proof
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that prohibition implies ugliness in the act's essence, just as command implies goodness, so the act becomes invalid and abrogated.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafis respond that the absolute term includes both complete and defective instances; the complete ugliness is in maintaining legal validity while being prohibited, not in complete invalidation. They accuse the Shafi'i view of reversing reality and metaphor.
5
Apply the principle to examples
Use the examples given: fasting on Eid, riba, invalid sales. Hanafis say they are legally valid but prohibited; Shafi'is say they are entirely invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النهي المطلق نوعان: نهي عن الأفعال الحسية، مثل الزنا والقتل، وشرب الخمر. ونهي عن التصرفات الشرعية، مثل الصوم والصلاة، والبيع، والإجارة، وما أشبه ذلك. فالنهي عن الأفعال الحسية دلالة على كونها قبيحةً في أنفسها؛ لمعنى في أعيانها بلا خلاف إلا إذا قام الدليل على خلافه. وأما النهي المطلق عن التصرفات الشرعية: فيقتضي قُبحاً لمعنى في غير المنهي عنه، عنه، لكن متصلاً به حتى يبقى المنهي عنه مشروعاً مع إطلاق النهي وحقيقته. وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : بل يقتضي هذا القسم قبحاً في عينه، حتى لا يبقى مشروعاً أصلاً بمنزلة القسم الأول إلا أن يقوم الدليل، فيجب إثبات ما احتمله النهي وراء حقيقته على اختلاف الأصول. وبيان هذا الأصل في صوم يوم العيد، وأيام التشريق، والربا، والبيوع الفاسدة أنها مشروعة عندنا لأحكامها وعنده باطلة منسوخة، لا حكم لها . احتج الشافعي رحمه الله بأن العمل بحقيقة كل قسم واجب، لا محالة، إذ الحقيقة أصل في كل باب والنهي في اقتضاء القبح حقيقةٌ، كالأمر في اقتضاء الحسن حقيقةً. ثم العمل بحقيقة الأمر واجب، حتى كان حسناً لمعنى في عينه إلا بدليل، فكذلك النهي في صفة القُبح، وهذا لأن المطلق من كل شيء يتناول الكامل منه، ويحتمل القاصر. والكمالُ في صفة القُبح فيما قلنا، فمن قال بأنه يكون مشروعاً في الأصل، قبيحاً في الوصف: يجعله مجازاً في الأصل، حقيقة في الوصف، وهذا عكس الحقيقة، وقلبُ الأصل. وإذا ثبت هذا الأصل كان له لتخريج الفروع طريقان: أحدهما أن ينعدم المشروع باقتضاء النهي. والثاني: أن ينعدم بحكمه. وبيان ذلك : أن من ضرورة كون التصرف مشروعاً : أن يكون مرضياً، قال الله تعالى: ﴿شَرَعَ لَكُم مِّنَ الدِّينِ مَا وَصَّى بِهِ نُوحًا﴾ الشورى / 13، وللمشروعات درجات وأدناها أن تكون مرضيةً، وكون الفعل قبيحاً منهياً : : ينافي هذا الوصف وإن كان داخلاً في المشيئة والقضاء والحكم، كالكفر وسائر المعاصي، فإنها بمشيئة الله تعالى وقضائه وحكمه توجد، لا برضاه، فصار النهي عن هذه التصرفات نسخاً بمقتضاه، وهو التحريم السابق. والثاني: أن من حكم النهي : وجوب الانتهاء، وأن يصير الفعل على خلاف موجبه معصيةً، وهذا موجب حقيقته، وبين كونه معصية وبين كونه مشروعاً وطاعة: تضادُّ ،وتنافِ ولهذا لم تثبت حرمة المصاهرة بالزنا؛
Absolute prohibition is of two types: prohibition of physical acts, such as adultery, murder, and drinking wine. And prohibition of legal acts, such as fasting, prayer, sale, lease, and the like. Thus, prohibition of physical acts indicates that they are inherently ugly, due to a meaning in their essence, without dispute unless evidence indicates otherwise. As for absolute prohibition of legal acts: it implies ugliness due to a meaning other than the prohibited act itself, but connected to it, so that the prohibited act remains legally valid despite the absolute nature and reality of the prohibition. Al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, said: Rather this category implies ugliness in its essence, so that it remains legally valid at all, like the first category, unless evidence [to the contrary] is established, so it is necessary to establish what the prohibition implies beyond its reality, according to the difference in principles. The clarification of this principle is in the case of fasting on the day of Eid, the days of Tashreeq, riba, and invalid sales: they are legally valid according to us regarding their rulings, whereas according to him they are invalid and abrogated, having no ruling. Al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, argued that acting according to the reality of each category is obligatory without doubt, since reality is the origin in every chapter, and prohibition is a reality in implying ugliness, just as command is a reality in implying goodness. Then acting according to the reality of command is obligatory, so that it is good due to a meaning in its essence unless evidence [indicates otherwise]; similarly is prohibition in the attribute of ugliness. This is because the absolute of anything includes the complete of it and admits the defective. And completeness in the attribute of ugliness is in what we have said. Whoever says that it is legally valid in origin and ugly in description makes it metaphorical in origin and real in description, and this is the reverse of reality and a reversal of the origin. And when this principle is established, there are two ways to derive the branches: one is that the legally valid is eliminated by the implication of the prohibition. The second: that it is eliminated by its ruling. The explanation of that: that it is necessary for an act to be legally valid that it be acceptable. Allah Almighty said: 'He has ordained for you of religion what He enjoined upon Noah' (ash-Shura 42:13). And legal acts have degrees, the lowest of which is acceptability. And an act being ugly and prohibited contradicts this attribute, even if it falls under the will, decree, and judgment, like disbelief and all sins, for they exist by the will, decree, and judgment of Allah, not by His pleasure. Thus, the prohibition of these acts became an abrogation by its implication, which is the previous prohibition. And the second: that from the rulings of prohibition is the obligation to desist, and that the act becomes a sin contrary to its requirement, and this is the requirement of its reality. And between it being a sin and being legally valid and an act of obedience is contradiction and mutual opposition. For this reason, the prohibition of marriage by adultery is not established.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of a fundamental dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the nature and effect of absolute prohibition (النهي المطلق) when applied to legal acts (التصرفات الشرعية) such as prayer, fasting, and sales, as opposed to physical acts (الأفعال الحسية) like murder and drinking. The author begins by categorizing prohibition into two types. The first type—prohibition of physical acts—indicates that the act is inherently evil and unlawful, without dispute, unless evidence indicates otherwise. The second type—prohibition of legal acts—is where the disagreement lies. According to the Hanafis, such prohibition implies ugliness due to a meaning external to the act itself but connected to it; consequently, the act remains legally valid (مشروع) in its essence despite being prohibited. Imam al-Shafi'i, however, holds that this type of prohibition renders the act evil in its very essence, making it invalid and abrogated, just like the first type, unless specific evidence indicates otherwise. The text then illustrates the practical difference using examples: fasting on Eid days, riba, and invalid sales. According to the Hanafis, these are legally valid in the sense that they have some legal effects (e.g., a fast that is observed may still require qada, or a sale may be void but still considered a contract), whereas according to al-Shafi'i they are completely invalid and void of any ruling. Imam al-Shafi'i's proof is based on the principle that acting upon the literal reality of each category is obligatory, and prohibition is a reality in implying ugliness just as command is a reality in implying goodness. Since the reality of command requires an act to be good unless evidence shows otherwise, similarly the reality of prohibition requires an act to be ugly. The author then presents the Hanafi response: the absolute (مطلق) of anything encompasses both the complete and the defective. The complete embodiment of ugliness in prohibition is what the Hanafis assert—that the act remains legally valid but is ugly in its description. If one were to claim that the act is invalid from the origin, that would make the origin (the legal validity) metaphorical and the description (the ugliness) real, which reverses the priority and is untenable. After establishing this principle, the text outlines two ways in which the prohibition affects the act: (1) the act's legal validity is negated by the implication of the prohibition, or (2) the ruling of the act is negated. The first way is explained by noting that a legally valid act must be acceptable (مرضية) to Allah, as indicated by the Quranic verse (42:13). However, an ugly and prohibited act contradicts this attribute of acceptability, even though it may exist under Allah's will and decree (like disbelief and sins). Thus, the prohibition amounts to an abrogation of the previous permissibility or obligation. The second way highlights that prohibition necessitates desistance and that performing the act becomes a sin, which is contradictory to the act being a valid act of obedience. This is why, for example, illicit sexual relations do not establish the prohibition of marriage affinity (i.e., the prohibition of marrying relatives by adulterous relations does not occur, unlike lawful marriage). The passage thus carefully defends the Hanafi position that prohibited legal acts are not null and void but retain some legal validity while being sinful.

Key Terms
النهي المطلق (al-nahy al-mutlaq): Absolute prohibition without qualification; it is categorized into prohibition of physical acts and prohibition of legal acts.
التصرف الشرعي (al-tasarruf al-shar'i): A legally recognized act such as prayer, fasting, sale, lease, etc.
قبيح (qabih): Something considered ugly, evil, or prohibited by Shariah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the effect of an absolute prohibition on a legal act like a sale?
A) The sale becomes completely invalid with no legal effects.
B) The sale remains legally valid in its structure but is considered sinful.
C) The sale is valid only if the prohibition is not absolute.
D) The sale becomes unlawful but its effects are retroactively nullified.

Key Takeaway
Absolute prohibition of legal acts renders them sinful but does not invalidate their legal structure — a core Hanafi usul principle distinguishing external and inherent ugliness.

Repeated Indefinite Nouns: Hanafi Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Presenting ikhtilaf on whether second indefinite noun refers to same entity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on repeated indefinite nouns and apply the dispute to a legal acknowledgment scenario.

Core Idea
A second indefinite noun refers to a different entity unless the same session or custom indicates continuity.

In Hanafi usul, interpreting repeated indefinite nouns (nakira) is crucial for legal statements like acknowledgments or vows. This lesson presents an internal debate: does the second mention refer to the same or a different entity? Understanding this rule sharpens your ability to analyze textual ambiguity and appreciate how custom (adah) competes with contextual unity (majlis) as a legal indicator.

Guided Path
1
Understand the baseline
Start with the default rule: indefinite nouns are indefinite. According to Abu Hanifa, two indefinite nouns refer to different entities by default.
This baseline is rooted in the principle that indefinite nouns lack a specific referent.
2
Learn Abu Hanifa's exception
Abu Hanifa makes an exception: if the two occurrences happen in the same session (majlis), then the second does refer to the same entity, because the context of the session provides a sense of prior reference (ahd).
The unity of session acts as a contextual definiteness.
3
Learn the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the second indefinite noun always refers to the same entity, regardless of whether the session is the same or different. Their proof is custom (adah): people in normal speech repeat an indefinite noun to mean the same thing.
Custom overrides the default presumption of disjunction.
4
Compare the proofs
Abu Hanifa's proof is istishab al-adam (presumption of original absence) and the lack of definite reference without contextual unity. The Sahibayn's proof is that custom (adah) indicates the meaning of ahd, which is strong enough to bind the two occurrences.
This is a clash between linguistic presumption and customary practice.
5
Apply to thamarah
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) appears in legal acknowledgments: if someone says 'I owe you a thousand' in two different sessions, Abu Hanifa counts two separate debts; the Sahibayn count one debt.
This illustrates how a small usul rule can produce significantly different fiqhi outcomes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كان كل واحد منهما نكرة : كان الثاني غير الأول عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله ، إلا أن يتحد المجلس، فيصير دلالةً على معنى العهد. وعند أبي يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله : يُحمَلُ الثاني على الأول وإن اختلف المجلس ؛ لدلالة العادة على معنى العهد.
And when each of the two is indefinite, then the second is other than the first according to Abu Hanifa, unless the session is the same, so it becomes an indication of the meaning of al-ahd. And according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: the second is carried upon the first even if the session differs, due to the indication of custom (al-‘adah) upon the meaning of al-ahd.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute (ikhtilaf) within the Hanafi school regarding the interpretation of repeated indefinite nouns. The author, al-Bazdawi, states the general rule: when two nouns are both indefinite (nakira), the second does not refer to the same entity as the first according to Imam Abu Hanifa. However, there is an exception: if the two occurrences happen in the same session (majlis), then the second does indicate the same entity, because the context of the same session gives a sense of 'ahd (previous reference). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) disagree: they hold that the second is always carried upon the first (i.e., refers to the same entity) regardless of whether the session is the same or different. Their reasoning is that custom (al-‘adah) indicates the meaning of 'ahd; in normal usage, when someone repeats an indefinite noun, it is understood to refer to the same thing. This ikhtilaf has practical implications in legal documents and testimony.

Key Terms
نكرة (nakirah): An indefinite noun that does not refer to a specific entity by itself.
المجلس (al-majlis): A session or context in which a statement is made; unity of majlis can give definiteness to an indefinite noun.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The noun must be defined by a definite article.
C) The speaker must intend the same thing.
D) Custom must indicate reference.

Key Takeaway
The reference of repeated indefinite nouns depends on contextual unity (Abu Hanifa) or customary practice (Sahibayn), with practical impact on legal obligations.

The Dual (Muthanna) as a Distinct Category in Hanafi Usul
Proving the dual is a distinct category from singular and plural with legal implications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that the dual (muthanna) is a distinct grammatical and legal category, and identify the key proofs (hadith, ijma, rational) that support it.

Core Idea
The dual is not a subset of the plural—it has its own unique form, ruling, and legal effect.

In Hanafi usul, the dual (muthanna) is treated as a separate category from both singular and plural. This may seem like a purely grammatical point, but it has direct legal consequences: for example, the imam steps forward if two are behind him just as if three were present, but not if only one. In this lesson, we will examine the evidence that establishes the dual as an independent division of speech and law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the Hanafi position
The central question is whether the dual is a distinct category or merely a subtype of the plural. The Hanafi school firmly holds that it is a separate division, with its own unique form (invariant -ān/-ayn suffix) and independent legal rulings.
2
Examine the first three proofs: hadith, consensus of linguists, and consensus of jurists
The hadith 'One is a devil, two are devils, three are a riding party' explicitly distinguishes one, two, and three. Linguists agree that speech divides into singular, dual, and plural. Jurists agree that the imam steps forward for two but not for one—showing two has its own ruling.
These proofs are from the text: the hadith is a Sunnah ahad proof; the linguistic consensus is ijma; the juristic consensus is also ijma.
3
Consider the rational argument and the response to counterarguments
The rational argument: adding one to one produces a pair, not a collective plural; three is the first number where unity is completely lost. The Hanafi then refutes opposing proofs: the Quranic dual in surah al-Tahrim is an exception for paired organs; the usage of 'we' for two is a linguistic concession; the hadith about devils is interpreted narrowly for inheritances or early travel bans.
This step shows how Hanafi usul handles apparent counter-evidence through takhsis and ta'wil.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and the tarjih
The practical difference: in prayer, the imam steps forward for two just as for three, but not for one. In inheritance, two daughters get two-thirds as a specific share. The Hanafi position is mufta bihi because the multiple proofs reinforce each other.
The tarjih is in favor of the dual as a distinct category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويستعمل المثنى استعمال الجمع اللغة، يقال: نحن فعلنا الاثنين. وقد قال الله تعالى: ﴿فَقَدْ صَغَتْ قُلُوبُكُمَا﴾ [التحريم / ٤]. ولا خلاف أن الإمام يتقدم إذا كان خلفه اثنان. وفي المثنى اجتماع، كما في الثلاثة. ولنا قول النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام : «الواحد شيطان، والاثنان شیطانان، والثلاثة ركب». ولنا أيضاً دليل من قبل الإجماع، ودليل من قبل المعقول. أما من قبل الإجماع : فإن أهل اللغة مُجمعون على أن الكلام ثلاثة أقسام : آحاد ،ومثنى وجمع، وعلى ذلك بنيت أحكام اللغة. فللمثنى صيغة خاصةً لا تختلف. وللوحدان أبنية مختلفة. وكذلك الجمع أيضاً تختلف أبنيته ، وليس للمثنى إلا مثال واحد، وله علامات على الخصوص. وأجمع الفقهاء أن الإمام لا يتقدم على الواحد، فثبت أنه قسم منفرد. وأما المعقول : فإن الواحد إذا أُضيف إليه الواحد : تعارض الفردان، فلم يثبت الاتحاد، ولا الجمع. وأما الثلاثة، فإنما يعارض كلّ فرد اثنان فسقط معنى الاتحاد أصلاً. وقد جعل الثلاثة في الشرع حداً في إبلاء الأعذار. فأما الحديث فمحمول على المواريث والوصايا، أو على سنة تقدم الإمام في الجماعة أنه يتقدم على الاثنين كما يتقدم على الثلاثة. وفي المواريث ثبت الاختصاص بقوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِن كَانَتَا اثْنَتَيْنِ فَلَهُمَا الثُّلْتَانِ مِمَّا تَرَكَ﴾ [النساء / ١٧٦]. والحجبُ يُبتنى على الإرث أيضاً، والوصية تبتنى عليه أيضاً. والثاني : قلنا إن الخبر محمول على ابتداء الإسلام، حيث نهى الواحد عن المسافرة. وأطلق الجماعة على ما روينا، فإذا ظهر قوة المسلمين قال: الاثنان فما فوقهما جماعة. وأما الجماعة: فإنما تكمل بالإمام حتى شَرَطنا في الجمعة ثلاثةً سوى الإمام. وأما قوله تعالى: ﴿فَقَدْ صَغَتْ قُلُوبُكُمَا﴾ [التحريم / ٤] : فلأن عامة أعضاء الإنسان زوج، فألحق الفرد بالزوج؛ لعظم منفعته، كأنه زوج. وقد جاء في اللغة خلاف ذلك. وقولهم: نحن فعلنا لا يصح إلا من واحد يحكي عن نفسه وعن غيره، كأنه تابع له، فلم يستقم أن يُفردا بصيغته، فاختير لهما الجمع
And the dual is used in the language the usage of the plural; one says: 'We did' for two. And Allah the Exalted said: 'Indeed, your hearts have inclined' (referring to two). And there is no disagreement that the imam steps forward when there are two behind him. And in the dual there is a gathering, just as in the three. And we have the statement of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'One is a devil, two are two devils, and three are a riding party.' And we also have a proof from consensus, and a proof from reason. As for consensus: the linguists are agreed that speech is three divisions: singular, dual, and plural, and upon that the rulings of the language are built. For the dual there is a specific form that does not vary. For the singular there are different patterns. Similarly, the plural also has varying patterns, and the dual has only one example, and it has distinctive markers. And the jurists are agreed that the imam does not step forward in front of one, so it is established that it (the dual) is an independent category. As for reason: when one is added to one, the two individuals oppose each other, so neither unity nor plurality is established. As for three, each individual is opposed by two, so the meaning of unity drops entirely. And three has been made in the Shariah a limit in the delegation of excuses. As for the hadith, it is interpreted in the context of inheritances and bequests, or upon the practice that the imam in congregation steps forward for two just as he steps forward for three. And in inheritances, the specification is established by His statement: 'If they are two, then for them is two-thirds of what he leaves.' And the blocking (from inheritance) is also based on inheritance, and the bequest is also based upon it. Alternatively, we say that the hadith is interpreted in the early period of Islam, when the single person was prohibited from traveling. And the congregation is considered to be as we have narrated; when the strength of the Muslims appeared, he said: 'Two and above are a congregation.' And as for the congregation, it is only completed with the imam, to the extent that we conditioned in Friday prayer three apart from the imam. And as for His statement: 'Indeed, your hearts have inclined' (in the dual), it is because most of the human organs are pairs, so the singular is joined to the pair due to the greatness of its benefit, as if it were a pair. And the opposite has occurred in the language. And their saying: 'We did' is only valid from one who narrates about himself and about another, as if he is a follower of him, so it was not appropriate that they be singled out with his form, so the plural was chosen for them.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul establishes that the dual (muthanna) is a distinct grammatical and legal category, separate from both singular and plural. The author begins by acknowledging that the dual is sometimes used like a plural in language (e.g., 'we did' for two people) but argues this is not the norm. He then presents a series of proofs: First, a hadith stating that one is a devil, two are devils, but three are a riding party, implying that the dual has its own status. Second, consensus of linguists that speech has three divisions—singular, dual, plural—each with its own forms; the dual has a unique, invariant form. Third, consensus of jurists that the imam does not step forward when only one is behind him, but does for two or more, showing the dual is distinct. Fourth, a rational argument: adding one to one results in a pair, not a collective plural; three is the first number where singularity is completely lost. The author then addresses counterarguments: the hadith about one and two being devils is interpreted narrowly to apply to inheritances and bequests, or to the early Islamic practice where solo travel was discouraged. The Quranic verse (al-Tahrim 4) uses a dual verb for two hearts, but this is explained as an exception due to the paired nature of organs. The phrase 'we did' for two is a linguistic concession where the dual is treated as plural for rhetorical reasons. All these show that the dual retains its own independent legal category in Hanafi usul.

Key Terms
المثنى (al-muthannā): The linguistic form indicating exactly two, with a unique invariant suffix (e.g., -ān/-ayn).
الجمع (al-jam‘): The linguistic form indicating three or more, with varying patterns.
الإمامة (al-imāmah): Leadership in congregational prayer; the ruling that the imam may step forward with at least two followers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi argument in this passage, which of the following is the primary evidence that the dual (muthanna) is a distinct category from the plural?
A) The Quranic verse 'Indeed, your hearts have inclined' using a dual verb.
B) The linguistic usage of 'we' for two people.
C) The hadith: 'One is a devil, two are devils, three are a riding party.'
D) The absence of a specific term for dual in Arabic.

Key Takeaway
The dual (muthanna) is an independent division in Hanafi usul, proven by hadith, linguistic consensus, juristic practice, and rational argument.

Isti'arah and the Validity of Marriage Contract with Transfer Words
Arguing that marriage can be contracted with sale/gift words using metaphorical extension based on causal connection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of isti'arah to evaluate whether a marriage contract can be validly concluded with sale/gift words, and identify the two types of connection (ittisal) that determine the permissibility of metaphorical extension.

Core Idea
Marriage is valid with sale words because causal connection between ownership of person and benefit enables isti'arah.

The passage addresses a classic usul dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'is over the required wording for the marriage contract. The Shafi'i school insists on specific words (nikah/tazwij) due to the unique nature of the contract. The Hanafi school, through the usul tool of isti'arah (metaphorical extension), argues that words like 'sale' and 'gift' can validly effect marriage because they share a causal link with the core objective of marriage: ownership of benefit (milk al-mut'a) stemming from ownership of the person (milk al-raqaba). This lesson unpacks the Hanafi reasoning and the crucial distinction between types of connections that governs the applicability of isti'arah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
What is the exact point of disagreement between Hanafi and Shafi'i regarding marriage wording?
2
State the Shafi'i position and its rationale
Why does al-Shafi'i restrict marriage to nikah/tazwij? What is his analogy to testimony and partnership?
3
Analyze the Hanafi response using isti'arah
How does the Hanafi school invoke metaphorical extension? What is the causal connection (ittisal) they identify between milk al-raqaba and milk al-mut'a?
4
Distinguish the two types of connection
The text differentiates between connection of ruling to cause ('illa) and connection of branch to pure cause (sabab). How does this distinction determine whether isti'arah is valid from one side or both sides?
5
Apply the principle to a counter-example
Why is isti'arah from nikah to sale not valid according to this framework? (mutual appropriateness is lacking)

ولا اختصاص للرسالة بالاستعارة ووجوه الكلام، بل الناس في وجوه التكلم سواء، فثبت أن هذا فصل لا خلاف فيه.
غير أن الشافعي رحمه الله أبى أن ينعقد النكاح إلا بلفظ النكاح أو التزويج؛ لأنه عقد شرع لأمور لا تحصى من مصالح الدين والدنيا، ولهذا شرع بهذين اللفظين.
وليس فيهما معنى التمليك، بل فيهما إشارة إلى ما قلنا فلم يصح الانتقال عنه؛ لقصور اللفظ عن اللفظ الموضوع له في الباب.
وهذا معنى قولهم: عقد خاص شرع بلفظ خاص.
وهذا كلفظ الشهادة، لما كان موجباً بنفسه بقوله: أشهد لم تقم اليمين مقامه، وهو أن يقول أحلف بالله؛ لأنه موجب بغيره، فلم تصلح الاستعارة.
وكذلك عقد المفاوضة لا ينعقد إلا بلفظ المفاوضة عندكم. كذلك حكي عن الكرخي؛ لأن غيره لا يؤدي معناه، ولهذا لم يجوزوا رواية الأحاديث بالمعاني.
والجواب: أن لفظ البيع والهبة وضع لملك الرقبة، وملك الرقبة سبب لملك المتعة؛ لأن ملك المتعة يثبت به تبعاً، فإذا كان كذلك قام هذا الاتصال مقام ما ذكرنا من المجاورة التي هي طريق الاستعارة، فصحت الاستعارة بهذا الاتصال بين السببين والحكمين.
والجواب عما قال: إن هذه الأحكام من حيث هي غير محصورة جعلت فروعاً وثمرات للنكاح، وبني النكاح على حكم الملك له عليها؛ لأنه أمر معقول معلوم.
وهو ألا يرى أن المهر يلزمه بالعقد لها، ولو كان ما ذكرت أصلاً صح إيجاب العوض على أحدهما ولذلك كان الطلاق بيد الزوج؛ لأنه المالك.
وإذا كان كذلك قلنا: لما شرع هذا الحكم بلفظ النكاح والتزويج، ولا يختصان بالملك وضعاً ولغةً، فلأن يثبت بلفظ التمليك والبيع والهبة وهي للتمليك وضعاً أولى.
وإنما صلح الإيجاب بلفظ النكاح والتزويج وإن لم يوضعا للملك؛ لأنهما اسمان جعلا علماً لهذا الحكم والعلم يعمل وضعاً لا بمعناه، بمنزلة النص في دلائل الشرع.
وإنما تعتبر المعاني لصحة الاستعارة على نحو ما تستعمل للقياس، فلما ثبت الملك بهما وضعاً صحت التعدية به إلى ما هو صريح في التمليك.
فإن قيل: فهلا صحت استعارة النكاح للبيع والمناسبة التي ذكرتم قائمة؛ لأنها تقوم بالطرفين جميعاً لا محالة؛ لأنه لا يناسب الشيء غير إلا وذلك يناسبه كالأخوين.
قيل له: الاتصال من هذا الوجه على نوعين: أحدهما اتصال الحكم بالعلة، والثاني اتصال الفرع بما هو سبب محض ليس بعلة وضعت له. فالأول يجب الاستعارة من الطرفين؛ لأن العلة لم تشرع إلا
And there is no exclusivity of [the Prophet's] message to metaphor (isti'arah) and modes of speech; rather, people are equal in modes of speech. So it is established that this is a point of no disagreement. However, al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) refused that marriage be contracted except with the word 'nikah' or 'tazwij', because it is a contract legislated for countless matters of religious and worldly benefits, and thus it was legislated with these two words. And they do not contain the meaning of transfer of ownership (tamlik), rather they indicate what we stated, so transfer from them is not valid due to the deficiency of the word compared to the word prescribed for this topic. This is the meaning of their statement: 'A special contract, legislated with a special word.' This is like the word of testimony (shahada); since it is effective by itself with the statement 'I testify', an oath does not take its place, that is, saying 'I swear by Allah' because it is effective by something else, so metaphor (isti'arah) is not valid. Similarly, the contract of partnership (mufawada) is only contracted with the word 'mufawada' according to you; thus it is narrated from al-Karkhi because other words do not convey its meaning, and therefore they did not permit narrating hadith by meaning. The answer: The words 'sale' (bay') and 'gift' (hiba) were coined for ownership of the person (milk al-raqaba), and ownership of the person is a cause for ownership of benefit (milk al-mut'a) because ownership of benefit is established through it as a consequence. Therefore, this connection (ittisal) takes the place of what we mentioned of adjacency (mujawara) which is the path of metaphor, so metaphor is valid through this connection between the two causes and the two rulings. And the answer to what he said: 'These rulings, insofar as they are not restricted, are made branches and fruits of marriage, and marriage is built on the ruling of ownership (milk) over her' because it is an intelligible known matter. And that is that you see that the dowry (mahr) is obligated by the contract for her, and if what you mentioned were original, it would be valid to impose compensation on one of them, and therefore divorce is in the husband's hand because he is the owner. And if that is the case, we say: Since this ruling was legislated with the word 'nikah' and 'tazwij', and they are not specific to ownership in coinage and language, then it is more appropriate that it be established with the word of transfer (tamlik), sale, and gift, which are for transfer by coinage. And the obligation (ijab) was only valid with the words 'nikah' and 'tazwij' even though they were not coined for ownership because they are names made as indicators (alam) for this ruling, and the indicator operates by coinage, not by its meaning, like the explicit text (nass) in the proofs of the Law. And meanings are considered for the validity of metaphor in the manner they are used for analogy (qiyas). So when ownership is established by them through coinage, extension (ta'diya) through them to what is explicit in transfer is valid. If it is said: 'Why is metaphor of nikah for sale not valid while the appropriateness you mentioned exists? Because it applies to both sides necessarily; for a thing is not appropriate to another except that the other is appropriate to it, like two brothers.' It is said to him: The connection (ittisal) from this aspect is of two types: One: connection of the ruling to the cause ('illa). The second: connection of the branch to what is a pure cause (sabab), not a cause ('illa) placed for it. The first necessitates metaphor from both sides; because the cause was only legislated...
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a detailed Hanafi response to the Shafi'i position that the marriage contract can only be concluded using the specific words 'nikah' or 'tazwij'. The author begins by noting that there is no disagreement that modes of speech are general, but al-Shafi'i restricts marriage to those two words because he considers it a special contract requiring special wording. The Hanafi counter-argument uses the usul principle of isti'arah (metaphorical extension). The author states that words like 'sale' and 'gift' are coined for ownership of the person (milk al-raqaba), which is a cause (sabab) for ownership of benefit (milk al-mut'a) — the very purpose of marriage. This causal connection (ittisal) allows using those words metaphorically for marriage, similar to how adjacency (mujawara) enables metaphor. The author also gives examples from testimony and partnership to illustrate when metaphor is not allowed. He then addresses objections about the one-sided nature of appropriateness, explaining that there are two types of connections: one between ruling and cause (which requires mutual appropriateness), and another between a branch and a pure cause (which does not). In marriage, the connection is of the second type, so metaphor from sale words to marriage is valid. The passage is a classic example of Hanafi reliance on meaning and causal relationships in contract law, contrasting with the Shafi'i emphasis on specific wording.

Key Terms
استعارة (isti'arah): Metaphorical extension of a word from its original meaning to a related meaning based on a connection (ittisal) between the two, such as using a word coined for one thing to refer to another due to a shared cause or adjacency.
تمليك (tamlik): The act of transferring ownership; the legal effect of words like 'I sell' or 'I give' which establish ownership of the thing transferred.
ملك الرقبة (milk al-raqaba): Ownership of the person (the spouse) itself, as opposed to merely the usufruct. In marriage, the husband acquires milk al-raqaba over the wife in a limited sense, which is the basis for his right to her conjugal companionship (milk al-mut'a).
ملك المتعة (milk al-mut'a): Ownership of the right to enjoy the spouse's companionship, which is the primary purpose of the marriage contract.
اتصال (ittisal): Connection or link between two things, such as between a cause (sabab) and its effect, or between a ruling and its cause. In this passage, itt isal is the basis for allowing isti'arah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do Hanafis hold that marriage can be contracted with the word 'sale' (بيع) according to the text?
A) Because sale and marriage are inherently similar contracts.
B) Because the word 'sale' is specifically mentioned as permissible in the Quran.
C) Because ownership of the person (milk al-raqaba) is a cause for ownership of benefit (milk al-mut'a), creating a connection that allows isti'arah.
D) Because there is no requirement for specific wording in marriage according to general permissibility.

Key Takeaway
The validity of using non-standard wording in Hanafi contracts hinges on the presence of a causal connection (ittisal) between the word's original meaning and the contract's purpose, allowing metaphorical extension (isti'arah).

Majaz as Khalaf: The Dispute Between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn
Explaining the nature of majaz as a substitute and the ikhtilaf on its legal effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's definitions of majaz as a substitute and apply each view to examples like paternity claims and oaths.

Core Idea
Majaz substitutes in speech, not ruling for Abu Hanifa; Sahibayn require original meaning possible for substitute to take effect.

This lesson delves into the nature of majaz as a substitute (khalaf) for haqiqah. The Hanafi masters disagreed on whether the substitution occurs in the act of speech or in the legal ruling itself. Understanding this disagreement is crucial for deriving rulings on manumission claims, oaths, and contracts where metaphorical language is used.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State Abu Hanifa's view that majaz is a substitute in speech (takallum) and the Sahibayn's view that it is a substitute in ruling (hukm).
Recall the key definition: khalaf means substitute; the disagreement is about what it substitutes for.
2
Analyze the older-slave example
Apply each position to the case of a man claiming paternity of an older slave. Determine manumission under each view and note why the Sahibayn deem the statement void.
The Sahibayn require the original (paternity) to be possible; since it is impossible, the statement is futile.
3
Examine oath analogies
Compare the touch-the-sky oath (possible in principle) with the ghamus oath (impossible from outset). See how the condition of probability (ihtimal) applies only to the substitute-in-ruling view.
For Abu Hanifa, the probability condition is irrelevant because substitution is in speech.
4
Consider marriage by gift example
Analyze why the Sahibayn accept marriage with the word 'gift' for a free woman but not for claiming paternity of an older slave. Understand how the two views diverge on the role of the object's possibility.
Abu Hanifa argues that the speech act itself is valid regardless of the object's probability.
5
Synthesize the thamarah al-khilaf
List the practical differences between the two views in manumission, oaths, and contracts. Identify which view is relied upon (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school.
The text indicates Abu Hanifa's view is the relied-upon position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقال : عَتَقَ الطير: إذا قوي وطار عن وكره، ومنه: عتاق الطير، ويقال : عَتَقَت البكر : إذا أدركت، وهذا شائع في كلام العرب.

وكذلك الرق ثابت على الكمال، وسلطان المالكية ساقط، فصح الإعتاق إثباتاً.
وليس بين إزالة القيد لتعمل القوة الشرعية عملها، وبين إثباتها بعد العدم مشابهة، كما ليس بين إحياء الميت وبين إطلاق الحي مشابهة، فما هذا إلا كمَن استعار الحمار للذكي، والأسد للجبان.
فإن قيل: أليس لا يصح أن يُستعار البيع للإجارة، كما لا تُستعار الإجارة للبيع، وملك المنفعة تابع لملك الرقبة.
قيل له : قد قال بعض مشايخنا رحمهم الله : إن البيع لا ينعقد بلفظ الإجارة، والإجارة تنعقد به وذلك يُتصوّر فى الحر، تقول: بعتُ نفسي منك شهراً بدرهم العمل كذا، وهذا جائز. فأما إذا قال: بعث منك منافع هذه الدارِ شهراً بكذا: لم يجز. كذا ذكره في أول كتاب الصلح.
وهذا ليس لفساد في الاستعارة لكن لفساد في المحل ؛ لأن المنفعة لا تصلح محلاً للإضافة ؛ لأن ذلك معدوم ليس في مقدور البشر. حتى لو أضاف إليها الإجارة : لم يجز، فكذلك ما يُستعار لها، ولكن العينَ أُقيمت مقامها في حق الإضافة في الأصل، فكذلك فيما يُستعار لها، وصار هذا كالبيع يُستعار للنكاح في غير محله وهي المَحرَم من النساء، فثبت أن فساده لإضافته إلى غير محله.

ومن أحكام هذا القسم أيضاً : أن المجازَ خَلَفٌ عن الحقيقة في حق التكلم ، لا في حق الحكم عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله.
وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله : هو خَلَفٌ عن الحكم.
بيانه فيمن قال لعبده وهو أكبر سنّاً منه : هذا ابني : لم يعتق عندهما ؛ لأن هذا الكلام لم ينعقد لِمَا وُضع له أصلاً ، فصار لغواً، لا حكم له، فلا يجب العمل بمجازه ؛ لأنه خَلَفٌ عنه في إثبات الحكم، ومِن شَرط الخَلَف : أن ينعقد السبب للأصل على الاحتمال، وامتنع وجوده بعارض. كمن حلف: ليمسَّنَّ السماء: أن اليمين انعقدت للبر؛ لاحتمال وجوده، فانعقدت للكفارة خلفاً عنه، فأما الغموس: فلم تنعقد للحكم الأصلي، وهو البِرُّ ، فلا تنعقد لخلفه، وهذا نظير مسألة الغموس. وقال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله : المجاز خلفٌ عن الحقيقة في التكلم، لا في الحكم، بل هو في حق الحكم أصل.
ألا يُرى أن العبارة تتغير به، دون الحكم، فكان تصرُّفاً في التكلم، فتشترط صحة الأصل من حيث إنه مبتدأ وخبر موضوع للإيجاب بصيغته، وقد وجد ذلك، فإذا وجد وتعذر العمل بحقيقته وله مجاز متعين: صار مستعاراً لحكمه بغير نية، كالنكاح بلفظ الهبة.
وقالا لفظ الهبة ينعقد لحكمه الأصلي في الحرة؛ لأن احتمال بيع الحرة وهبتها مثل احتمال مس السماء، وأما هذا فمستحيل بمرة. وقال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله : هذا تصرُّفٌ في التكلم، فلا يتوقف على احتمال الحكم، كالاستثناء، فإن من قال لامرأته: أنت طالق ألفاً إلا
It is said: The bird became free (ataqa) when it became strong and flew from its nest, and from that [comes] 'ataq al-tayr (free bird). And it is said: The virgin became free when she reached maturity, and this is common in the speech of the Arabs.

And similarly, slavery is established in its perfection, and the authority of ownership is removed, so the manumission is valid as an affirmation.
There is no similarity between removing the shackle so that the legal force can operate and affirming it after non-existence, just as there is no similarity between reviving the dead and releasing the living. This is nothing like one who borrows the donkey for the intelligent and the lion for the coward.
If it is said: Is it not that sale cannot be metaphorically used for hire, just as hire cannot be metaphorically used for sale, and the right to usufruct follows the right to the corpus?
It is said to him: Some of our teachers (may Allah have mercy on them) have said that sale does not contract with the word of hire, but hire contracts with it, and that is conceivable in a free person: you say 'I sold myself to you for a month for a dirham for such work' and this is permissible. But if he said 'I sold to you the usufruct of this house for a month for such and such' it is not permissible. This is mentioned at the beginning of the Book of Compromise.
This is not due to a defect in the metaphor but due to a defect in the locus; because the usufruct is not a suitable locus for attribution, because it is non-existent and not within human capability. So even if one attributes hire to it, it is not permissible, and likewise what is metaphorically used for it. But the [tangible] object has been put in its place in terms of attribution in the original, so likewise in what is metaphorically used for it. This becomes like sale being metaphorically used for marriage in its improper place, i.e., a prohibited woman, so it is established that its invalidity is due to attributing it to an improper place.

And among the rulings of this section also is that metaphor (majaz) is a substitute for the literal meaning (haqiqah) in terms of speech, not in terms of ruling according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him).
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) said: It is a substitute in terms of the ruling.
Its explanation: If someone says to his slave who is older than him 'This is my son' — it does not cause manumission according to them, because this speech was not contracted for its original meaning at all, so it becomes futile, no ruling for it, so its metaphorical meaning must not be acted upon because it is a substitute for it in affirming the ruling. And among the conditions of the substitute is that the cause for the original must be contracted with probability, and its occurrence was prevented by an obstacle. Like one who swears: 'I will surely touch the sky' — the oath is contracted for fulfillment due to the possibility of its occurrence, so it is contracted for expiation as a substitute for it. As for the false oath (al-ghamus), it is not contracted for the original ruling, which is fulfillment, so it is not contracted for its substitute. This is analogous to the issue of the false oath.
Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Metaphor is a substitute for the literal meaning in speech, not in the ruling; rather, in terms of the ruling it is an original.
Do you not see that the expression changes with it while the ruling does not? So it is a disposal in speech. Thus, the validity of the original is required in that it is a subject and predicate formulated for affirmation in its form, and that has been found. So when it is found and acting upon its literal meaning is impossible and it has a specified metaphorical meaning, it becomes metaphorically used for its ruling without intention, like marriage with the word 'gift'.
They (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: The word 'gift' contracts for its original ruling in a free woman because the possibility of selling a free woman and gifting her is like the possibility of touching the sky. As for this (the case of the older slave), it is impossible entirely.
Abu Hanifa said: This is a disposal in speech, so it does not depend on the probability of the ruling, like exception (istithna), for if a man says to his wife 'You are divorced a thousand times except...'
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of majaz (metaphorical usage) as a substitute (khalaf) for haqiqah (literal meaning), and the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), on whether it is a substitute in the act of speech or in the legal ruling.

The passage begins with linguistic examples of the root 'ataqa to illustrate the literal and metaphorical uses. Then it sets up a discussion about the validity of metaphorical usage in contracts, using the example of borrowing terms for hire and sale. The author argues that invalidity of a metaphor can arise from a defect in the locus (mahall) rather than in the metaphor itself, as with usufruct.

The core of the passage is the ruling that majaz is a substitute for haqiqah, but in what sense? Abu Hanifa holds it is a substitute in speech (fi haqq al-takallum), not in ruling (fi haqq al-hukm). According to him, the metaphorical expression is valid if the speech act is properly formed, even if the literal meaning is impossible. The Sahibayn hold it is a substitute in ruling, requiring that the original meaning be possible in principle, otherwise the metaphorical effect does not take place.

The dispute is illustrated with three examples:
1. A man says to his older slave 'This is my son' — according to the Sahibayn, this does not cause manumission because the statement is impossible for its literal meaning (paternity), so the metaphorical meaning (manumission by claim) cannot be a substitute. For Abu Hanifa, the expression is a valid speech act, and when the literal meaning is impossible, the metaphorical meaning takes effect, causing manumission.
2. An oath to touch the sky — this oath is contracted for fulfillment (al-birr) because it is theoretically possible (e.g., by climbing), so if broken, the substitute (expiation) applies. In contrast, a false oath (al-ghamus) is not contracted for fulfillment at all, so no substitute applies.
3. Marriage with the word 'gift' (hiba) — for the Sahibayn, this is valid for a free woman because selling or giving her is possible, but for an older slave claiming paternity, it is impossible. Abu Hanifa argues that it is a verbal transaction, independent of the possibility of the subject matter.

Thus, the passage establishes the Hanafi position that majaz operates at the level of speech, not ruling, which is a key usul principle affecting many branches of fiqh.

Key Terms
المجاز (al-majaz): Figurative or metaphorical usage of a word in a meaning other than its original literal meaning, considered a substitute (khalaf) for the literal.
الخلف (al-khalaf): A substitute for the original (al-asl) used when the literal meaning is impossible; its application depends on the condition that the original was contracted with probability.
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): The original literal meaning of a word, for which the word was originally coined.
الغموس (al-ghamus): A false oath that is deliberately false from the outset, such that it never had a chance of being fulfilled.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why does saying 'this is my son' to an older slave cause manumission, while the Sahibayn disagree?
A) Because the slave is actually his son.
B) Because the Sahibayn hold that majaz is a substitute in speech.
C) Because Abu Hanifa holds that majaz is a substitute in speech, so the validity of the expression suffices.
D) Because the Sahibayn consider the statement impossible in both speech and ruling.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa treats majaz as a substitute in speech (takallum), making the validity of the expression independent of the feasibility of the literal meaning; the Sahibayn require the literal meaning to be possible for the substitute to carry legal weight.

The Nature of Majaz as Khalaf: Speech vs. Effect
Dispute on the nature of majaz as khalaf: speech vs. effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether majaz is a substitute in speech or effect, apply the distinction to the older-slave example, and evaluate the Sahibain's counter-argument.

Core Idea
For Abu Hanifa, majaz is a substitute in speech but not in legal effect; in effect, it acts as an original.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the nature of majaz (metaphorical usage) as a substitute (khalaf) for haqiqah (literal meaning) is a contested issue. This lesson examines the dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two companions regarding whether the substitution occurs in the realm of speech (takallum) or legal effect (hukm). The practical consequence determines the validity of certain speech acts, such as a man declaring an older slave to be his son, and whether that effects emancipation.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify Abu Hanifa's view: majaz is a substitute in speech (takallum) but not in effect (hukm). In effect, it acts as an original (asl).
This means the speech act itself is valid even if the literal effect is impossible.
2
Identify the opposing view
The Sahibain (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that majaz is a substitute in effect, meaning the legal consequence must be possible for the substitution to occur.
They compare to the case of an oath that is impossible: without possibility for birr, no substitute for kaffarah.
3
Follow the Sahibain's proof
They argue that the condition for substitution is the probability of the original effect. In the older slave case, the effect (emancipation) is impossible because the slave is older, so no substitute.
They analogize to an oath to touch the sky: there is probability, so substitution works. For a false oath (yamin ghamus), no probability, so no substitute.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's response
Abu Hanifa argues that substitution is in speech, not effect. The condition is the validity of the speech act, not the possibility of the effect. He cites the istithna example: saying 'a thousand except 999' is valid speech even though the effect of more than three is impossible; the exception operates on speech.
Similarly, in the slave case, the speech 'this is my son' is valid as a sentence, so the borrowing for the effect of emancipation is correct.
5
Determine the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf is the ruling on the older slave case: Abu Hanifa rules the slave is emancipated (in judicial judgment), while the Sahibain rule no emancipation.
This shows how the usul principle directly impacts fiqh outcomes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أحكام هذا القسم أيضاً : أن المجازَ خَلَفٌ عن الحقيقة في حق التكلم ، لا في حق الحكم عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله : هو خَلَفٌ عن الحكم. بيانه فيمن قال لعبده وهو أكبر سنّاً منه : هذا ابني : لم يعتق عندهما ؛ لأن هذا الكلام لم ينعقد لِمَا وُضع له أصلاً ، فصار لغواً، لا حكم له، فلا يجب العمل بمجازه ؛ لأنه خَلَفٌ عنه في إثبات الحكم، ومِن شَرط الخَلَف : أن ينعقد السبب للأصل على الاحتمال، وامتنع وجوده بعارض. كمن حلف: ليمسَّنَّ السماء: أن اليمين انعقدت للبر؛ لاحتمال وجوده، فانعقدت للكفارة خلفاً عنه، فأما الغموس: فلم تنعقد للحكم الأصلي، وهو البِرُّ ، فلا تنعقد لخلفه، وهذا نظير مسألة الغموس. وقال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله : المجاز خلفٌ عن الحقيقة في التكلم، لا في الحكم، بل هو في حق الحكم أصل. ألا يُرى أن العبارة تتغير به، دون الحكم، فكان تصرُّفاً في التكلم، فتشترط صحة الأصل من حيث إنه مبتدأ وخبر موضوع للإيجاب بصيغته، وقد وجد ذلك، فإذا وجد وتعذر العمل بحقيقته وله مجاز متعين: صار مستعاراً لحكمه بغير نية، كالنكاح بلفظ الهبة. :وقالا لفظ الهبة ينعقد لحكمه الأصلي في الحرة؛ لأن احتمال بيع الحرة وهبتها مثل احتمال مس السماء، وأما هذا فمستحيل بمرة. وقال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله : هذا تصرُّفٌ في التكلم، فلا يتوقف على احتمال الحكم، كالاستثناء، فإن من قال لامرأته: أنت طالق ألفاً إلا تسعمائة وتسعة وتسعين : فإنه تقع واحدة. كذا ذكره في «المنتقى». وإيجاب ما زاد على الثلاث من طريق الحكم باطل، لكن من طريق التكلم صحيح، والاستثناء تصرُّفٌ في التكلم بالمنع ، فصح، فكذا هذا، : لما كان تصرُّفاً في حق التكلم صحت الاستعارة به؛ لحكم حقيقته وإن لم ينعقد لإيجاب تلك الحقيقة، ومن حكم الحقيقة : عتقه من حين ملکه، فجعل إقراراً به، فعَتَقَ في القضاء. بخلاف النداء؛ لأنه لاستحضار المنادَى بصورة الاسم، لا بمعناه، فإذا لم يكن المعنى مطلوباً : لم تجب الاستعارة لتصحيح معناه. بخلاف قوله : يا حر فإنه يستوي فيه نداؤه وخبره؛ لأنه موضوع للتحرير، فصار عينه قائماً مقام معناه، فصار المعنى مطلوباً بكل حال.
And from the rulings of this section also: that majaz is a substitute (khalaf) for haqiqah with respect to speech (takallum), not with respect to legal effect (hukm) according to Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: it is a substitute for the effect. Explanation: regarding someone who said to his slave, who is older than him: 'this is my son' - it does not cause emancipation according to them because this speech was not established for what it was originally placed for, so it becomes nonsense with no rule, so acting on its majaz is not obligatory because it is a substitute in establishing the effect, and among the conditions of the substitute is that the cause for the original be established with probability, and its existence is prevented by an obstacle. Like one who swore: 'I will touch the sky' - the oath was established for fulfillment (birr) due to the possibility of its existence, so it is established for expiation (kaffarah) as a substitute for it. As for the false oath (yamin ghamus), it is not established for the original effect, which is birr, so it is not established for its substitute. This is analogous to the issue of the false oath. Abu Hanifa said: majaz is a substitute for haqiqah in speech, not in the effect; rather, with respect to the effect it is original. Do you not see that the expression changes with it, but the effect does not? So it is a disposal in speech; thus the validity of the original is required in that it is a subject and predicate placed for affirmation with its form, and that has been found. So when it is found and acting on its haqiqah is impossible and it has a determined majaz, it becomes borrowed for its effect without intention, like marriage (nikah) using the word hibah (gift). They (the two) said: the word hibah is established for its original effect regarding a free woman because the possibility of selling a free woman and giving her as a gift is like the possibility of touching the sky; but this (the slave case) is absolutely impossible. Abu Hanifa said: this is a disposal in speech, so it does not depend on the possibility of the effect, like istithna (exception); for if a man says to his wife: 'you are divorced a thousand except nine hundred and ninety-nine', it falls as one. Thus it is mentioned in al-Muntqa. Affirmation of more than three is invalid in terms of effect, but valid in terms of speech, and istithna is a disposal in speech by prevention, so it is valid. So likewise this: since it was a disposal concerning speech, the borrowing is valid for the effect of its haqiqah even if it was not established for the affirmation of that haqiqah, and from the effect of haqiqah is his emancipation from the time he owned him, so it is made an acknowledgment of it, so he is emancipated in judicial judgment. Contrary to calling (al-nida'), because it is for summoning the called one by the form of the name, not its meaning; so when the meaning is not intended, borrowing is not obligatory to correct its meaning. Contrary to his saying: 'O free man' (ya hurr), for in it his calling and his informing are equal because it is placed for freedom, so its very essence stands in place of its meaning, so the meaning is intended in every case.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute in Hanafi usul al-fiqh regarding the nature of majaz (metaphorical usage) when it serves as a substitute (khalaf) for haqiqah (literal meaning). The dispute is between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Abu Hanifa holds that majaz is a substitute only with respect to speech (takallum) but not with respect to legal effect (hukm); in terms of effect, the majaz itself acts as an original (asl). The Sahibain, on the other hand, assert that majaz is a substitute in the realm of legal effect. The passage demonstrates the implications through examples: a man who says of his older slave 'this is my son' — according to the Sahibain, this does not effect emancipation because the statement was not validly formed for its original purpose (to affirm lineage) and thus becomes void, so its majaz (the implication of freedom via acknowledgment) cannot be applied. According to Abu Hanifa, such a statement is valid speech; the disposal is in the realm of speech, so the borrowing for the effect of haqiqah is permissible. Further examples include oaths: one who swears 'I will touch the sky' — the oath is valid for potential fulfillment (birr) and if impossible, it substitutes for kaffarah. But the false oath (yamin ghamus) does not even form for the original effect, so no substitute applies. Abu Hanifa supports his position with the analogy of istithna (exception) in divorce: a man saying 'you are divorced a thousand except 999' results in a single divorce; although the number beyond three is void in effect, the speech is valid, and the exception operates on the speech. Similarly, the slave case: the speech is valid, so the borrowing for the effect of emancipation occurs. The author distinguishes this from calling out a name (nida') where the meaning is not required, versus saying 'O free man' which explicitly carries the meaning of freedom regardless of usage.

Key Terms
المجاز (al-majaz): Metaphorical usage: a word used for something other than its original literal meaning due to a connection (alāqah) and a preventer from literal meaning.
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): Literal meaning: the meaning for which a word is originally placed.
خَلَف (khalaf): Substitute: something that stands in place of the original when the original is impossible or not intended.
التكلم (al-takallum): Speech: the act of uttering words; the linguistic aspect of expression.
الحكم (al-hukm): Legal effect: the juridical consequence attached to a valid speech act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the effect of divorce is limited to three, so the exception is invalid.
B) Because the exception operates on the speech act, which is valid, not on the legal effect.
C) Because the woman was already divorced three times.
D) Because the number 'thousand' is metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
The key takeaway is that for Abu Hanifa, the validity of a majaz as a substitute depends on the integrity of the speech act (takallum), not on the possibility of the literal effect; this distinguishes his approach from that of the Sahibain.

Oath on Marriage to an Ajnabiyyah: Literal vs Metaphorical
Dispute on whether literal meaning is dropped when impossible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that haqiqah is abandoned when impossible, using the example of an oath on marriage.

Core Idea
When literal meaning is impossible, it is not intended; the oath is not broken.

This lesson delves into a key usul dispute: does an oath remain binding when its literal act becomes impossible? The Hanafi school answers with a nuanced principle—when impossibility strikes, the literal meaning is abandoned, preserving the oath's integrity. We'll examine the proof from a classic Hanafi ruling on marriage oaths, and understand why the preferred view saves the oath from violation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
The core question is whether the literal meaning (haqiqah) of an oath is abandoned when fulfillment becomes impossible.
2
State the two positions
Position 1: Haqiqah is dropped when impossible, so oath not broken. Position 2: Haqiqah is never dropped, so oath broken.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The example of an oath not to marry an ajnabiyyah: Hanafi scholars restrict 'nikah' to the marriage contract, not zina, thereby dropping the literal meaning when impossible. This serves as an ijma proof.
4
Determine the tarjih
The text says 'الأول أشبه' (the first is more appropriate), indicating the preferred view.
5
Apply the principle
The rule: haqiqah is used when possible, abandoned when impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلانة، فقيل : لما كان متعذراً: لم يكن مراداً ، فلا يحنث. وقيل : بل الحقيقة لا تسقط بحال، فيحنث. والأول أشبه ؛ لأن أصحابنا رحمهم الله قالوا فيمن حلف أن لا ينكح أجنبيةٌ : إنه يقع على العقد ؛ فإن زنى بها : لم يحنث، فأسقطوا الحقيقة، وهي حقيقته.
Fulanah, it was said: Since it (the literal meaning) is impossible, it was not intended, so he does not break his oath. And it was said: Rather, the literal meaning is not dropped under any circumstance, so he breaks his oath. The first is more appropriate, because our scholars (may Allah have mercy on them) said regarding someone who swears that he will not marry a foreign woman: that it applies to the marriage contract; so if he commits zina with her, he does not break his oath, thus they dropped the literal meaning, and it is its literal meaning.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute regarding an oath not to marry a specific woman (Fulanah). The first opinion holds that if the literal act (marriage contract) is impossible, the oath is not violated because the impossible is not intended. The second insists that the literal meaning is never abandoned, so any act of intimacy breaks the oath. The preferred view is the first, supported by the Hanafi masters' ruling on an oath not to marry a foreign woman: they restrict it to the contract, excluding zina, thus dropping the literal meaning when it is impossible. The principle is that haqiqah (literal meaning) is used when possible, but abandoned when impossible.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): The literal, real meaning of a word as originally coined
المتعذر (al-muta'azzir): That which is impossible to achieve
الحنث (al-hanth): Breaking an oath by doing what was sworn against

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the oath not to marry an ajnabiyyah, what did the Hanafi scholars conclude regarding zina?
A) It breaks the oath because the literal meaning of 'nikah' includes zina
B) It does not break the oath because 'nikah' refers only to the marriage contract
C) It breaks the oath only if the woman is not a foreigner
D) The ruling depends on the intention at the time of the oath

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, when the literal meaning of an oath becomes impossible to fulfill, it is considered not intended, so the oath is not broken.

Two Approaches to Parentage Declarations for an Older Person
Complex inheritance/freeing calculations and two usul approaches

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that when literal lineage is impossible, a parentage declaration is interpreted as emancipation, and distinguish the two approaches of Abu Hanifa.

Core Idea
When declared parentage is impossible literally, the Hanafi school interprets it as emancipation — but with two different methods.

In the Hanafi school, ambiguous declarations of parentage require careful legal interpretation, especially when the person declared cannot be one's biological child. This lesson examines a complex case from al-Jami' where a master declares 'one of these is my son' among multiple candidates, and explores two approaches of Abu Hanifa regarding an older person: whether the declaration is an acknowledgment of existing freedom or a new act of emancipation. Understanding this distinction reveals how usul principles operate when literal meaning is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Scenario
First, understand the scenario: a master has a slave with a son and grandchildren. The master says 'one of these is my son' but dies without clarifying. All are potentially his sons. We need to determine the effect on freedom.
The fractions calculated (e.g., first one quarter) are based on probability.
2
Distinguish Between Possible and Impossible Lineage
Note that the passage then addresses a case involving an 'older person' — someone who cannot literally be the master's son. Here, literal lineage is impossible, so the declaration cannot be an acknowledgment of parentage.
This impossibility triggers a different legal analysis.
3
Map Abu Hanifa's Two Ways
Identify the two approaches: (1) The declaration is an acknowledgment of freedom (iqrar bil-hurriya), which also affects the mother's rights. (2) It is an initial emancipation (tahrir mubtada), creating freedom from that moment without retroactive effect.
The second view is supported by an analogy from Kitab al-Da'wa.
4
Trace the Usul Principle: Metaphorical Interpretation
Understand the usul principle: when literal meaning is impossible, the statement is taken metaphorically. Here, claiming lineage for an older person is impossible, so the declaration is interpreted as emancipation, analogous to the case in Kitab al-Da'wa where claiming lineage is treated as freeing.
This is an application of the rule 'al-majaz yujazzu idha ta'adhdhara al-haqiqa'.
5
Analyze the Consequence (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
Under the first view, the mother becomes umm walad (free upon master's death). Under the second, she does not. Identify this practical difference and note which view is stronger according to the text.
The text seems to favor the second view by analogy.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال في «الجامع : في رجل له عبد، ولعبده ابن، ولابنه ابنان، فقال المولى في صحته أحد هؤلاء ابني، ثم مات قبل البيان وكلَّهم يصلح ابناً له : إنه يعتق من الأول ربعه، ومن الثاني ثلثه، ومن كل واحد من الآخرين ثلاثة أرباعه. وعلى قياس ذلك الجواب : لو كان لابن العبد ابن واحد، وكلُّهم يولد لمثله : أنه يعتق من الأول ثلثه ومن الثاني نصفه، الثالث كله ؛ لاحتمال النسب، ولو كان تحريراً لعتق من كل واحد منهم ثلثه. وأما في الأكبر سنّاً منه : فلأبي حنيفة رحمه الله طريقان: أحدهما : أنه إقرار بالحرية، فيجب أن يصير مقراً بحق الأم أيضاً؛ لأنه يحتمل الإقرار. والثاني : تحريراً مبتدأ. : أنه تحرير مبتدأ؛ من قبل أن الإقرار بالنسب لو ثبت: ثبت حتى قلنا) في كتاب الدعوى في رجلين ورثا عبداً، ثم ادعى أحدهما أنه ابنه غَرِم لشريكه كأنه أعتقه في الحال؛ لأن ثبوت النسب مضاف إلى خبره ؛ لأن المخبر به قائم بخبره ، فإذا كان كذلك: جعل مجازاً عن التحرير، وحقُّ الأم لا يحتمل الوجود بابتداء تصرف المولى؛ لأنه
And he said in al-Jami': concerning a man who has a slave, and the slave has a son, and that son has two sons. The master says during his health 'One of these is my son', then dies before clarification, and all of them could be his son. So: he is freed from the first one quarter, from the second one third, and from each of the other two three quarters. And by analogy, if the slave's son had one son, and all are born of a similar type: he is freed from the first one third, from the second half, and the third entirely, due to the possibility of lineage; and if it were a declaration of emancipation (tahrir), each would be freed to the extent of one third. As for the older than him: Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) has two ways: one: it is an acknowledgment of freedom, so he must become an acknowledgment regarding the mother as well, because it is possible for acknowledgment. The second: it is an initial emancipation (tahrir mubtada): it is an initial emancipation, because the acknowledgment of lineage if established: is established only in the manner we said in The Book of Claims: two men inherited a slave, then one claimed that the slave is his son - he pays his partner as if he had freed him immediately, because the establishment of lineage is attached to his report. So when that is the case, it is taken metaphorically as emancipation, and the right of the mother cannot exist through the master's initial action because...
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage from al-Jami' presents complex cases where a master ambiguously declares parentage among multiple potential sons. Shares of freedom are calculated based on the probability of lineage. For example, if there are three individuals (first, second, and two others), the first is freed a quarter, the second a third, and each of the other two three-quarters. These fractions are derived from the possibility that the master's son could be any one of them. The text then discusses an older person: Abu Hanifa has two narrations—one treats the declaration as an acknowledgment of freedom (which also affects the mother), and the other treats it as a new emancipation (tahrir mubtada), supporting the latter by analogy with a case in Kitab al-Da'wa where claiming lineage is treated metaphorically as emancipation. The passage ends incomplete but illustrates the usul principle that when literal lineage is impossible, the declaration is interpreted as emancipation.

Key Terms
الإقرار بالحرية (al-iqrar bi-l-hurriyyah): Acknowledgment of freedom, where the declarant affirms that the person is free
التحرير المبتدأ (al-tahrir al-mubtada): An initial act of emancipation, not based on acknowledgment of existing freedom
احتمال النسب (ihtimal al-nasab): The possibility of lineage, which affects the calculation of shares

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case from al-Jami' where there are three individuals (first, second, and third younger), what fraction of the first is freed?
A) One quarter
B) One third
C) One half
D) Three quarters

Key Takeaway
When literal lineage is impossible, a parentage declaration is interpreted metaphorically as emancipation, with the second approach of Abu Hanifa being methodologically stronger.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

The Hanafi Position on the Waw of Conjunction
Dispute over the particle waw and its legal implications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the waw of conjunction, identify the opposing Shafi'i argument, and apply the difference to a fiqh scenario.

Core Idea
The waw indicates absolute conjunction—no order, no simultaneity—until external evidence proves otherwise.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul introduces a foundational dispute: does the conjunction particle waw (واو) imply order (tartib)? The Hanafi school, following most linguists and jurists, holds that waw is for absolute conjunction (mutlaq al-'atf) without any inherent sequence. Some Shafi'i scholars argue that waw necessitates order, citing Quranic verses and prophetic practice. Grasping this disagreement is essential because it directly impacts rulings in wudu, salat, and other acts of worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position on waw
Locate the statement 'وهي عندنا لمطلق العطف من غير تعرض لمقارنة، ولا ترتيب'. Note that waw simply conjoins without implying simultaneity (muqāranah) or order (tartib).
This is the baseline: the waw is neutral in terms of sequence.
2
Recognize the Shafi'i counterposition
Find the phrase 'وقال بعض أصحاب الشافعي رحمهم الله : إن الواو توجب الترتيب'. Understand that some Shafi'is claim waw imposes order.
Their view leads to different fiqh rulings.
3
Examine the opposing proofs
Analyze the two proofs they cite: (1) Quranic verse of wudu (al-Ma'idah 5:6) where sequential mention of face and hands is taken to mandate washing order; (2) Hadith of sa'i where the Prophet began with al-Safa, saying 'We begin with what Allah began,' referring to the verse pairing al-Safa and al-Marwah with waw.
Also they mention 'Bow and prostrate' (al-Hajj 22:77).
4
Derive the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Consider a person washing hands before face in wudu. Under Hanafi view, the waw does not require order, so wudu is valid. Under Shafi'i view, order is required, so wudu would be invalid if order is not followed.
This shows how a particle's interpretation changes actual fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يتصل بهذا القسم : حروفُ المعاني، فإنها تنقسم إلى حقيقة، ومجاز، وشَطْرُ من مسائل الفقه مبني على هذه الجملة، وهذا الباب لبيان ما يتصل بها من الفروع، والله أعلم.
ومن باب حروف المعاني
هذه الجملة : حروف العطف، وهي أكثرها وقوعاً.
حرف الواو :
وأصل هذا القسم: الواو :
وهي عندنا لمطلق العطف من غير تعرض لمقارنة، ولا ترتيب،
وعلى هذا عامة أهل اللغة، وأئمة الفتوى.
وقال بعض أصحاب الشافعي رحمهم الله : إن الواو توجب الترتيب،
حتى قالوا في قول الله تعالى: ﴿فَاغْسِلُوا وُجُوهَكُمْ وَأَيْدِيَكُمْ إِلَى الْمَرَافِقِ﴾ (المائدة/٦) : يوجب الترتيب.
واحتجوا بأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بدأ بالصفا في السعي، وقال:"نبدأ بما بدأ الله عزّ وجل"، يريد به قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِنَّ الصَّفَا وَالْمَرْوَةَ مِن شَعَائِرِ اللهِ﴾ (البقرة/١٥٨) ، ففَهم من ذلك وجوب الترتيب.
ووَجَبَ الترتيب بقوله تعالى: ﴿أَرْكَعُوا وَاسْجُدُوا﴾ (الحج/٧٧) :
(١) باب حروف المعاني: حديث نبدأ بما بدأ الله بذكره: أخرج مسلم في حدیث جابر في صفة الحج أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم خرج من باب الصفا، فلما دنا من الصفا، قرأ: ﴿إِنَّ الصَّفَا وَالْمَرْوَةَ مِن شَعَائِرِ اللَّهِ﴾، أبدأ بما بدأ الله به، فبدأ بالصفا. الحديث.
And what pertains to this section: the particles of meaning, for they are divided into literal and figurative, and many issues of fiqh are built upon this principle, and this chapter is to explain the branches connected to them, and Allah knows best. From the chapter of particles of meaning: this principle: the particles of conjunction, and they are the most frequent. The letter waw: and the origin of this section: the waw: it is according to us for absolute conjunction without indicating simultaneity or order, and upon this are the majority of linguists and the leading jurists. And some followers of al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on them, said that the waw necessitates order, even to the point that they said regarding the saying of Allah the Exalted: {So wash your faces and your hands up to the elbows} (al-Ma'idah 5:6): it requires order. And they argued that the Prophet, peace be upon him, began with al-Safa in the sa'i, and said: "We begin with what Allah began," meaning His saying: {Indeed, al-Safa and al-Marwah are among the symbols of Allah} (al-Baqarah 2:158), so they understood from that the obligation of order. And order is required by His saying: {Bow and prostrate} (al-Hajj 22:77). (1) Chapter of particles of meaning: Hadith: "We begin with what Allah began mentioning." Narrated by Muslim in the hadith of Jabir describing the Hajj that the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, left from the door of al-Safa, and when he approached al-Safa, he recited: {Indeed, al-Safa and al-Marwah are among the symbols of Allah}, "I begin with what Allah began," so he began with al-Safa. The end of the hadith.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul introduces the letters of meanings (huruf al-ma'ani), specifically conjunction particles, and then focuses on the letter waw. The author states the Hanafi position: waw is for absolute conjunction without indicating simultaneity or order (tartib). This is the view of most linguists and jurists. Then he presents the opposing view from some Shafi'i scholars, who argue that waw implies order. They cite two proofs: first, the verse of wudu (al-Ma'idah 5:6), where the sequential mention of face then hands is taken to impose a sequence in washing; second, the hadith of the Prophet's sa'i beginning with al-Safa, where he said 'We begin with what Allah began,' referring to the verse (al-Baqarah 2:158) that pairs al-Safa and al-Marwah with waw. They also cite the verse 'Bow and prostrate' (al-Hajj 22:77) to argue that order is required. The footnote provides the full hadith from Sahih Muslim. The author does not provide a rebuttal in this passage, simply presenting the dispute to illustrate how differing interpretations of a particle (waw) lead to different fiqh rulings.

Key Terms
الواو (al-waw): The conjunction particle waw; according to the Hanafi school, it indicates absolute conjunction without order or simultaneity.
حرف العطف (harf al-'atf): A coordinating conjunction particle, among the ḥurūf al-ma‘ānī (particles of meaning), used to connect words or phrases.
حقيقة (haqīqah): Literal meaning of a word; in this context, the literal use of a particle like waw.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It implies order (tartib) between the connected items.
B) It implies simultaneity (muqāranah) between the connected items.
C) It implies absolute conjunction without order or simultaneity.
D) It implies alternation (takhīr) between the connected items.

Key Takeaway
The waw in Hanafi usul is for absolute conjunction; order is only established by a separate qarīnah.
