Contextual Interpretation of Waw: The Khulʿ Dispute
Analyzing the ikhtilaf on interpreting the waw in khul' statement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of contextual evidence (qarīna) to interpret ambiguous particles, using the dispute on 'Tallaqni wa laka alf' as a case study.

Core Idea
Context can override default linguistic meaning: in khulʿ, waw shifts from conjunction to exchange or condition.

This lesson examines a famous usul dispute among the Hanafi imams: how should the waw in a wife's statement 'Divorce me and for you a thousand' be interpreted? Abu Hanifa takes the default conjunction reading, but the Sahibayn argue that the context of khulʿ (a commutative transaction) transforms the waw into an indicator of exchange. By studying their two proofs, we learn how contextual evidence (qarīna) can override default linguistic rules in deriving legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed element
Locate the specific linguistic element in dispute: the waw in 'wa laka alf'. Is it a simple conjunction (ʿaṭf), a metaphor for bāʾ (exchange), or a circumstantial ḥāl (condition)?
The dispute is about the legal effect, not the literal grammar alone.
2
State the two positions
Abu Hanifa says no payment is due because the waw is conjunctive. The Sahibayn say payment is due because the waw indicates exchange. Understand the practical consequence (thamarah).
Both are within Hanafi school – this is intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
3
Analyze the first proof: istiʿāra
The Sahibayn argue that waw can be metaphorically used for bāʾ, as in oaths. They analogize to the common saying 'Carry this food to my house and for you a dirham', where 'wa laka' is understood as 'bika' (with a dirham).
This analogy demonstrates qiyas based on ʿurf (customary usage).
4
Analyze the second proof: waw as ḥāl
Alternatively, the waw can be interpreted as ḥāl (circumstance), making the payment conditional on the divorce. They analogize to 'Give me a thousand and you are free' and 'Dismount and you are safe'.
This reading also relies on the qarīna of exchange present in khulʿ.
5
Understand the limit: the muḍāraba exception
The Sahibayn note that in the statement 'Take this capital and trade with it as muḍāraba', the waw remains conjunctive because no context of exchange exists. This shows that qarīna must be present for interpretation to shift.
The default (conjunction) holds unless a clear indication of exchange overrides it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفوا في قول المرأة لزوجها: طلقني ولك ألف درهم:
فحمله أبو يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله على المعاوضة، حتى إذا طلقها وجب له الألف
وحَمَلَه أبو حنيفة رحمه الله على واو عطف الجملة، حتى إذا طلقها :
لم يجب له شيء.
ولأبي يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله طريقان:
أحدهما أن الواو قد تُستعار للباء كما استعير له في باب القسم، على ما نبين إن شاء الله عزّ وجلَّ، فحمل على هذا المجاز بدلالة حال المعاوضة؛ لأن حال الخلع : حال المعاوضة.
كما قيل في قول الرجل لآخر: احمل هذا الطعام إلى منزلي ولكَ درهم أنه يُحمل على الباء، أي: بدرهم.
وبدلاً.
والثاني: أن الواو للحال، بدلالة حال المعاوضة أيضاً؛ ليصير شرطاً
ونظيره : قوله : أد إليَّ ألفاً وأنت حر، و: انزل وأنت آمن.
وهذا بخلاف قوله : خُذْ هذا المال واعمل به مضاربةً، فإنه لا معنى للباء ها هنا، وإنما حُمل في مسألة الخلاف على الحال؛ لدلالة المعاوضة، ولم توجد.
And they differed concerning the woman's statement to her husband: 'Divorce me and for you a thousand dirhams.' Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) interpreted it as an exchange (muʿāwaḍa), so if he divorces her, he is entitled to the thousand. Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) interpreted it as waw of conjunction of sentences (ʿaṭf al-jumla), so if he divorces her, nothing is owed to him. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad have two ways: First, that the waw may be borrowed for the letter bāʾ, as it is borrowed in the chapter of oaths, as we will explain if Allah wills, so they interpreted it by this metaphor based on the context of exchange, because the situation of khulʿ is a situation of exchange. Just as it is said in the statement of one man to another: 'Carry this food to my house and for you a dirham' – it is interpreted as bāʾ, i.e., with a dirham. And as an exchange (badalan). Second, that the waw is for ḥāl, also based on the context of exchange, so as to make it a condition. Its analogy is the statement: 'Give me a thousand and you are free,' and 'Dismount and you are safe.' This differs from the statement: 'Take this capital and trade with it as muḍāraba,' because there is no meaning of bāʾ here, and it is only interpreted as ḥāl in the issue of dispute due to the context of exchange, and it is not present.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage presents a legal dispute among the Hanafi imams regarding the interpretation of the waw in the wife's statement 'Divorce me and for you a thousand dirhams' – a formula for khulʿ (divorce initiated by wife in exchange for compensation). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani interpret the waw as indicating an exchange (muʿāwaḍa): the wife offers a thousand dirhams in return for the divorce, so if the husband divorces her, he is entitled to the payment. Abu Hanifa, however, interprets the waw as simple conjunction (ʿaṭf al-jumla): the wife said 'divorce me' and also informed him that he has a thousand (e.g., as a gift), but without linking the two; thus if he divorces, he does not necessarily receive the money. The Sahibayn provide two lines of reasoning. First, the waw can be metaphorically used to mean the preposition bāʾ (by/with), just as in oaths one says 'wa-llāhi' meaning 'by Allah'. Likewise, when someone says 'Carry this food to my house and for you a dirham,' the waw is interpreted to mean 'with a dirham' (i.e., in exchange for a dirham). So here, 'for you a thousand' means 'with a thousand' or 'in exchange for a thousand.' Second, they argue that the waw can be interpreted as ḥāl (circumstance), which makes the payment conditional on the divorce – similar to the earlier examples of the slave and the harbī. The difference is that in the muḍāraba example, there is no context of exchange, so it remains conjunctive. The passage highlights how contextual evidence (qarīna) overrides the default meaning of waw.

Key Terms
المعاوضة (muʿāwaḍa): Exchange or commutative contract, where one thing is given in return for another.
عطف الجملة (ʿaṭf al-jumla): Simple conjunction of two sentences without implying exchange or condition.
استعارة (istiʿāra): Metaphorical borrowing; here, waw is used in place of the letter bāʾ to indicate exchange.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's first argument for interpreting 'wa laka alf' as an exchange?
A) The waw is a conjunction and must be taken literally.
B) The waw is metaphorically used for the preposition bāʾ, similar to its use in oaths.
C) The context of marriage indicates a condition.
D) The wife's statement is a gift, not a sale.

Key Takeaway
Contextual evidence (qarīna) can override the default linguistic meaning of a particle, as shown by the Sahibayn's interpretation of waw in khulʿ.

The Interpretation of Waw in Khul': A Hanafi Usul Dispute
Analyzing linguistic and legal interpretation of waw in khul'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the effect of the conjunction 'waw' in legal phrases and evaluate the Hanafi internal dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding its interpretation in khul'.

Core Idea
Waw is originally for conjunction; context must clearly override it — Abu Hanifa holds firm, Sahibayn allow metaphorical reading.

In this passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul, we dive into a classic internal Hanafi dispute over how to interpret the simple conjunction 'waw' in phrases like 'Divorce me and you have a thousand dirhams.' The ruling changes dramatically depending on whether the waw is read as a simple conjunction, as a conditional state, or even as a substitute for the preposition 'ba' (with). By examining the legal nature of the transaction — whether it is a primary exchange like ijara or a conditional oath like khul' — the two sides arrive at opposite conclusions. This lesson will walk you through the conflicting arguments, the analogies used, and the practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed phrase and the positions
Focus on the phrase 'طلقني ولك ألف درهم'. Note Abu Hanifa's view (no compensation) vs Sahibayn (compensation).
The dispute is internal to the Hanafi school.
2
Analyze the linguistic basis
Understand the three possible interpretations of waw: conjunction (عطف الجملة), state (الحال), or metaphorical substitution for ba (الباء).
Each leads to a different legal outcome.
3
Examine the role of context (qarina)
The context of exchange (دلالة المعاوضة) in khul' is central. Why does Abu Hanifa reject it as sufficient?
Abu Hanifa argues khul' is oath-like, not a primary exchange.
4
Follow the analogies and distinctions
Compare with 'carry this food and you have a dirham' (interpreted as ba), 'pay me a thousand and you are free' (condition), and mudaraba (conjunction). Distinguish based on legal nature.
These analogies are used by Sahibayn to support their view.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical outcome: in a khul' scenario, does the husband get the compensation or not?
This difference is the direct result of the linguistic interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

واختلفوا في قول المرأة لزوجها: طلقني ولك ألف درهم:
فحمله أبو يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله على المعاوضة، حتى إذا طلقها وجب له الألف
وحَمَلَه أبو حنيفة رحمه الله على واو عطف الجملة، حتى إذا طلقها :
لم يجب له شيء.
ولأبي يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله طريقان:
أحدهما أن الواو قد تُستعار للباء كما استعير له في باب القسم، على ما نبين إن شاء الله عزّ وجلَّ، فحمل على هذا المجاز بدلالة حال المعاوضة؛ لأن حال الخلع : حال المعاوضة.
كما قيل في قول الرجل لآخر: احمل هذا الطعام إلى منزلي ولكَ درهم أنه يُحمل على الباء، أي: بدرهم.
وبدلاً.
والثاني: أن الواو للحال، بدلالة حال المعاوضة أيضاً؛ ليصير شرطاً
ونظيره : قوله : أد إليَّ ألفاً وأنت حر، و: انزل وأنت آمن.
وهذا بخلاف قوله : خُذْ هذا المال واعمل به مضاربةً، فإنه لا معنى للباء ها هنا، وإنما حُمل في مسألة الخلاف على الحال؛ لدلالة المعاوضة، ولم توجد.
وكذلك في قوله : أنت طالق وأنت مريضة.
وقال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله : الواو في الحقيقة للعطف، فلا تترك إلا بدليل، ولا تصلح المعاوضةُ دلالةً؛ لأن ذلك في الطلاق أمر زائد.
ألا يُرى أن الطلاق إذا دخله العوض كان يميناً من جانب الزوج، فلم يستقم تَرْكُ الأصل بدلالة هي من باب الزوائد.
بخلاف الإجارة ؛ لأنها شرعت معاوضةً أصليةً كسائر البيوع. - وقولها : ولك ألف: ليست بصيغة الحال أيضاً؛ لأن الحال فعل، أو اسم فاعل.
وأما قوله : أد إلى ألفاً وأنت حر فصيغته للحال، وصدر الكلام غير مفيد إلا شرطاً للتحرير، فحمل عليه.
وقوله : أنت طالق مفيد بنفسه.
وقوله : وأنت مريضةٌ : جملةً تامة، لا دلالة فيها على الحال، لكنها تحتمل ذلك، فصحت نيته.
وأما قوله : أد إلي ألفاً: لا يصلح ضريبة، فصلح دلالة على الحال. وقوله : واعمل به في باب المضاربة لا يصلح حالاً للأخذ، فبقي قوله : خذ هذا المال مضاربةً : مطلقاً.
وقوله : انزل وأنت آمن فيه دلالةُ الحال؛ لأن الأمان إنما يُراد به إعلاء الدين، وليعاين الحربي معالم الدين ومحاسنه، فكان الظاهر فيه الحال ؛ ليصير معلقاً بالنزول إلينا ، والكلام يحتمل الحال أيضاً.
They said regarding mudaraba: If a man says to another, 'Take this capital as a mudaraba and work with it in cloth,' this waw is for joining sentences, not for the state, so it does not become a condition, but rather becomes advice, and the mudaraba remains general. They differed regarding a woman's statement to her husband, 'Divorce me and you have a thousand dirhams': Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) interpreted it as an exchange, so that if he divorces her, the thousand is obligatory for him. Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) interpreted it as a waw joining sentences, so that if he divorces her, nothing is obligatory for him. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad have two approaches: One: that the waw is sometimes used metaphorically for the letter ba, just as it was used metaphorically for it in the chapter of oaths, as we will explain if Allah wills, so it is interpreted according to this metaphor due to the evidence of exchange context, because the situation of khul' is one of exchange. Just as it is said in the statement of a man to another, 'Carry this food to my house and you have a dirham' that it is interpreted as 'with a dirham' (i.e., for a dirham) and as a substitute. The second: that the waw is for the state, also due to the evidence of exchange context, to make it a condition. An analogous example is his statement: 'Pay me a thousand and you are free,' and 'Descend and you are safe.' This is contrary to his statement: 'Take this wealth and work with it as mudaraba,' because there is no meaning of ba here, and it is only interpreted in the case of dispute as being for the state due to the evidence of exchange, which is not present. Similarly in his statement: 'You are divorced and you are sick.' Abu Hanifa said: The waw in reality is for conjunction, so it is not abandoned except by evidence, and the exchange context is not sufficient as evidence because that in divorce is an additional matter. Do you not see that when divorce includes compensation, it becomes an oath from the husband's side, so it is not correct to abandon the original meaning based on evidence that is from the category of additional matters. Unlike hire (ijara), because it was legislated as a primary exchange like other sales. And her statement 'and you have a thousand' is also not a form of the state because the state is a verb or a participle. As for his statement 'Pay me a thousand and you are free,' its wording is for the state, and the beginning of the sentence is not beneficial except as a condition for freeing, so it is interpreted accordingly. His statement 'You are divorced' is beneficial by itself. His statement 'and you are sick' is a complete sentence, with no indication of the state, but it is possible, so his intention is valid. As for his statement 'Pay me a thousand' is not valid as a tax (?) so it is valid as an indication of the state. And his statement 'and work with it' in the chapter of mudaraba is not valid as a state for taking, so his statement 'Take this wealth as mudaraba' remains absolute. His statement 'Descend and you are safe' has an indication of the state because safety is intended only for the elevation of religion, and for the warring person to witness the signs and beauties of religion, so the apparent meaning is the state, to make it conditional upon descending to us, and the speech also admits the possibility of the state.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul discusses the interpretation of the conjunction 'waw' in contractual and conditional phrases. The author presents an internal Hanafi ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the two students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, regarding how to understand waw in statements like 'Divorce me and you have a thousand'. The key issue is whether the waw indicates a conjunction of two independent sentences, or whether it can indicate a condition or even be substituted for the preposition 'ba' (with) to create a contract of exchange. Abu Hanifa holds that waw is originally for conjunction and should not be abandoned without strong evidence, which is lacking in divorce cases because divorce with compensation is a kind of oath, not an ordinary exchange like hire. The Sahibayn argue that the context of exchange (as in khul') provides sufficient evidence to interpret the waw either metaphorically as 'ba' or as indicating a condition (hal), thereby making the compensation obligatory. The passage illustrates these positions with several examples: mudaraba (where Sahibayn interpret waw as conjunction to keep mudaraba general), khul' (where the dispute arises), and other analogical extensions. The discussion shows how linguistic interpretation in usul al-fiqh is closely tied to the legal nature of the transaction and the presence of contextual indicators.

Key Terms
الواو (al-waw): The conjunction 'and' in Arabic; used to connect words, phrases, or sentences; can indicate conjunction, state, or be used metaphorically.
عطف الجملة (atf al-jumla): Conjunction of sentences, where each sentence is independent and the waw simply connects them without implying a condition or circumstance.
الحال (al-hal): The circumstantial state or condition; when waw is interpreted as indicating the state of the subject, it often becomes a condition (shart) for the preceding action.
دلالة المعاوضة (dalalat al-mu'awada): The contextual indication that a transaction is an exchange (e.g., sale, hire, khul').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling if a woman says to her husband, 'طلقني ولك ألف درهم' (Divorce me and you have a thousand dirhams)?
A) The husband must divorce and is entitled to the thousand.
B) The husband may divorce and is entitled to the thousand.
C) The husband may divorce but does not receive the thousand.
D) The divorce is invalid without consent.

Key Takeaway
The interpretation of a common conjunction like waw can hinge on the deeper legal nature of the transaction — primary exchange vs. oath — and usul al-fiqh provides the tools to resolve such linguistic ambiguities.

The Legal Meaning of 'Fa' in Hanafi Usul
Explaining the legal implications of the conjunction 'fa'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for the conjunction 'fa' to distinguish between immediate sequence and delay, and to state the Hanafi position in the 'dirham fa dirham' dispute.

Core Idea
The particle 'fa' indicates immediate sequence unless the result is continuous, then it implies delay.

In this lesson, we examine the conjunction particle 'fa' (ف) and its legal implications in Hanafi usul. A hadith is used to illustrate how 'fa' indicates a direct causal link between two actions. We then explore how the meaning shifts when the result is continuous, and how this leads to a key dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i over the phrase 'dirham fa dirham'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic default of 'fa'
Identify the default meaning of 'fa' in Arabic: it typically indicates immediate sequence (al-ta'aqib) between two conjoined elements.
Contrast with 'thumma' which indicates delay.
2
Analyse the hadith proof
Read the hadith: 'lā yujzi'u waladun wālidahu ḥattā yajidahu mamlūkan, fayashtarihi, faya'tiquhu.' Identify that the two 'fa's in 'fayashtarihi' and 'faya'tiquhu' show that freeing is the direct consequence of purchase.
This establishes the principle that 'fa' creates a causal link.
3
Apply to conditional statements
Examine the example: 'If you enter this house, then this house, my slave is free.' Understand that the condition requires immediate entry into the second house after the first.
No delay is permitted between the two entries.
4
Understand the exception for continuous results
When the result is continuous (e.g., freedom, safety), 'fa' can indicate delay. Study the examples: 'Pay me a thousand, so you are free' (freed immediately because freedom is perpetual) and 'Descend, so you are safe' (safe regardless of descent).
Continuous results resemble delay because the effect endures.
5
Evaluate the dispute with al-Shafi'i
Contrast the Hanafi and Shafi'i interpretations of 'dirham fa dirham'. The Hanafis hold two dirhams are owed; al-Shafi'i holds one. Analyze the proofs and the principle 'al-ḥaqīqatu aḥaqqu mā amkan'.
This dispute illustrates how a single particle can change legal obligations.

I fed him: that is, with this feeding. And the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'A child cannot repay his parent until he finds him a slave, then buys him, then frees him.' So that indicates that his being a freeer is a ruling of purchase through ownership. And for this we said regarding one who says: 'If you enter this house, then this house, my slave is free': The condition is that you enter the last after the first without delay. And the particle 'fa' can also enter upon causes if that is continuous, then it becomes in the sense of delayed, as is said: 'Rejoice, for relief has come to you, and you are saved.' And its parallel is what our scholars, may Allah have mercy on them, said regarding the authorized slave, in one who said to his slave: 'Pay me one thousand, so you are free': He becomes free immediately, its estimation being: 'Pay me one thousand, for you have been freed,' because freedom is continuous, so it resembles the delayed. And they said in al-Siyar al-Kabir: 'Descend, so you are safe': He is safe whether he descends or not, because of what we said; it is not made in the sense of conditional, as if he implied the condition, because the speech is valid without implication. And implication is necessary only in the original, and for this we said regarding one who said: 'For so-and-so I have a dirham, so a dirham': He is liable for two dirhams, because the conjoined is other than the first, and the order is directed to the obligation, not the obligated, or it is made metaphorical in the sense of 'and'. And al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, said: He is liable for one dirham, because the meaning of order is futile, so it is treated as a new sentence to affirm the first, i.e., so it is a dirham, as the poet said: 'And poetry is not within the power of one who wrongs it, he wants to parse it but makes it ambiguous,' and as the Almighty said: 'That He might make clear to them and Allah leads astray whom He wills' (Ibrahim 4), except that this is not valid except with implication that abandons the literal meaning, and the literal meaning is more deserving as long as possible.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage discusses the particle 'fa' (ف) as a conjunction and its legal implications. It begins by citing a hadith where the Prophet states that a child cannot fully repay a parent unless he finds the parent as a slave, buys him, and then frees him. The use of 'fa' in 'fayatrihi' and 'fa yatiquhu' indicates that the freeing is a direct consequence of the purchase through ownership. The passage then applies this principle to a conditional statement: if someone says, 'If you enter this house, then this house, my slave is free,' the condition is that the second entry occurs immediately after the first without delay. However, when the result is continuous, 'fa' can take a delayed meaning, as in the example 'Rejoice, for relief has come to you' — the joy is immediate but the relief continues. Similarly, in the case of a slave told 'Pay me a thousand, so you are free,' he is freed immediately because freedom is perpetual, resembling a delayed effect. In al-Siyar al-Kabir, the phrase 'Descend, so you are safe' is interpreted as safety regardless of descent, indicating that 'fa' does not always imply conditionality. The passage then addresses a dispute with Al-Shafi'i over the phrase 'a dirham so a dirham'. According to the Hanafis, this obligates two dirhams because the conjoined element is separate, and the order applies to the obligation itself. Al-Shafi'i argues it obligates one dirham, treating the 'fa' as meaningless and the phrase as a new sentence. The Hanafis respond that al-Shafi'i's interpretation requires ellipsis, whereas the literal meaning should be preserved when possible.

Key Terms
الفاء (al-fa'): A conjunction particle normally indicating immediate sequence and causality, but can indicate delay when the result is continuous.
التراخي (al-tarakhi): Delay or interval between two events in conjunction.
الحقيقة أحق ما أمكن (al-ḥaqīqatu aḥaqqu mā amkan): The literal meaning is more deserving as long as possible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One dirham
B) Two dirhams
C) Three dirhams
D) None, it is invalid

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul rule for 'fa' is that it defaults to immediate sequence, and this literal meaning is preserved unless the result is continuous or compelling evidence requires otherwise.

The Hanafi Dispute over 'Thumma': Disconnection or Delay?
Explaining the disagreement among Hanafi scholars on the effect of 'thumma' in conditional statements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two Hanafi interpretations of the particle 'thumma' in conditional statements and evaluate how each interpretation changes the legal ruling in a divorce example.

Core Idea
Thumma can mean complete disconnection or mere delay in existence — the difference determines whether a conditional divorce takes effect immediately or only upon the condition.

The particle 'thumma' is a conjunction that indicates delay (tarakhi). But what kind of delay? This lesson explores a classic dispute within the Hanafi school: Abu Hanifa argues that 'thumma' completely disconnects the conjoined items, while his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, hold that the delay is only in existence, not in the legal ruling. Understanding this difference is crucial when parsing conditional pronouncements, especially in divorce cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic principle
Recognize that 'thumma' is defined as a conjunction for delay (tartib ma'a tarakhi). The question is about the nature of this delay: does it affect the legal ruling (hukm) or only the temporal existence of the events?
This is a purely linguistic-legal debate.
2
State the two positions
Learn Abu Hanifa's view: 'thumma' implies complete disconnection (inqita'), so each conjoined item is treated as an independent statement. Then learn the Sahibayn's view: the delay is only in existence, while in the ruling of speech (hukm al-takallum) they are connected.
The difference is subtle but has major practical consequences.
3
Apply to the divorce example
Consider a man says to his wife before consummation: 'You are divorced, then divorced, then divorced if you enter the house.' According to Abu Hanifa, the first divorce occurs immediately (unconditionally), and the rest are void. According to the Sahibayn, the first divorce may be conditional on entry, the second can occur after entry, and the third is void.
Note how the placement of the condition matters.
4
Trace the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference: under Abu Hanifa's view, a single unconditional divorce occurs; under the Sahibayn's view, up to two divorces may occur if the condition is met. This affects the total number of divorces and the possibility of revocability.
5
Evaluate the reasoning
Consider the arguments: Abu Hanifa relies on the full meaning of tarakhi, while the Sahibayn emphasize the connectivity of speech. Note that the Hanafi school accepts both as valid interpretations, and the mufti may choose based on context.
This is an ongoing ikhtilaf within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله : التراخي راجع إلى الوجود، فأما في حكم التكلم : فمتصل.

[Letter: thumma:] And as for thumma, it is for conjunction in the manner of delay, that is its subject; it is specified with a meaning uniquely its own. And our companions differed regarding the effect of the delay: Abu Hanifa, may Allah be pleased with him, said: It is in the sense of disconnection, as if it is a new ruling, in keeping with complete delay. And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on them, said: The delay refers to existence, but as for the ruling of speech, it is connected. Its clarification is in one who said to his wife before consummation: 'You are divorced, then divorced, then divorced if you enter the house.' The first. Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The first occurs, and what follows is void, as if he fell silent. And if he placed the condition earlier: the first is conditional, the second occurs, and the third is void, as if
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage discusses the particle 'thumma' (ثم) as a conjunction indicating delay (tarakhi). It first establishes that 'thumma' is specifically for delay. Then it presents a disagreement among Hanafi scholars regarding the legal impact of this delay. Abu Hanifa holds that 'thumma' implies complete disconnection between the conjoined items, so each item is treated as a new independent statement. In contrast, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two disciples) argue that the delay applies to the occurrence of the events themselves, but in terms of speech and legal ruling, the items are connected. The example clarifies: a man says to his wife before consummation, 'You are divorced, then divorced, then divorced if you enter the house.' According to Abu Hanifa, the first divorce occurs immediately, and the subsequent divorces are void because the 'thumma' disconnects them; it is as if he remained silent after the first. If the condition is placed before the first (e.g., 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, then divorced, then divorced'), then the first is conditional on entry, the second occurs (due to disconnected thumma), but the third is void. The text cuts off here but the pattern is clear.

Key Terms
ثم (thumma): A conjunction particle indicating delay (tarakhi) between conjoined items.
التراخي (al-tarakhi): Delay or interval between two events in conjunction, the core meaning of thumma.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The first divorce occurs immediately, and the second is void.

C) The first divorce is conditional on entry; the second occurs immediately.
D) No divorce occurs until entry.

Key Takeaway
The particle 'thumma' in Hanafi usul can indicate either complete disconnection (Abu Hanifa) or delay in existence with connected ruling (Sahibayn), affecting the number of effective divorces in conditional statements.

The Legal Meanings of 'على' in Hanafi Usul
Explaining the different legal meanings of 'على' in debt, contracts, and divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three distinct legal meanings of the preposition 'على' (obligation, attachment, condition) and apply the Hanafi rule to distinguish between debt, commutative contracts, and divorce contexts.

Core Idea
The meaning of 'على' shifts: obligation in debts, attachment in contracts, condition in divorce.

Prepositions are not always fixed in legal language. In this passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul, we explore how a single word 'على' carries different legal meanings depending on context—debt acknowledgment, sales contracts, or divorce formulae. Understanding these nuances is essential for accurate istinbat and for resolving ikhtilaf among the Hanafi masters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the literal meaning
The passage begins with the literal meaning of 'على': physical elevation/placement. This grounds its use for obligation.
Literal meaning is the starting point for haqiqah / majaz analysis.
2
Apply to debt acknowledgment
In 'for so-and-so upon me is a thousand dirhams', 'على' establishes a binding debt—meaning obligation.
Debt is a non-commutative context.
3
Contrast with pure commutative contracts
In sale, lease, marriage, 'على' takes the meaning of 'باء' (attachment) because contracts require bindingness. This is a metaphorical use (majaz).
Conditional meaning is impossible in contracts.
4
Distinguish the divorce context
In divorce, Abu Hanifa argues 'على' retains its literal conditional meaning (shart), because talaq can be conditional.
The Sahibayn analogize to 'باء' instead.
5
Analyze the dispute and proofs
Abu Hanifa's proof: condition is possible in talaq, so the literal meaning prevails. The Sahibayn's proof: analogy to contracts. The Quranic verses support conditional meaning.
This is a key example of ta'lil (reasoning) based on linguistic principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما كلمة على: فإنها وُضعت لوقوع الشيء على غيره، وارتفاعه وعُلوه فوقه، فصار هو موضوعاً للإيجاب والإلزام في قول الرجل : لفلان عليَّ ألف درهم: أنه دين، إلا أن يصل به الوديعة.
فإن دخلت في المعاوضات المحضة كانت بمعنى الباء إذا استعملت في البيع والإجارة والنكاح ؛ لأن اللزوم يناسب الإلصاق، فاستعير له.
وإذا استعملت في الطلاق كانت بمعنى الشرط عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله ، حتى إن من قالت له امرأتُه : طلقني ثلاثاً على ألف درهم، فطلقها واحدةً : لم يجب شيء عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله.
وعندهما: يجب ثلث الألف، كما في قولها: بألف درهم. وقال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله : كلمة: على: للزوم، على ما قلنا، وليس بين الواقع وبين ما لزمها مقابلةٌ، بل بينهما معاقبة، وذلك معنى الشرط والجزاء، فصار هذا بمنزلة حقيقة هذه الكلمة، وقد أمكن العمل به؛ لأن الطلاق وإن دخله المالُ فيصلح تعليقه بالشروط، حتى إنه من جانب الزوج يمين، فيصير هذا منها طلباً لتعليق المال بشرط الثلاث، فإذا خالف لم يجب، وفي المعاوضات المحضة يستحيل معنى الشرط فوجب العمل بمجازه، وهو الباء.
قال الله تعالى: ﴿حَقِيقُ أَن لَّا أَقُولَ عَلَى اللَّهِ إِلَّا الْحَقَّ﴾ الأعراف / ١٠٥. وقال تعالى: ﴿يُبَايِعْنَكَ عَلَى أَن لَّا يُشْرِكْنَ بِاللَّهِ شَيْئًا﴾ الممتحنة / ١٢، أي يُبَايِعْنَكَ بِهَذَا الشَّرْطِ.
As for the word 'ala', it is placed for something falling upon another, and its rising and elevation above it, so it became used for obligation and binding in the saying of a man: 'For so-and-so upon me is one thousand dirhams,' meaning it is a debt, unless he connects it with a deposit. If it enters into pure commutative contracts, it takes the meaning of 'baa' when used in sale, lease, and marriage, because bindingness suits attachment, so it is borrowed for it. When used in divorce, it has the meaning of condition according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, such that if a woman says to her husband 'Divorce me three times for one thousand dirhams,' and he divorces her once, nothing is owed according to Abu Hanifa. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), one-third of the thousand is owed, as in her saying 'for one thousand dirhams' (with 'baa'). Abu Hanifa said: The word 'ala' is for obligation, as we said, and there is no exchange between what occurs and what is owed, but rather there is a conditional relation (mu'aqaba), and that is the meaning of condition and reward (shart wa jaza), so this becomes like the literal meaning of this word, and it is possible to act upon it because divorce, even if it involves money, can be suspended on conditions, so it is from the husband's side an oath, thus this from her is a request to suspend the money on the condition of three divorces. If he violates, it is not owed. In pure commutative contracts, the meaning of condition is impossible, so one must act upon its metaphorical meaning, which is 'baa'. Allah Almighty said: 'It is fitting that I should not say about Allah except the truth' [Al-A'raf 7:105], and Almighty said: 'They pledge allegiance to you upon that they will not associate anything with Allah' [Al-Mumtahina 60:12], meaning they pledge allegiance to you with this condition.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul discusses the preposition 'على' and its legal meanings. Originally denoting physical elevation or placement, it is used for obligation, as in acknowledging a debt. In pure commutative contracts (like sale, lease, marriage), 'على' takes the meaning of 'باء' (attachment) because the bindingness of contracts requires attachment; it is a metaphorical use. In divorce, however, Imam Abu Hanifa holds that 'على' retains its conditional meaning. He illustrates with a case where a wife says 'Divorce me three times for one thousand dirhams' using 'على'. If the husband divorces once, nothing is owed according to Abu Hanifa, because the condition of three divorces is not fulfilled. The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) analogize to 'باء' and rule that one-third is owed, as if she said 'بألف درهم'. Abu Hanifa's reasoning: 'على' implies obligation but not direct exchange; between the stipulated (three divorces) and the payment there is a conditional relationship (shart wa jaza). Divorce can be suspended on conditions, and from the husband's side it is like an oath. The wife's request is to tie the payment to the condition of three divorces. When the husband violates the condition, no payment is due. In pure commutative contracts, such a conditional meaning is impossible, so 'على' is interpreted metaphorically as 'باء'. The passage concludes with two Quranic examples where 'على' indicates a condition: ‘I should not say about Allah except the truth’ and 'They pledge allegiance to you upon that they will not associate...' meaning with that condition.

Key Terms
على (ala): A preposition primarily indicating elevation and placement, used in legal language for obligation and, in divorce, for condition.
معاوضة محضة (mu'aawada mahdah): Pure commutative contracts where exchange is the primary objective, such as sale, lease, and marriage.
شرط (shart): A condition upon which a legal consequence depends; here it refers to the conditional meaning of 'على'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a wife says 'Divorce me three times for (على) one thousand dirhams' and the husband divorces her once, what is the ruling?
A) The husband owes the full thousand dirhams.
B) The husband owes one-third of the thousand dirhams.
C) The husband owes nothing.
D) The divorce is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The legal meaning of 'على' is context-dependent: obligation in debts, attachment in contracts, and condition in divorce—a key example of how linguistic analysis underpins Hanafi istinbat.

The Legal Semantics of the Preposition 'Ala in Hanafi Usul
Parsing the preposition 'ala's legal meanings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the meaning of 'ala as 'obligation', 'exchange', or 'condition' based on context, and apply the correct ruling in a contract or divorce scenario.

Core Idea
One preposition, three legal meanings: obligation (debt), exchange (sale), condition (divorce).

Prepositions carry enormous legal weight. In Hanafi usul, the word 'ala is paradigmatic: it shifts meaning depending on the domain of fiqh. In debts it imposes an obligation, in commutative contracts it signals exchange (like 'ba), and in divorce it introduces a condition. This lesson walks through the textual evidence and the famous dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn over a wife's payment when divorce is incomplete.

Guided Path
1
Identify the literal meaning
The passage states that 'ala is literally 'elevation' and 'imposition'. From this comes the legal sense of obligation (ijab/ilzam). In the phrase 'I have 1000 dirhams upon me', it creates a debt.
Except when combined with 'deposit' (wadi'a), where it negates obligation.
2
Analyze 'ala in commutative contracts
In sale, lease, and marriage, the contract is binding (luzum). The binding nature suits the idea of 'adhesion' (ilsaq), so 'ala is metaphorically used for 'ba' (exchange). Find the analogy.
Condition (shart) is impossible in these contracts because they are 'pure exchange'.
3
Analyze 'ala in divorce
Divorce can be conditional. Here 'ala retains its literal sense of 'imposition' but the imposition is conditional on the occurrence of three divorces. The wife's offer is a request to make payment conditional on the triple divorce.
The Sahibayn analogize to commutative contracts, but Abu Hanifa argues divorce is different.
4
Examine the Quranic evidence
Two verses: Al-A'raf 7:105 uses 'ala to mean 'about/concerning', showing obligation to speak truth. Al-Mumtahina 60:12 uses 'ala to introduce the condition of the pledge.
The second verse directly supports the conditional meaning.
5
Apply the ruling to the dispute
Wife says 'Divorce me three times on 1000 dirhams', husband divorces once. Abu Hanifa: no payment (condition unfulfilled). Sahibayn: proportional payment (333.33 dirhams). State the thamarah al-khilaf.
The text sides with Abu Hanifa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما كلمة على: فإنها وُضعت لوقوع الشيء على غيره، وارتفاعه وعُلوه فوقه، فصار هو موضوعاً للإيجاب والإلزام في قول الرجل : لفلان عليَّ ألف درهم: أنه دين، إلا أن يصل به الوديعة.
فإن دخلت في المعاوضات المحضة كانت بمعنى الباء إذا استعملت في البيع والإجارة والنكاح ؛ لأن اللزوم يناسب الإلصاق، فاستعير له.
وإذا استعملت في الطلاق كانت بمعنى الشرط عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله ، حتى إن من قالت له امرأتُه : طلقني ثلاثاً على ألف درهم، فطلقها واحدةً : لم يجب شيء عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله.
وعندهما: يجب ثلث الألف، ثلث الألف، كما في كما في قولها: بألف درهم. وقال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله : كلمة: على: للزوم، على ما قلنا، وليس بين الواقع وبين ما لزمها مقابلةٌ، بل بينهما معاقبة، وذلك معنى الشرط والجزاء، فصار هذا بمنزلة حقيقة هذه الكلمة، وقد أمكن العمل به؛ لأن الطلاق وإن دخله المالُ فيصلح تعليقه بالشروط، حتى إنه من جانب الزوج يمين، فيصير هذا منها طلباً لتعليق المال بشرط الثلاث، فإذا خالف لم يجب، وفي المعاوضات المحضة يستحيل معنى الشرط فوجب العمل بمجازه، وهو الباء.
قال الله تعالى: ﴿حَقِيقُ أَن لَّا أَقُولَ عَلَى اللَّهِ إِلَّا الْحَقَّ الأعراف / ١٠٥. وقال تعالى: (يُبا يعنَكَ عَلَى أَن لا يُشركن بِاللهِ شَيْئًا ) الممتحنة / ١٢، أي يُبَايِعْنَكَ بهذا الشرط.
As for the word 'ala: it was placed for the occurrence of a thing upon another, and its elevation and being above it. So it became a subject for imposition and obligation in a man's statement: 'I have one thousand dirhams due upon me to so-and-so' — that it is a debt, unless he connects it with a deposit.
If it enters into pure commutative contracts, it carries the meaning of 'ba' when used in sale, lease, and marriage, because bindingness suits adhesion, so it was borrowed for that.
If it is used in divorce, it carries the meaning of condition according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, such that if a woman says to her husband, 'Divorce me three times on one thousand dirhams,' and he divorces her once, nothing is due according to Abu Hanifa.
According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), one-third of the thousand is due, just as in her saying 'with one thousand dirhams.' Abu Hanifa said: The word 'ala is for binding, as we said, and there is no correspondence between what occurs and what is binding upon her, but rather there is a reciprocal relationship between them, and that is the meaning of condition and consequence. So this becomes like the real meaning of this word, and it is possible to act upon it because, although divorce involves money, it can be made conditional, such that from the husband's side it is an oath. So this becomes from her a request to make the money conditional upon the condition of three, so if he violates, nothing is due. In pure commutative contracts, the meaning of condition is impossible, so acting upon its metaphorical meaning, which is 'ba', is necessary.
Allah said: 'Truly I am worthy of not saying about Allah except the truth' (Al-A'raf 7:105). And He said: 'They swear allegiance to you upon [the condition] that they do not associate anything with Allah' (Al-Mumtahina 60:12), meaning they swear allegiance to you with this condition.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of the preposition 'ala in various legal contexts. The author first establishes its literal meaning: physical elevation or imposition. From this, it derives the legal meaning of obligation (ijab/ilzam) in debts. Then he distinguishes three contexts: (1) In pure commutative contracts (sale, lease, marriage), 'ala functions like the preposition 'ba' because the binding nature of these contracts aligns with the idea of adhesion (ilsaq). (2) In divorce, according to Abu Hanifa, 'ala indicates a condition (shart). The example: a wife says 'divorce me three times on one thousand dirhams' — the husband divorces once. Abu Hanifa rules no payment is due because 'ala here establishes a condition: payment is contingent on three divorces. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) analogize to commutative contracts and rule one-third is due. The author supports Abu Hanifa's view, arguing that divorce, unlike commutative contracts, can accommodate conditions because it is an oath-like act. (3) The two Quranic verses are cited as evidence: the first ('ala Allah) shows 'ala meaning 'about' or 'concerning' in the context of truthful speech; the second ('ala an la yushrikna) shows 'ala meaning 'upon the condition that'. Thus, 'ala's legal meaning varies by context.

Key Terms
على (ala): Preposition with multiple legal meanings: obligation, exchange, or condition depending on context
الزوم (al-luzum): Binding obligation
الشرط (al-shart): Condition; in this context, a conditional clause linking divorce and payment

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) 333.33 dirhams
C) 0 dirhams
D) 500 dirhams

Key Takeaway
The meaning of 'ala in Hanafi fiqh is context-dependent: obligation in debts, exchange in commutative contracts, and condition in divorce.

The Preposition 'Ila' and Its Legal Implications in Hanafi Usul
Explaining the legal implications of 'ila' in debts, divorce, and limits

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the preposition 'ila' as a ghaya (limit) in Hanafi usul, distinguish its effect on timing (ta'jil vs tanjiz), and apply the rule that a self-standing limit excludes what is beyond it.

Core Idea
Ila indicates termination of the limit, delaying the act it enters; but if the limit is self-standing, it excludes the limit itself.

In this passage, the author examines the preposition 'ila' (to/until) as a marker of ghaya (limit). Understanding its function is crucial for interpreting contractual terms, divorce expressions, and ritual acts. The analysis includes a dispute between Imam Muhammad and Zufar regarding the effect of 'ila' in divorce when no intention is expressed, and a general rule about whether the limit itself is included or excluded.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary meaning of 'ila'
The text states that 'ila' is placed for 'intiha al-ghaya' (termination of limit). Recognize that this is the foundational meaning used in debt terms and other contexts.
This is a lexical meaning, but its legal effect varies with context.
2
Analyze the divorce scenarios
When a man says 'You are divorced to (ila) a month', the outcome depends on intention: immediate effect if he intends tanjiz, delay if he intends idafa, and if no intention, there is a dispute between Zufar and the Hanafi school.
The dispute is within the Hanafi school; Zufar was a prominent Hanafi scholar.
3
Compare the Hanafi and Zufar positions
Zufar argues that 'ila' indicates timing and timing does not prevent immediate occurrence. The Hanafis (we say) argue that timing itself implies delay, so 'ila' must defer the divorce.
The thamarah al-khilaf: immediate vs. delayed divorce.
4
Extract the general principle about limits
The text gives two scenarios: (a) a self-standing limit (e.g., 'I sold from this garden to this garden') excludes the second garden; (b) if the beginning of the speech covers the whole, the limit only excludes what is beyond, so the limit itself remains included (e.g., elbows in wudu).
This principle is derived from the Quranic verse about fasting until night.
5
Apply the principle to the Quranic verse and khiyar
In Quran 2:187, 'ila al-layl' shows that the night is excluded from the fast. Abu Hanifa applied the same logic to the option period (khiyar): the limit is included.
This demonstrates how usul principles are applied to different fiqh topics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إلى فلانتهاء الغاية، لذلك وضعت، ولذلك استعملت آجال الديون.
وإذا دخلت في الطلاق في قول الرجل : أنت طالق إلى شهر : فإن نوى التنجيز: وقع.
وإن نوى الإضافة: تأخر.
وإن لم تكن له نية وقع للحال عند زفر رحمه الله ؛ لأن إلى: للتأجيل، والتأجيل لا يمنع الوقوع.
وقلنا نحن : إن التأجيل لتأخير ما يدخله، وها هنا دخل على أصل الطلاق، فأوجب تأخيره
والأصلُ في الغاية إذا كان قائماً بنفسه : لم تدخل في الحكم، مثل قول الرجل : بعتُ من هذا البستان إلى هذا البستان.
وقول الله تعالى: ﴿ثُمَّ أَتِمُّوا القِيَامَ إِلَى الَّيْلِ ﴾ البقرة / ۱۸۷ . إلا أن يكون صدرُ الكلام يقع على الجملة : فيكون غايةً لإخراج ما وراءها ، فيبقى داخلاً بمطلق الاسم، مثل ما قلنا في المرافق.
ولهذا قال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله في الغاية في الخيار إنه يدخل.
As for 'ila': it is for the termination of the limit, for that it was placed, and for that it is used for the terms of debts.
If it enters into divorce in a man's statement: 'You are divorced for (ila) one month': if he intends immediate effect (tanjiz), it occurs; if he intends deferment (idafa), it is delayed; and if he has no intention, it occurs immediately according to Zufar, may Allah have mercy on him, because 'ila' is for timing, and timing does not prevent occurrence.
But we say: Timing is for delaying what it enters, and here it enters upon the original divorce, so it necessitates its delay.
The principle regarding the limit: if it is self-standing, it does not enter into the ruling, like a man's statement: 'I sold from this garden to this garden.'
And the saying of Allah: 'Then complete the fast until (ila) the night' (Al-Baqarah 2:187). Unless the beginning of the speech applies to the whole, then it becomes a limit for excluding what is beyond it, so what is before remains included by the absolute name, like we said about the elbows.
For this reason, Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, said regarding the limit in the option (khiyar) that it enters.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage analyzes the preposition 'ila' (to/until) and its legal applications. First, its primary meaning is 'termination of limit' (intiha al-ghaya), used for debt terms. In divorce, if a man says 'You are divorced for one month' (using 'ila'), the ruling depends on intention: immediate effect if he intends tanjiz, deferment if he intends idafa. Zufar holds that without intention, it takes effect immediately because 'ila' indicates timing but does not prevent immediate occurrence. The Hanafi position (we say) is that 'ila' delays the divorce because it enters upon the original act. Then the passage discusses the general rule: when a limit is mentioned independently, what is beyond the limit is excluded (e.g., selling from one garden to another means the second garden is not included). The Quranic verse about fasting until night shows that the limit (night) is excluded (fast ends at night). However, if the beginning of the phrase covers the whole, the limit only excludes what is beyond it, as in the case of washing the hands to the elbows (the elbows are included). Abu Hanifa applies this to the option period (khiyar) where the limit is included.

Key Terms
إلى (ila): Preposition indicating the end limit (intiha al-ghaya)
التنجيز (al-tanjiz): Immediate effect
الإضافة (al-idafa): Deferment to a future time

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Immediately
B) At the end of the month
C) It depends on the wife's intention
D) It is invalid

Key Takeaway
The preposition 'ila' (to/until) indicates a termination of limit; its legal effect depends on intention and context, with the Hanafi default being deferment in divorce, and the general rule that a self-standing limit excludes what is beyond it.

Oath Particles in Hanafi Usul: تالله, أيم الله, and لعمر الله
Explanation of permissible oath particles and their derivations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the permissible forms of oath particles in Hanafi usul, distinguish the Hanafi position on 'أيم الله' from the Kufan view, and explain the grammatical rule for the accusative when the oath particle is omitted.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school defines 'أيم الله' as an indivisible particle for oaths, not derived from 'يمين', and holds that when the oath letter is omitted, the word 'الله' remains in the accusative.

In this lesson, we examine the rules governing oath particles (حروف القسم) in the Hanafi school. The source text explains which names can take the تاء oath, the derivation of 'أيم الله', and the grammatical case of 'الله' when the particle is omitted. Understanding these details clarifies the Hanafi approach to linguistic analysis in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule for the تاء oath particle
The text states that the تاء used in oaths only enters the name 'الله' because He is the one most frequently sworn by. Thus, 'تالله' is permissible, but 'تالرحمن' and 'تالرحيم' are not.
This is a linguistic restriction specific to the oath تاء, not a general grammatical rule.
2
Analyze the omission of the oath particle
When the oath letter (like واو or باء) is omitted for brevity, the word 'الله' remains. The Hanafi school (following the Basran grammarians) holds that it is in the accusative case (نصب), while the Kufans hold it is genitive (خفض). The text affirms the Basran/Hanafi position: 'وهو مذهبنا'.
This dispute is purely grammatical; both views accept the validity of the oath.
3
Examine the derivation of 'أيم الله'
Two views are presented. The Kufans derive 'أيم الله' from 'أيمن' (plural of يمين). The Basrans/Hanafis consider it an indivisible particle (صلة وضعت للقسم) with no derivation, akin to words like صه and مه. Evidence includes the linking hamza (همزة الوصل) not being part of a plural pattern.
The Hanafi position is that the hamza is a hamzat al-wasl, which drops when preceded by a letter.
4
Understand the phrase 'لعمر الله'
The lam is for ibtida' (ابتداء), and 'umr means existence. So the phrase means 'by the existence of Allah'. This is considered an explicit statement of the oath's meaning, analogous to a man saying 'I made this slave your property for a thousand dirhams' as an explicit statement of sale.
This shows that explicit statements of meaning are treated like the actual contracts or oaths themselves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول والثاني، فقيل : لا تدخل إلا في اسم الله تعالى؛ لأنه هو المقسم به غالباً، فجاز تالله ، ولم يَجُزْ: تالرحمن، وتالرحيم.
- وقد يُحذف حرفُ القَسَم تخفيفاً، فيقال: الله لأفعلن كذا. لكنه بالنصب عند أهل البصرة، وهو مذهبنا، وبالخفض: عند أهل الكوفة.
وقد ذكر في الجامع ما يتصل بهذا الأصل، مثل قول الرجل : والله الله، والله الرحمن والرحيم على ما ذكرنا في «الجامع». وأما : أيم الله : فأصله : أيمن الله ، وهو جَمْعُ: يمين، وهذا مذهب أهل الكوفة.
وأما مذهب أهل البصرة، وهو قولنا أن ذلك صلة وضعت للقسم، لا اشتقاق لها.
مثل : صَهْ، ومَهُ، وبَحْ، والهمزة للوصل، ألا يُرى أنها توصل إذا تقدمها حرفٌ مثل سائر حروف الوصل، ولو كان لبناء الجمع وصيغته : لَمَا ذهب عند الوصل، والكلام فيه يطول.
وأما : لعَمْرُ الله : فإن اللام فيه للابتداء، والعمر: هو البقاء، ومعناه: لبقاء الله، هو الذي أُقسم به، فيصير تصريحاً لمعنى القسم، بمنزلة قول الرجل : جعلتُ هذا العبد ملكاً لك بألف درهم : أنه تصريح لمعنى البيع، فيجري مجراه ، فكذلك هذا.
For the first and second, it was said: it [the تاء] only enters the name of Allah the Exalted, because He is the one sworn by mostly. So 'tallah' is permissible, and 'tar-rahman' and 'tar-rahim' are not permissible.
- And sometimes the letter of oath is omitted for brevity, so it is said: 'Allah la-af'alanna kadha' (By Allah, I will do such). But it is in the accusative case according to the Basrans, and that is our school, and in the genitive according to the Kufans.
And it has been mentioned in al-Jami' what relates to this principle, such as a man's saying: 'Wallah Allah', 'Wallah ar-Rahman ar-Rahim' as we mentioned in al-Jami'. As for 'Aym Allah': its origin is 'Ayman Allah', and it is a plural of yamin, and this is the school of the Kufans.
As for the school of the Basrans, which is our statement, that [alif] is a particle placed for oath, having no derivation.
Like: 'Sah', 'Mah', 'Bah', and the hamza is for linking. Do you not see that it is linked when a letter precedes it, like other linking letters? If it were for the construction and form of the plural, it would not drop upon linking. And the discussion about it is lengthy.
As for 'La-'amru Allah': the lam is for initiation, and al-'umr is existence, and its meaning is: by the existence of Allah, which is the thing sworn by. So it becomes an explicit statement of the meaning of the oath, like a man's saying: 'I made this slave your property for a thousand dirhams' — that is an explicit statement of the meaning of sale, so it takes its ruling. Similarly this.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage discusses the rules regarding particles used in oaths (qasam). It begins with the rule that the letter 'ta' (تاء) used in oaths (as in تالله) can only be attached to the name 'Allah', not to 'ar-Rahman' or 'ar-Rahim', because 'Allah' is the name most frequently used in oaths. Then it explains that the letter of oath may be omitted for brevity, resulting in phrases like 'الله لأفعلن' and mentions the grammatical dispute: Basrans (including the Hanafis) hold that the word after omission is in the accusative, while Kufans hold it is in the genitive. The author references 'al-Jami' for further details. Then it discusses 'أيم الله': according to Kufans it is derived from 'أيمن' (plural of يمين), but according to Basrans/Hanafis it is an indivisible particle. Evidence includes that it behaves like other indivisible particles (صه, مه, بح) in that the hamza is a linking hamza (hamzat al-wasl), not part of a plural pattern. Finally, 'لعمر الله' is analyzed: the lam is for isti'naf (initiation), 'umr means existence, so the oath is by the existence of Allah, and this is considered an explicit expression of the meaning of the oath, analogous to explicitly stating the meaning of a sale.

Key Terms
تالله (tallah): A specific oath particle formed by attaching تاء to the name الله.
أيم الله (aym allah): An indivisible particle used for oaths according to the Hanafi school; not derived from يمين.
لعمر الله (la-'amru llah): An oath expression meaning 'by the existence of Allah'; considered an explicit statement of the oath's meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Emphatic (توكيد)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines oath particles with specific linguistic rules: only 'Allah' takes the تاء, 'أيم الله' is an indivisible particle, and omitted oath particles leave the name in the accusative.

Conditional Particles 'In' and 'Idha' in Hanafi Usul
Analyzing linguistic and legal differences between 'in' and 'idha' in conditional oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'in' and 'idha' in Hanafi usul, explain the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the ruling to conditional divorce oaths.

Core Idea
A single word—whether 'idha' retains temporal meaning—determines if a conditional divorce takes effect immediately or at death.

The Arabic conditional particles 'in' and 'idha' may seem interchangeable, but Hanafi usul al-fiqh draws a critical linguistic distinction. This lesson unpacks the debate between Abu Hanifa, who follows Kufan grammar, and the Sahibayn, who side with the Basrans, and shows how this grammatical disagreement yields different legal outcomes in marriage oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particles and their primary functions
State what each particle is used for linguistically: 'in' is a pure conditional for uncertain events; 'idha' is primarily a time particle.
2
Understand the Hanafi rule for 'in'
Explain that 'in' suspends a ruling on a condition that is uncertain (khatar). In the divorce example, the condition 'If I do not divorce you' only ceases at death.
This is the default ruling.
3
Contrast the two views on 'idha'
Kufans (Abu Hanifa): 'idha' can be a conditional, dropping temporal meaning. Basrans (Sahibayn): 'idha' retains temporal meaning even when used conditionally, like 'mata'.
The key difference is whether the particle creates real conditionality or just indicates time.
4
Apply to the divorce scenario
Using 'idha' in a similar oath, Abu Hanifa rules divorce at death; the Sahibayn rule immediately. Identify the thamarah al-khilaf.
This shows how linguistic analysis directly changes a fiqh ruling.
5
Evaluate the linguistic evidence
Consider the Quranic examples (81:1, 30:36) and common speech. Which view aligns with the literal meaning? Discuss the strength of each position.
Both positions have valid linguistic support; the dispute is legitimate ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما نذكر في هذا الكتاب من هذه الجمل ما تُبتنى عليه مسائل أصحابنا رحمهم الله على الإشارة. وأما حرف : إن : فهو الأصل في هذا الباب، وضع للشرط، وإنما يدخل على كلِّ أمرٍ معدوم على خطر، ليس بكائن، لا محالة. أمثلته. تقول : إن زُرتَني أكرمتك. ولا يجوز : إن جاء غد أكرمتك. وأثره: أن يمنع العلةَ عن الحكم أصلاً حتى يبطل التعليق، وهذا تكثر. وعلى هذا قلنا : إذا قال الرجل لامرأته : إن لم أطلقكِ فأنت طالق ثلاثاً: إنها لا تطلق حتى يموت الزوج، فتطلق في آخر جزء من أجزاء حياته ؛ لأن العدم لا يثبت إلا بقرب موته. وكذلك : إذا ماتت المرأة : طلقت ثلاثاً قبل موتها، في أصح الروايتين. وأما إذا : فإن مذهب أهل اللغة والنحو من الكوفيين فيها: أنها تصلح للوقت وللشرط على السواء، فيُجازى بها مرةً، ولا يُجازى بها أخرى، فإذا جُوزي بها : فإنما يُجازى بها على سقوط معنى الوقت عنها ، كأنها حرف شرط، وهو قول أبي حنيفة رحمه الله. وأما البصريون من أهل اللغة والنحو فقد قالوا: إنها للوقت، وقد تُستعمل للشرط من غير سقوط معنى الوقت عنها، مثل: متى، فإنها للوقت، لا يسقط عنها ذلك بحال، والمجازاة بها لازمة في غير موضع الاستفهام. والمجازاة بـ: إذا غير لازمة، بل هي في حيّز الجواز. وإلى هذا الطريق ذهب أبو يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله. وبيانه فيمن قال لامرأته إذا لم أطلقك فأنت طالق : قال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله : لا يقع الطلاق حتى يموت أحدهما، مثل قوله : إن لم أطلقك. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله : يقع كما فرغ من اليمين، مثل قوله : متى لم أطلقك ؛ لأن : إذا : اسم للوقت بمنزلة سائر الظروف، وهو للوقت المستقبل، وقد استعملت للوقت خالصاً. فقيل : كيف الرُّطَب إذا اشتدَّ الحَرُّ؟ : أي حينئذ. ولا يصلح : إن : ها هنا. ويقال : آتيك إذا اشتدَّ الحَرَّ، ولا يجوز إن اشتد الحر؛ لأن الشرط يقتضي خطراً وتردُّداً هو أصله. وإذا: تدخل للوقت على أمرٍ كائن أو منتظر، لا محالة، كقوله تعالى: ﴿إِذَا الشَّمْسُ كُوِّرَتْ﴾ التكوير / ١ . وتستعمل للمفاجأة، قال الله تعالى: ﴿إِذَا هُمْ يَقْنَطُونَ﴾ الروم / ٣٦ . وإذا كان كذلك كان مفسراً من وجه ولم يكن مبهماً، فلم تكن شرطاً، إلا أنه قد تُستعمل فيه مستعاراً مع قيام معنى الوقت، مثل: متى، أن المجازاة في متى ألزم، ومع هذا لم تسقط عنه حقيقته، وهو : مع
We only mention in this book from these sentences what the issues of our companions (may Allah have mercy on them) are built upon by indication. As for the letter 'in': it is the original in this chapter, placed for condition, and it only enters upon every non-existent matter at risk, not necessarily occurring. Its examples: you say 'If you visit me, I honor you.' And it is not permissible: 'If tomorrow comes, I honor you.' Its effect: that it prevents the cause from the ruling completely until the attachment is invalidated, and this is plentiful. And upon this we said: If a man says to his wife 'If I do not divorce you, you are thrice divorced': she is not divorced until the husband dies, so she is divorced in the last part of his life because non-existence is only established near his death. Likewise, if the woman dies: she is divorced three times before her death, in the more correct of the two narrations. As for 'idha': the school of the linguists and grammarians from the Kufans is that it is suitable for time and for condition equally, so it is used for jazā' sometimes and not used for jazā' other times. When it is used for jazā', it is used with dropping the meaning of time from it, as if it is a conditional particle, and that is the saying of Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him). As for the Basrans from the linguists and grammarians, they said: it is for time, and it may be used for condition without dropping the meaning of time from it, like 'mata' (when) which is for time, not dropping that in any case, and jazā' with it is necessary in non-interrogative contexts. And jazā' with 'idha' is not necessary, rather it is in the realm of permissibility. And to this way went Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them). Its clarification: concerning one who said to his wife 'If I do not divorce you, you are divorced': Abu Hanifa said: divorce does not occur until one of them dies, like his saying 'If I do not divorce you.' Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: it occurs as soon as he finishes the oath, like his saying 'When I do not divorce you,' because 'idha' is a noun for time like other adverbs, and it is for future time, and it has been used purely for time. So it is said: 'How are the dates when the heat intensifies?' i.e., at that time. And 'in' is not suitable here. And it is said: 'I come to you when the heat intensifies,' and it is not permissible 'if the heat intensifies' because the condition implies risk and uncertainty which is its essence. And 'idha' enters for time upon a matter that exists or is expected necessarily, like His saying: 'When the sun is wrapped up.' And it is used for suddenness, Allah said: 'When they despair.' And if that is so, it is explicative in one aspect and not ambiguous, so it is not a condition, except that it may be used metaphorically while the meaning of time remains, like 'mata' (when), that jazā' with 'mata' is necessary, and despite this its reality is not dropped, which is: with...
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul deals with the linguistic and legal implications of the conditional particles 'in' (إن) and 'idha' (إذا). The author begins by stating that only principles foundational to Hanafi legal issues will be mentioned. 'In' is the primary conditional particle, used for uncertain future events; its effect is to suspend a ruling on a condition. The example of a conditional divorce illustrates that when a man says 'If I do not divorce you, you are thrice divorced,' the divorce occurs only at the last moment of his life because the condition (non-divorce) only ceases to be true at death. The same applies if the wife dies first—she is divorced before death in the stronger narration. The discussion then turns to 'idha.' The Kufan linguists, whose view Abu Hanifa adopts, hold that 'idha' can function as either a time particle or a conditional, but when used conditionally it loses its temporal meaning. The Basrans, followed by Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, maintain that 'idha' remains a time particle even when used conditionally, similar to 'mata' (when). In the divorce scenario, this leads to a practical difference: Abu Hanifa says divorce does not occur until death (like with 'in'), while the Sahibayn say it occurs immediately upon the oath, exactly as with 'mata.' The Basran argument is supported by linguistic evidence: 'idha' is a noun for future time, used purely temporally in phrases like 'when the heat intensifies,' where 'in' would be incorrect. The Quranic verses 'When the sun is wrapped up' and 'When they despair' show 'idha' indicating a definite or sudden event, not a conditional. Thus, 'idha' is generally not a conditional particle, though it can be used metaphorically as such while retaining its temporal essence.

Key Terms
إن (In): A conditional particle (ḥarf al-sharṭ) used to suspend a ruling on a future uncertain event. It applies only to non-existent matters that are at risk.
إذا (Idha): A particle used primarily for time (ẓarf zamān), indicating future time. It can also be used metaphorically as a conditional, but according to Basran grammarians it retains its temporal meaning.
شرط (Shart): Condition; a linguistic element that makes a ruling contingent on the occurrence of a future event. It requires uncertainty (khaṭar) and doubt (taraddud).
وقت (Waqt): Time; the temporal dimension. In this context, particles like 'idha' are used for time, indicating when an event occurs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Immediately after the oath

C) Only if he never divorces her

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's intra-madhhab disagreement on 'idha' shows how linguistic analysis (usul al-lughah) directly impacts fiqh rulings, with no single universally accepted position.

Dispute on Conditional Delegation and Definitions of Ambiguous Words
Usul dispute with linguistic clarifications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi and Sahibayn positions on conditional delegation involving ambiguous expressions, and identify the linguistic classifications of 'kam' and 'hayth' as nouns according to Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Intention is required for valid conditional delegation per Abu Hanifa; Sahibayn treat intangible conditions as attached to their origin.

This passage from Al-Bazdawi presents a classic usul dispute: when a husband delegates a conditional divorce to his wife, does his intention matter? The two main disciples of Abu Hanifa offer a different view based on the principle of indication (ishara). The passage then clarifies the linguistic categories of 'kam' and 'hayth', which often appear in oaths and conditions, showing how precise definitions resolve legal ambiguity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute participants
The text starts with 'He said' (Abu Hanifa) and then 'They two said' (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Note who holds which position.
The pronoun 'He' refers to Imam Abu Hanifa; 'They two' are the Sahibayn.
2
Parse the Hanafi position on conditional delegation
Abu Hanifa states that the condition delegated to the wife is valid only if the husband intended that condition. Focus on the phrase 'بشرط نية الزوج' (with the condition of the husband's intention).
This emphasizes that mere utterance without inner resolve is insufficient.
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's principle of indication
They argue that what cannot be pointed to (intangible conditions) has its condition and description in the same status as its origin. The legal attachment follows the origin automatically.
The key term is 'الإشارة' (indication); only tangible things can be pointed to.
4
Understand the definitions of 'kam' and 'hayth'
The text defines 'kam' as a noun for actual number, and 'hayth' as a noun for ambiguous place that enters upon the will. These classifications resolve ambiguity in oaths.
These are linguistic clarifications that affect legal interpretation (e.g., in conditional divorce).
5
Connect definitions to the dispute
Both parts relate to ambiguous expressions in legal context: the first shows how ambiguity in conditions is treated, the second clarifies words that often create ambiguity.
The overall theme is mujmal (ambiguous) and its resolution (bayan).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو الحال مفوَّضاً إليها بشرط نية الزوج. وقالا : ما لا يقبل الإشارة: فحاله ووصفه بمنزلة أصله، فيتعلق الأصل بتعلقه. وأما : كم : فاسم للعدد الذي هو الواقع. و حيث : اسمٌ لمكان مُبهم دخل على المشيئة، والله أعلم المراد.
And it is the condition delegated to her with the condition of the husband's intention. And they two said: That which does not accept indication: its condition and description are in the position of its origin, so the origin is attached by its attachment. And as for 'kam': it is a noun for the number that is actual. And 'haythu': it is a noun for an ambiguous place that enters upon the will, and Allah knows best the intended meaning.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling concerning conditional delegation in marriage or divorce. The first sentence states that a condition delegated to the wife is valid only if the husband intended that condition. This is likely the position of Imam Abu Hanifa. Then the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) are quoted as saying that something which cannot be pointed to (i.e., an intangible condition) has its condition and description in the same status as its origin; thus the legal attachment follows the attachment of the origin. The passage then defines the words 'kam' (how many) as a noun for actual number, and 'hayth' (where) as a noun for an ambiguous place that enters upon the will. This is a clarification of ambiguous expressions used in oaths or conditions. The author concludes with 'And Allah knows best the intended meaning.'

Key Terms
الحال (al-hal): The condition or state that is delegated, here referring to a conditional clause in delegating divorce or similar.
الإشارة (al-ishara): Indication or pointing, used here to describe what can be physically pointed to; abstract conditions cannot be indicated.
كم (kam): A noun (ism) for the actual number, used as an interrogative or declarative for quantity.
حيث (haythu): A noun for an ambiguous place, used as a conditional or locative particle.

Key Takeaway
In conditional delegation, intention is required per Abu Hanifa, but the Sahibayn deem intangible conditions as automatically attached to the origin; 'kam' and 'hayth' are nouns that resolve ambiguity in oaths.

The Dispute on the Meaning of Janaaya Establishing Qisas
Dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the definition of janaaya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two positions on the meaning of janaaya that establishes qisas and evaluate the Hanafi argument against the Sahibayn using the principle of completeness.

Core Idea
The complete form is the basis for qisas; the deficient cannot establish it, especially in punishments averted by suspicions.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, deriving the effective cause (illa) of a ruling requires precision. This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute over the meaning (ma'na) that makes qisas obligatory for injury. Abu Hanifa insists on a wound that breaks the body's structure, while the Sahibayn include any lethal force. The debate turns on whether the deficient (non-wounding) act can serve as the basis for qisas, especially given the principle that punishments are averted by doubts.

Guided Path
1
State the issue
Identify the contested ma'na (operative meaning) that triggers qisas for injury. Both sides agree qisas is based on equivalence, but they differ on what constitutes the complete janaaya.
The issue is not whether qisas applies to killing, but specifically to non-fatal injuries and the definition of janaaya.
2
Identify Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa holds that the ma'na is a wound that breaks the body's structure outwardly and inwardly. His proof is the principle of completeness: the complete (kamil) is the basis; the deficient cannot be the origin, especially in hudud-like matters averted by suspicions.
The 'complete' injury mirrors the completeness of existence; the soul is the target, and the body is the medium.
3
Identify the Sahibayn's position and proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that the ma'na is any force the body cannot bear, leading to death, regardless of wound. They reason that qisas is a deterrent and the body is merely a means; the real objective is protecting life.
They cite the example of killing with a great stone: qisas is wajib even without a wound because lethal force was used.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa responds that the body is not a mere means; the soul (nafs) is the true subject of injury. Since the spirit cannot be directly injured, the complete injury is one that breaks the body's structure, which is the closest to harming the soul. He accuses the Sahibayn of error in considering the body as a means.
The response emphasizes that in punishments, the complete form must be used as the basis to avoid extending qisas to doubtful cases.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: In killing with a great stone (no wound), the Sahibayn obligate qisas; Abu Hanifa says no qisas because the complete janaaya (wound breaking structure) is absent. This illustrates how the ma'na affects actual rulings.
Other examples: poisoning, strangulation, drowning – all would be treated differently depending on the position.
