الجناية بالجرح ، وما يُشبهه، والحكم جزاء يُبتنى على المماثلة في الجناية، وكان ثابتاً بذلك المعنى. واختلف فيه، فقال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله : ذلك المعنى هو الجرح الذي ينقض البنية ظاهراً وباطناً. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله : معناه ما لا تطيق البنية احتماله ، فتهلك جرحاً كان أو لم يكن حتى قالا: يجب القود بالقتل بالحجر العظيم؛ لأنا نعلم أن القصاص وجب عقوبةً وزجراً عن انتهاك حرمة الأنفس، وصيانة حياتها، وانتهاك حرمتها بما لا تطيق حمله، ولا تبقى معه، فأما الجَرحُ على البدن: فلا عبرة به، إنما البدن وسيلة، فما يقوم بغير وسيلة : كان أكمل. والجواب لأبي حنيفة رحمه الله : أن معنى الجناية هو: ما لا تطيق النفس احتماله، لكن الأصل في كل فعل هو الكمال، والنقصان بالعوارض، فلا يُجعل الناقص أصلاً ، بل الكاملُ يُجعل أصلاً، ثم يتعدى حكمه إلى الناقص إن كان من جنس ما يثبت بالشبهات، فأما أن يُجعل الناقص أصلاً خصوصاً فيما يُدرأ بالشبهات : فلا. وها هنا الكامل فيما قلنا ما ينقض البنية ظاهراً وباطناً هو الكامل في النقض على مقابلة كمال الوجود. وقولهما : إن البدن وسيلةٌ : وَهَمٌ وغَلَطٌ ؛ لأنا لا نعني بهذا الجناية على الجسم، لكن نعني به الجناية على النفس التي هي معنى الإنسان خلقةً، فالقصاص مقابل بذلك، أما الجسم ففَرْعُ ، وأما الروح : فلا تقبل الجناية. ومعنى الإنسان خلقة بدمه ،وطبائعه فلا تتكامل الجناية عليه إلا ويقع على معناه قصداً، فصار هذا أولى، خصوصاً في العقوبات. وقد ذكر عن ابن بشير، والطبراني من حديث ابن مسعود والدارقطني من حديث أبي هريرة، وابن أبي شيبة من مرسل الحسن، وفي أسانيدها مقال.
The injury by wounding and what resembles it, and the ruling is a recompense based on equivalence in the injury, and it was established by that meaning. And there is disagreement on it: Abu Hanifa said: that meaning is the wound that breaks the structure outwardly and inwardly. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: its meaning is what the structure cannot bear, so it perishes, whether wound or not, so much so that they said: qisas is obligatory for killing with a great stone; because we know that qisas was made obligatory as a punishment and deterrent against violating the sanctity of lives, and protecting their life, and violating their sanctity with what they cannot bear to carry, nor survive with it. As for the wound on the body, it is not considered; the body is only a means, and what exists without a means is more complete. The answer for Abu Hanifa: that the meaning of injury is what the soul cannot bear to endure, but the principle in every action is completeness, and deficiency is by accidents, so the deficient is not made the basis, rather the complete is made the basis, then its ruling extends to the deficient if it is of the kind that is established by suspicions. But that the deficient be made the basis, especially in what is averted by suspicions, no. And here the complete, in what we said, is what breaks the structure outwardly and inwardly; it is the complete in breaking in contrast to the completeness of existence. And their saying: the body is a means is a delusion and error; because we do not mean by this the injury to the body, but we mean by it the injury to the soul which is the meaning of the human being by creation, so qisas is in exchange for that. As for the body, it is a branch; as for the spirit, it does not accept injury. And the meaning of the human being by creation is with his blood and his natures, so injury upon him is not complete unless it falls upon his meaning intentionally, so this is more appropriate, especially in punishments. And it has been narrated from Ibn Bashir, al-Tabarani from hadith of Ibn Mas'ud, al-Daraqutni from hadith of Abu Hurayra, Ibn Abi Shayba from mursal of al-Hasan, and in their chains there is discussion.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage presents a usul dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the definition of the meaning (ma'na) that establishes qisas for injury. Abu Hanifa holds that the operative meaning is a wound that breaks the body's structure both externally and internally, as this is the complete form of injury. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) argue that the meaning is any harm that the body cannot endure, leading to death, whether a wound or not, because the body is a mere means and the real target is the soul. The response from Abu Hanifa's perspective argues that in every action, the complete form is the basis; the deficient (e.g., a non-wounding lethal act) cannot be the basis, especially in cases where suspicions avert punishments. The passage then clarifies that the injury is intended on the soul (the meaning of the human), not the body itself, so qisas is matched accordingly. The mention of a stone confirms the Sahibayn's position. The final hadith citation appears to be an external support but is not fully integrated.

Key Terms
الجناية (al-jinaya): An injury that causes harm, either by wound or other means.
القصاص (al-qisas): Retaliation in kind for injury or killing.
المعنى (al-ma'na): The legally operative meaning or cause for a ruling.
الكامل (al-kamil): The complete or perfect form.
الناقص (al-naqis): The deficient or incomplete form.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The Sahibayn require a wound for qisas; Abu Hanifa does not.
B) Abu Hanifa considers killing with a great stone as subject to qisas; the Sahibayn do not.
C) The Sahibayn obligate qisas for killing with a great stone; Abu Hanifa does not.
D) Both agree on qisas for stone killing but differ on the reason.

Key Takeaway
The effective cause for qisas must be the complete janaaya (wound breaking structure), not any lethal force, due to the principle that the complete is the basis and punishments are averted by suspicions.

Intent Cannot Restrict General Conditions: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Usul dispute: intent vs general wording

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that general wording ('amm) cannot be restricted by hidden intent, and identify the Shafi'i counterposition.

Core Idea
Hidden intent does not narrow general wording; legal certainty relies on the literal meaning of the condition.

In this passage, Al-Bazdawi presents the Hanafi position on the role of intention in conditional oaths and vows. Through a series of examples, he shows that a general condition applies to all instances within its literal scope, regardless of what the speaker secretly intended. This contrasts with the Shafi'i view that allows intention to specify the condition. Understanding this principle is critical for deriving rulings from general texts in Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
State the rule at issue
Identify the core question: Can hidden intention restrict the scope of a general conditional statement? Determine that the Hanafi answer is no, unless the wording itself is ambiguous.
2
Analyze the iddah example
Examine the first example: a husband commands his wife to observe iddah after consummation, intending divorce. The command implies a divorce has occurred. Note that this shows iqtida' (implication) rather than specification by intent.
The divorce is revocable and not three, because the implication is a single divorce.
3
Examine the conditional oath examples
For each example (eating, drinking, going out, bathing, unnamed doer), identify the general wording and the specific intent. Confirm that the Hanafi ruling consistently rejects the restrictive intent.
The general wording applies to all possibilities.
4
Contrast with the Shafi'i view
Understand the opposing position: Shafi'is prioritize the swearer's intention. Then see the Hanafi rebuttal: clear wording is necessary for legal certainty; otherwise, contracts and oaths become unreliable.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf in usul.
5
Identify the linguistic basis (iqtida' vs hadhf)
The passage ends by distinguishing implication (iqtida') from elision (hadhf) for brevity. Recognize that iqtida' is linguistically sound and does not depend on hidden intent, supporting the Hanafi stance.
The cut-off text likely completes the proof of iqtida'.

وإذا قال الرجل لامرأته بعد الدخول بها: اعتدي، ونوى الطلاق وقع الطلاق؛ مقتضى الأمر بالاعتداد. ولهذا لم تصح نية الثلاث، ولهذا كان رجعياً. ومثال خلاف الشافعي رحمه الله: إن أكلتُ فعبدي حر، أو: إن شربت، ونوى خصوص الطعام أو الشراب: لم يصدق عندنا. ومَن قال: إن خرجتُ فعبدي حر، ونوى مكاناً دون مكان: لم يصدق عندنا. ومَن قال: إن اغتسلتُ فعبدي حرِّ، ونوى تخصيص الأسباب: لم يصدق عندنا؛ لما قلنا. ولو قال: إن اغتسل الليلة في هذه الدار فعبدي حر، فلم يُسم الفاعل، ونوى خصوص الفاعل: لم يصدق عندنا. بخلاف قوله: إن اغتسل أحد أو: إن اغتسلتُ غُسلاً. وقد يُشكل على السامع الفصل بين المقتضى وبين المحذوف على وجه الاختصار، وهو ثابت لغةً. وآية ذلك: أن ما اقتضى غيره: ثبت عند صحة الاقتضاء، وإذا كان
And if a man says to his wife after consummation: 'Observe iddah,' and intends divorce, divorce occurs, by implication of the command to observe iddah. For this reason, the intention of three [divorces] is not valid, and for this reason it is revocable. And an example of disagreement with al-Shafi'i: If [someone says]: 'If I eat then my slave is free,' or 'If I drink,' and intends a particular food or drink, it is not believed according to us. And whoever says: 'If I go out then my slave is free,' and intends a place other than the place [he goes out], it is not believed according to us. And whoever says: 'If I take a bath then my slave is free,' and intends a specification of the reasons, it is not believed according to us, due to what we said. And if he says: 'If tonight someone bathes in this house then my slave is free,' and does not name the doer, and intends a specific doer, it is not believed according to us. Contrary to his saying: 'If one of you bathes' or 'If I take a bath.' And it may be confusing for the listener to distinguish between the implied and the elided for brevity, but it is linguistically established. And the proof of that: that which implies something else is established when the implication is sound, and if
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi position on the role of intent in conditional statements. The first example: a husband commanding his wife to observe iddah after consummation – the command implies a divorce that has already occurred, so the wife must observe iddah. The divorce is revocable and cannot be three. Then the text contrasts the Hanafi view with that of al-Shafi'i in several examples: if a person makes a general conditional statement (e.g., 'If I eat, my slave is free') but intends a specific kind of food or drink, the Hanafi school does not accept that restrictive intent. The reason is that the wording is general, and the intention cannot override the literal meaning. The exception is when the wording itself specifies the condition, such as 'If one of you bathes' (which implies any person) or 'If I take a bath' (which specifies the doer). The final remark addresses the linguistic distinction between iqtida' (implication) and hadhf (elision) for brevity, stating that the former is established and valid.

Key Terms
النية (al-niyyah): The internal intention of the speaker; in Hanafi usul, it cannot restrict a general expression unless the expression itself is open to specification.
العموم (al-'umum): Generality; coverage of all individuals of a category by a word or phrase.
الاقتضاء (al-iqtida'): Implication; a word's meaning that requires another element for completeness or legal effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The slave is freed only if he eats that specific fruit.
B) The slave is freed upon eating any food.
C) The oath is invalid due to the restrictive intent.
D) The intention is accepted, and the slave is freed only if he eats that specific fruit.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, general wording governs all its instances; hidden intent cannot restrict it, unlike Shafi'i where intention can specify general conditions.

Refuting the Inference from 'Water is from Water'
Critique of the Ansar's inference from the hadith 'Water is from water'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical fallacy in the Ansar's inference from the hadith 'Water is from water' and explain the Hanafi counter-argument using Quran 9:36.

Core Idea
Attaching a ruling to a specific description does not negate it elsewhere.

This lesson unpacks the first part of the Hanafi refutation against the claim that a specific description implies negation. The passage begins with the famous hadith 'Water is from water' which the Ansar understood to exempt mere contact from requiring ghusl. The Hanafis challenge this reasoning, citing Quran 9:36 and logical principles.

Guided Path
1
State the opposing inference
Identify the hadith and the Ansar's conclusion: ghusl is not due for mere contact because the hadith says 'water is from water,' implying the absence of water (semen) negates the obligation.
The hadith is often interpreted to mean that ghusl is required only when water (semen) is emitted.
2
Present the Hanafi response
The Hanafis declare this inference false. They cite Quran 9:36 where injustice is forbidden at all times, not only in sacred months, showing that a specific description does not restrict a ruling to that context alone.
The verse uses the sacred months but the ruling on injustice is universal.
3
Explain the logical argument
If the opponent means the ruling is not established elsewhere by the text itself, the Hanafis agree. But if they claim the text prevents the ruling elsewhere, that is an error; the text only affirms in its named context, not negates elsewhere.
The text cannot both affirm in one place and negate in another simply by mentioning a description.
4
Invoke consensus on ta'lil
The jurists agree on the validity of reasoning by cause (ta'lil). If a specific description prevented extension, analogy would contradict the text, which is false.
Ta'lil allows the ruling to apply to other cases that share the underlying cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالوا: وذلك مثل قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام «الماء من الماء»: فهم الأنصار رضي عنهم من ذلك أن الغُسل لا يجب بالإكسال؛ لعدم الماء. وقلنا نحن: هذا باطل، وذلك كثير في الكتاب والسنة. قال الله تعالى: ﴿ذَلِكَ الدِّينُ الْقَيِّمُ فَلَا تَظْلِمُوا فِيهِنَّ أَنفُسَكُمْ﴾ [التوبة/36]، والظلم حرام في كل وقت. ولأنه يقال له: إن أردت أن هذا الحكم غير ثابت في غير المسمى بالنص فكذلك عندنا؛ لأن حكم النص في غيره لا يثبت به، بل بعلة النص. وإن عنى به أنه لا يثبت فيه بكون النص مانعاً: فهذا غلط ظاهر؛ لأن النص لم يتناوله، فكيف يمنع؟ ولأنه لإيجاب الحكم في المسمى، فكيف يوجب النفي وهو ضده؟ وقد أجمع الفقهاء على جواز التعليل ولو كان لخصوص الاسم أثر بالمنع في غيره: لصار التعليل على مضادة النص، وهو باطل.
وأما: «الماء من الماء»: فإن الاستدلال منها كان بلام المعرفة وهي لاستغراق الجنس وتعريفه، وعندنا هو كذلك فيما يتعلق بعين الماء، غير أن العلة الماء، ثبتت عياناً مرة وتارة دلالة. ذلك ما حكي عن الشافعي رحمه الله: أن الحكم إذا أضيف إلى مسمى بوصف خاص: كان دليلاً على نفيه عند عدم ذلك الوصف. وعندنا: هذا باطل أيضاً. وذلك مثل قول الله تعالى: ﴿وَرَبَائِبُكُمُ الَّتِي فِي حُجُورِكُم مِّن نِّسَائِكُمُ الَّتِي دَخَلْتُم بِهِنَّ﴾ [النساء/23]، إن وصف كون المرأة من نسائنا: يوجب أن لا يثبت عند عدمه، وذلك في الزنا في حرمة المصاهرة. وذلك مثل قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: في خمس من الإبل السائمة شاة. وهذه المسألة بناء على مسألة التعليق بالشرط على مذهبه؛ لأن التعليق عنده يوجب الوجود عند وجوده والعدم عند عدمه، والوصف بمعنى الشرط. بيانه: أن الشرط لما دخل على ما هو موجب، لولا هو صار الشرط.
And they said: That is like his statement, peace be upon him, 'Water is from water': the Ansar understood from that that ghusl is not obligatory due to contact without emission, because of the absence of water. And we said: This is false, and such [reasoning] is frequent in the Book and the Sunnah. Allah Almighty said: 'That is the straight religion, so do not wrong yourselves therein' [al-Tawba 36], and injustice is forbidden at all times. And because it is said to him: If you mean that this ruling is not established in other than what is named by the text, then it is the same with us; for the ruling of the text in other than it is not established by it, but rather by the cause of the text. And if he meant that it is not established in it because the text prevents it, then this is a clear error; for the text does not encompass it, so how could it prevent? And because [the text] is for affirming the ruling in what is named, so how could it necessitate negation while it is its opposite? And the jurists have unanimously agreed on the permissibility of ta‘lil (reasoning by cause). If the specificity of the name had an effect of preventing in other than it, then ta‘lil would contradict the text, and that is false.
As for 'Water is from water': the inference from it was with the definite article, which indicates generality of the genus and its definition, and with us it is likewise concerning the specific water, except that the cause is water, established sometimes by sight and sometimes by indication. That is what is related from al-Shafi‘i, may Allah have mercy on him: that when a ruling is added to a named thing with a specific description, it is evidence for its negation in the absence of that description. And with us: this is also false. That is like Allah Almighty's saying: 'And your step-daughters who are in your custody from your women whom you have entered with' [al-Nisa 23]; indeed, the description of the woman being from our women necessitates that it is not established in its absence, and that applies to zina in the prohibition of affinity. And that is like his statement, peace be upon him: 'In five grazing camels, a sheep.' And this issue is built upon the issue of suspension by condition according to his madhhab; because suspension with him necessitates existence at its existence and non-existence at its non-existence, and description is in the meaning of condition. Its clarification: that when the condition enters upon what is entailed, were it not for it, the condition would become...
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute between the Hanafis and those (like al-Shafi‘i) who argue that when a ruling is attached to a specific description, it implies the negation of the ruling in the absence of that description. The Hanafis refute this principle. The text begins by citing the opponents' example: the hadith 'Water is from water' (i.e., ghusl is required only when water is emitted). The Ansar understood from this that ghusl is not required for mere contact without discharge because there is no water (i.e., emission). The Hanafis respond that this reasoning is false and cite Quran 9:36: 'Do not wrong yourselves therein [in the sacred months]' — injustice is forbidden at all times, not just in those months. The Hanafis then argue: if the opponent means that the ruling (e.g., prohibition of injustice) is not established in other than what is named by the text (e.g., other months), then the Hanafis agree, because the text's ruling cannot be extended without a cause. But if they mean that the text actively prevents the ruling from applying elsewhere, this is wrong because the text does not address those other contexts. The text is there to affirm the ruling in its named context, not to negate it elsewhere. The Hanafis also invoke the consensus of jurists on the validity of ta‘lil (reasoning by cause). If the mere name prevented extension, then analogy (qiyas) would contradict the text.
Next, the hadith 'Water is from water' is analyzed: the definite article (al-) indicates generality of the genus, not a specific restriction. The cause is water (i.e., emission), which can be established by direct observation or by indication (e.g., in sleep). The text then reports al-Shafi‘i's position: when a ruling is attached to a named thing with a specific description, it indicates negation in the absence of that description. The Hanafis reject this absolutely. Two more examples illustrate: Quran 4:23 mentions step-daughters in the custody of wives with whom one has consummated marriage. The description 'from your women whom you have entered with' might seem to imply that step-daughters from a wife not consummated or from zina are not included in the prohibition. The Hanafis argue that the description does not necessarily negate the prohibition in those cases. The second example is the hadith 'In five grazing camels, a sheep' — the description 'grazing' might imply that camels fed at home are exempt from zakat, but the Hanafis do not accept that implication. The Hanafis tie this issue to the concept of suspension by condition (ta‘liq bi’l-shart): according to the opponent (al-Shafi‘i), suspension by condition implies existence when the condition exists and non-existence when it does not. They treat description (wasf) like condition. The Hanafis reject this equation; for them, a description does not necessarily imply negation in its absence.

Key Terms
الوصف الخاص (al-wasf al-khāṣṣ): A specific description or attribute attached to a subject in a ruling, claimed to imply negation of the ruling when absent.
التعليق بالشرط (al-ta‘līq bi’l-sharṭ): Suspension of a ruling upon a condition, where the ruling exists when the condition exists and is absent when it is absent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do the Hanafis reject the inference from the hadith 'Water is from water' as false?
A) Because the hadith is not authentic.
B) Because the verse Quran 9:36 shows that a specific description does not restrict a universal ruling.
C) Because ghusl is not required for emission.
D) Because the Prophet explicitly said otherwise.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafis argue that attaching a ruling to a specific description does not negate it elsewhere; the ruling's extension depends on its cause.

10 min
The Definite Article and the Cause: Hanafi Rebuttal to al-Shafi'i
Linguistic and causal analysis of the hadith 'Water is from water' and refutation of al-Shafi'i's principle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafis consider the definite article in 'water' to indicate the genus and why the description 'grazing' in the zakat hadith does not exempt non-grazing camels.

Core Idea
The definite article indicates the genus; a specific description does not equate to a condition.

This lesson continues the Hanafi refutation by analyzing the hadith 'Water is from water' from a linguistic perspective: the definite article (al-) indicates the genus, not a specific restriction. It then presents al-Shafi'i's principle and rejects it, using the examples of step-daughters and grazing camels to show that descriptions do not automatically negate rulings. Finally, the passage links this issue to the concept of suspension by condition (ta'liq bi'l-shart).

Guided Path
1
Analyze the linguistic form
The definite article in 'al-mā' (water) indicates the genus (istighrāq al-jins). It does not refer to a specific kind of water; it refers to water as a category. Thus, the hadith is not restricting the cause to a specific type.
2
Identify the underlying cause
The cause for ghusl is 'water' in the sense of seminal fluid, which can be established by direct observation (in wakefulness) or by indication (in sleep). The ruling applies whenever the cause exists, regardless of the specific description.
3
State al-Shafi'i's principle
Al-Shafi'i holds that when a ruling is attached to a named thing with a specific description, it indicates the negation of the ruling in the absence of that description.
4
Present the Hanafi counter-examples
Quran 4:23 describes step-daughters 'in your custody from your women whom you have entered with.' The description does not negate the prohibition for step-daughters from a wife not consummated or from zina, because the cause (affinity) may apply. Similarly, the hadith 'In five grazing camels, a sheep' does not imply that non-grazing camels are exempt from zakat.
5
Distinguish description from condition
Al-Shafi'i treats description like a condition (shart). The Hanafis reject this equation. A condition expressly suspends a ruling, while a mere description does not necessarily do so.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالوا: وذلك مثل قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام «الماء من الماء»: فهم الأنصار رضي عنهم من ذلك أن الغُسل لا يجب بالإكسال؛ لعدم الماء. وقلنا نحن: هذا باطل، وذلك كثير في الكتاب والسنة. قال الله تعالى: ﴿ذَلِكَ الدِّينُ الْقَيِّمُ فَلَا تَظْلِمُوا فِيهِنَّ أَنفُسَكُمْ﴾ [التوبة/36]، والظلم حرام في كل وقت. ولأنه يقال له: إن أردت أن هذا الحكم غير ثابت في غير المسمى بالنص فكذلك عندنا؛ لأن حكم النص في غيره لا يثبت به، بل بعلة النص. وإن عنى به أنه لا يثبت فيه بكون النص مانعاً: فهذا غلط ظاهر؛ لأن النص لم يتناوله، فكيف يمنع؟ ولأنه لإيجاب الحكم في المسمى، فكيف يوجب النفي وهو ضده؟ وقد أجمع الفقهاء على جواز التعليل ولو كان لخصوص الاسم أثر بالمنع في غيره: لصار التعليل على مضادة النص، وهو باطل.
وأما: «الماء من الماء»: فإن الاستدلال منها كان بلام المعرفة وهي لاستغراق الجنس وتعريفه، وعندنا هو كذلك فيما يتعلق بعين الماء، غير أن العلة الماء، ثبتت عياناً مرة وتارة دلالة. ذلك ما حكي عن الشافعي رحمه الله: أن الحكم إذا أضيف إلى مسمى بوصف خاص: كان دليلاً على نفيه عند عدم ذلك الوصف. وعندنا: هذا باطل أيضاً. وذلك مثل قول الله تعالى: ﴿وَرَبَائِبُكُمُ الَّتِي فِي حُجُورِكُم مِّن نِّسَائِكُمُ الَّتِي دَخَلْتُم بِهِنَّ﴾ [النساء/23]، إن وصف كون المرأة من نسائنا: يوجب أن لا يثبت عند عدمه، وذلك في الزنا في حرمة المصاهرة. وذلك مثل قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: في خمس من الإبل السائمة شاة. وهذه المسألة بناء على مسألة التعليق بالشرط على مذهبه؛ لأن التعليق عنده يوجب الوجود عند وجوده والعدم عند عدمه، والوصف بمعنى الشرط. بيانه: أن الشرط لما دخل على ما هو موجب، لولا هو صار الشرط.
And they said: That is like his statement, peace be upon him, 'Water is from water': the Ansar understood from that that ghusl is not obligatory due to contact without emission, because of the absence of water. And we said: This is false, and such [reasoning] is frequent in the Book and the Sunnah. Allah Almighty said: 'That is the straight religion, so do not wrong yourselves therein' [al-Tawba 36], and injustice is forbidden at all times. And because it is said to him: If you mean that this ruling is not established in other than what is named by the text, then it is the same with us; for the ruling of the text in other than it is not established by it, but rather by the cause of the text. And if he meant that it is not established in it because the text prevents it, then this is a clear error; for the text does not encompass it, so how could it prevent? And because [the text] is for affirming the ruling in what is named, so how could it necessitate negation while it is its opposite? And the jurists have unanimously agreed on the permissibility of ta‘lil (reasoning by cause). If the specificity of the name had an effect of preventing in other than it, then ta‘lil would contradict the text, and that is false.
As for 'Water is from water': the inference from it was with the definite article, which indicates generality of the genus and its definition, and with us it is likewise concerning the specific water, except that the cause is water, established sometimes by sight and sometimes by indication. That is what is related from al-Shafi‘i, may Allah have mercy on him: that when a ruling is added to a named thing with a specific description, it is evidence for its negation in the absence of that description. And with us: this is also false. That is like Allah Almighty's saying: 'And your step-daughters who are in your custody from your women whom you have entered with' [al-Nisa 23]; indeed, the description of the woman being from our women necessitates that it is not established in its absence, and that applies to zina in the prohibition of affinity. And that is like his statement, peace be upon him: 'In five grazing camels, a sheep.' And this issue is built upon the issue of suspension by condition according to his madhhab; because suspension with him necessitates existence at its existence and non-existence at its non-existence, and description is in the meaning of condition. Its clarification: that when the condition enters upon what is entailed, were it not for it, the condition would become...
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute between the Hanafis and those (like al-Shafi‘i) who argue that when a ruling is attached to a specific description, it implies the negation of the ruling in the absence of that description. The Hanafis refute this principle. The text begins by citing the opponents' example: the hadith 'Water is from water' (i.e., ghusl is required only when water is emitted). The Ansar understood from this that ghusl is not required for mere contact without discharge because there is no water (i.e., emission). The Hanafis respond that this reasoning is false and cite Quran 9:36: 'Do not wrong yourselves therein [in the sacred months]' — injustice is forbidden at all times, not just in those months. The Hanafis then argue: if the opponent means that the ruling (e.g., prohibition of injustice) is not established in other than what is named by the text (e.g., other months), then the Hanafis agree, because the text's ruling cannot be extended without a cause. But if they mean that the text actively prevents the ruling from applying elsewhere, this is wrong because the text does not address those other contexts. The text is there to affirm the ruling in its named context, not to negate it elsewhere. The Hanafis also invoke the consensus of jurists on the validity of ta‘lil (reasoning by cause). If the mere name prevented extension, then analogy (qiyas) would contradict the text.
Next, the hadith 'Water is from water' is analyzed: the definite article (al-) indicates generality of the genus, not a specific restriction. The cause is water (i.e., emission), which can be established by direct observation or by indication (e.g., in sleep). The text then reports al-Shafi‘i's position: when a ruling is attached to a named thing with a specific description, it indicates negation in the absence of that description. The Hanafis reject this absolutely. Two more examples illustrate: Quran 4:23 mentions step-daughters in the custody of wives with whom one has consummated marriage. The description 'from your women whom you have entered with' might seem to imply that step-daughters from a wife not consummated or from zina are not included in the prohibition. The Hanafis argue that the description does not necessarily negate the prohibition in those cases. The second example is the hadith 'In five grazing camels, a sheep' — the description 'grazing' might imply that camels fed at home are exempt from zakat, but the Hanafis do not accept that implication. The Hanafis tie this issue to the concept of suspension by condition (ta‘liq bi’l-shart): according to the opponent (al-Shafi‘i), suspension by condition implies existence when the condition exists and non-existence when it does not. They treat description (wasf) like condition. The Hanafis reject this equation; for them, a description does not necessarily imply negation in its absence.

Key Terms
لام المعرفة (lām al-ma‘rifa): The definite article 'al-' used for the genus (istighrāq al-jins), indicating all individuals of that category.
الوصف الخاص (al-wasf al-khāṣṣ): A specific description or attribute attached to a subject in a ruling, claimed to imply negation of the ruling when absent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafis, why does the description 'grazing' in the hadith 'In five grazing camels, a sheep' not negate zakat on non-grazing camels?
A) Because the hadith is weak.
B) Because the cause for zakat is not grazing but the potential for growth and trade, and the description is not a condition.
C) Because the Prophet specifically said so.
D) Because all camels are subject to zakat.

Key Takeaway
A specific description does not equate to a condition; the cause (illa) is the basis for extending the ruling to other cases.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

The Hanafi Rule on Conjunction (ʿAṭf) and the Dispute with al-Shafiʿi
Usul dispute on the effect of grammatical conjunction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi linguistic principle that conjunction of complete sentences does not necessitate shared rulings, and evaluate the criticism of al-Shafiʿi's contrary interpretation.

Core Idea
Conjunction of complete sentences does not mean shared rulings; partnership only arises from incomplete dependent clauses.

In Hanafi usul, the effect of grammatical conjunction (ʿaṭf) on legal rulings is nuanced. While some argue that joining two sentences with 'and' (wāw) forces them to share the same ruling, the Hanafis distinguish between complete and incomplete sentences. This lesson unpacks that distinction and shows how it applies to conditional statements and Quranic verses, ultimately challenging al-Shafiʿi's approach.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Disputed Usul Rule
Recognize that the passage addresses a dispute: does conjunction (ʿaṭf) of two sentences necessitate a shared legal ruling? Some scholars say yes, based on the idea of partnership (shirkah).
This is a meta-dispute about how to interpret grammatical conjunction in legal texts.
2
State the Hanafi Position and Its Linguistic Basis
The Hanafi position is that conjunction of a complete sentence with another does not force a shared ruling. The key is whether the second sentence is incomplete (al-jumlat al-nāqiṣah) and needs the first to be meaningful. If it is complete, it remains independent.
The Hanafi argument relies on Arabic usage, citing abundant Quranic examples where independent clauses are conjoined without shared rulings.
3
Apply to the Conditional Divorce Example
In the man's statement 'If you enter, you are divorced and my slave is free', the second clause 'my slave is free' is grammatically complete but contextually dependent on the condition. The Hanafi view makes manumission conditional on the entry, not independent.
This shows that even complete sentences can be linked by context, not by grammatical conjunction alone.
4
Analyze the Qadhf Verse (Surah an-Nur 24:4)
The verse commands flogging for false accusers and then, with a wāw, commands not accepting their testimony. The Hanafis see both as penalties dependent on the same condition (the act of qadhf), so they share the conditional ruling despite being grammatically independent.
The passage argues that 'wounding testimony' (جرح الشهادة) is like beating, both as punishments.
5
Examine the Critique of al-Shafiʿi
Al-Shafiʿi is criticized for disconnecting 'and do not accept testimony' from the condition (evidence of connection) and connecting 'and those are the wicked' to the preceding (evidence of disconnection). The Hanafi view sees these as errors.
The strong language ('كل ذلك غلط') indicates the author's certainty in the Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ذلك أن القرآن في النظم يوجب القرآن في الحكم عند بعضهم مثل قول بعضهم في قوله تعالى : اللهم وَأَقِيمُوا الصَّلوةَ وَءَاتُوا الزكوة من البقرة / ۱۱۰ : إن القرآن يوجب أن لا تجب على الصبي الزكاة، وقالوا: لأن العطف يوجب الشركة، واعتبروا بالجملة الناقصة. وقلنا نحن إن عطف الجملة على الجملة في اللغة: لا يوجب الشركة ؛ لأن الشركة إنما وجبت بينهما لافتقار الجملة الناقصة إلى ما تتم به ، فإذا تم بنفسه : لم تجب الشركة إلا فيما يفتقر إليه. وهذا أكثر في كتاب الله تعالى من أن يُحصى. ولهذا قلنا في قول الرجل : إن دخلت الدار فأنتِ طالق وعبدي هذا حر: إن العتق يتعلق بالشرط وإن كان تاماً ؛ لأنه في حكم التعليق قاصر. وعلى هذا قلنا في قول الله تعالى: ﴿فَاجْلِدُوهُمْ ثَمَنِينَ جَلْدَةً وَلَا نَقْبَلُوا هَم شَهدَة أَبدا النور / ٤ : إن قوله تعالى : فاجلدوهم: جزاء، وقوله: ﴿وَلَا نَقْبَلُوا لَمْ شَهَدَةٌ : وإن كان تاماً، ولكنه من حيث إنه يصلح جزاء واحداً: مفتقر إلى الشرط، فجعل ملحقاً بالأول. ألا يُرى أن جرح الشهادة إيلام كالضرب، وألا يُرى أنه فُوض إلى الأئمة. فأما قوله: ﴿ وَأُوْلَيْكَ هُمُ الْفَنسِقُونَ ﴾ النور / ٤ : فلا يصلح جزاء؛ لأن الجزاء ما يُقام ابتداء بولاية الإمام. وأما الحكاية عن حال قائمة : فلا فاعتُبر تمامها بصيغتها، فكانت في حق الجزاء: في حكم الجملة المبتدأة، مثل قوله تعالى: ﴿وَيَمْحُ اللَّهُ الْبَطِلَ الشورى / ٢٤ ، ومثل قوله تعالى: ﴿وَنُقِرُّ فِي الْأَرْحَامِ مَا نَشَاءُ ( الحج / ٥ ، وَيَتُوبُ اللهُ عَلَى مَن يَشَاءُ ) التوبة / ١٥. والشافعي رحمه الله قَطَعَ قولَه : ﴿ وَلَا نَقْبَلُوا لهم النور / ٤ ، مع قيام دليل الاتصال، ووَصَلَ قولَه : وَأَوْلَيكَ هُمُ الْفَسِقُونَ ) النور / ٤ ، بما قبله، مع قيام دليل الانفصال، وكل ذلك غلط.
And from that is that the conjunction in arrangement (nazm) necessitates conjunction in ruling according to some, like the saying of some regarding His statement, the Most High: 'O Allah, establish prayer and give zakah' [al-Baqarah 110]: that the conjunction necessitates that zakah is not obligatory upon a minor, and they said: because the conjunction necessitates partnership, and they considered the incomplete sentence. And we said: Indeed, the conjunction of a complete sentence upon a complete sentence in the language does not necessitate partnership; partnership is only necessitated between them due to the incomplete sentence needing what completes it. So when it is complete by itself, partnership is not necessitated except for that which it depends upon. This is more abundant in the Book of Allah the Exalted than can be counted. And for this we said in the statement of a man: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced and my slave is free': that the manumission is attached to the condition even though it is complete, because it is in the ruling of a deficient attachment. And upon this we said regarding the statement of Allah the Exalted: 'So flog them eighty lashes and do not accept from them testimony ever' [an-Nur 4]: that His statement 'flog them' is the consequence (jaza'), and His statement 'and do not accept from them testimony ever' even though it is complete, but from the perspective that it is valid as a single consequence needing the condition, it is made annexed to the first. Do you not see that wounding testimony is pain like beating? Do you not see that it was referred to the imams? As for His statement 'and those are the wicked' [an-Nur 4]: it is not valid as a consequence, because the consequence is what is established initially by the authority of the imam. And as for a narration about an existing state, its completeness is not considered by its wording, so it is in terms of the consequence in the ruling of an independent sentence, like His statement the Exalted: 'And Allah erases falsehood' [ash-Shura 24], and like His statement 'And We settle in the wombs what We will' [al-Hajj 5], 'And Allah accepts repentance from whomever He wills' [at-Tawbah 15]. And al-Shafiʿi (may Allah have mercy on him) cut off His statement 'and do not accept from them' [an-Nur 4] despite evidence of connection, and connected His statement 'and those are the wicked' with what preceded despite evidence of disconnection, and all of that is wrong.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage addresses a key usul dispute regarding the effect of grammatical conjunction (ʿaṭf) on legal rulings. Some scholars argue that when two sentences are conjoined by 'and' (al-wāw), they share the same ruling. For example, in the verse 'أقيموا الصلاة وآتوا الزكاة' (establish prayer and give zakah), they infer that since prayer is obligatory on all, zakah must also be obligatory on all, including minors? Actually, they argue that the conjunction necessitates that zakah is not obligatory on the minor. The Hanafis respond by distinguishing between complete and incomplete sentences. If a sentence is complete in itself, conjunction does not force a shared ruling; only if the second sentence is incomplete and depends on the first does it share the ruling. This principle is applied to a man's conditional statement: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced and my slave is free.' The manumission is also contingent on the condition, even though the sentence 'my slave is free' is complete, because it is effectively a dependent clause in the context of the condition. Similarly, in the verse of qadhf (false accusation of adultery), both the flogging and the rejection of testimony are penalties that depend on the condition (the accusation itself), so they are considered together. Al-Shafiʿi is criticized for severing the connection where it should be connected and connecting where it should be severed.

Key Terms
العطف (al-ʿaṭf): Grammatical conjunction, particularly the use of the wāw or other particles to join words or sentences.
الجملة الناقصة (al-jumlat al-nāqiṣah): An incomplete sentence that requires another sentence to complete its meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, when does a conjunction of two sentences require a shared ruling?
A) Always, because the wāw implies partnership.
B) Only when the second sentence is incomplete and depends on the first.
C) Only when both sentences are imperative.
D) Never, because each sentence is independent.

Key Takeaway
Conjunction of complete sentences does not force shared rulings; only contextual dependence does.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Conditional Sentences and the Shafi'i Dispute on Ta'liq
Explains third and fourth sentence types with usul dispute on ta'liq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on the effect of conditional suspension (ta'liq) and apply the distinction to derive rulings on divorce, emancipation, and vows.

Core Idea
For Hanafis, a condition does not create non-existence; it only prevents causation.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul examines the third and fourth types of conditional sentences in Arabic, then pivots to a foundational usul dispute: does a conditional suspension (ta'liq bi al-shart) actively create a state of non-existence until the condition is met? The Hanafi school answers no—the suspended matter remains on its original non-existence—while al-Shafi'i holds that it does. This disagreement has significant practical consequences across multiple fiqh chapters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sentence types
Distinguish the third type (response to a previous statement) from the fourth type (independent start with extra wording). Note how the author clarifies that extra wording may be considered a beginning to avoid nullification, or a confirmation if intended as response.
The examples use conditional emancipations (ta'liq al-'itq) to illustrate.
2
Grasp the Hanafi principle
The core Hanafi principle: 'العندنا أن العدم لم يثبت به، بل بقي المعلّق على أصل العدم.' The condition does not establish non-existence; the suspended matter remains on its original non-existence. The condition merely prevents the suspended matter from being a cause (sabab).
This is tied to the concept of 'in'iqaad' (formation) – the condition blocks formation.
3
Contrast with Shafi'i view
Al-Shafi'i holds that the condition is a deferrer (mu'akhkhir) and establishes non-existence until fulfillment. This leads him to invalidate suspending divorce or emancipation on future ownership, and to permit hastening a conditional vow or expiation.
The Shafi'i cites Quran 4:25 to support his view: suspending permission on non-existence of ability implies the opposite when ability exists.
4
Examine the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response, though implicit in this passage, is that the condition does not create non-existence but only prevents the cause from being effective. The Quranic verse is interpreted as permission based on inability, not on the condition per se.
The tarjih (preferred view) is Hanafi, as indicated by 'عندنا'.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequences: Shafi'i invalidates suspending divorce or emancipation on future ownership (e.g., 'if you become my wife, you are divorced'), Hanafi allows it. Shafi'i permits hastening a conditional vow (e.g., paying charity before the condition occurs), Hanafi does not.
Understanding this dispute helps in analyzing conditional contracts and vows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثالث: فمثل قول الرجل لرجل: تغدَّ معي، فيقول الآخر: إن تغديت فعبدي حر: إنه يتعلق به.
و
كذلك إذا قيل: إنك تغتسل الليلة في هذه الدار من جنابة، فقال: إن اغتسلت فعبدي حرِّ : فهذا خرج جواباً ، فتضمن إعادة السؤال الذي سبق، وقد يحتمل الابتداء.
ولو قال : إن اغتسلتُ الليلةَ، أو في هذه الدار فعبدي حر: صار مبتدأ ؛ احترازاً عن إلغاء الزيادة.
فإن عنى به الجواب : صُدَّق فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى، فتصير الزيادة توكيداً، وأمثلته كثيرة. - ومن ذلك : أن الشافعي رحمه الله جعل التعليق بالشرط يوجب العدم. وعندنا أن العدم لم يثبت به، بل بقي المعلّق على أصل العدم. وحاصله : أن المعلّق بالشرط عندنا لا ينعقد سبباً، وإنما الشرط يمنع الانعقاد.
وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : هو مؤخِّرُ، ولذلك أبطل تعليق الطلاق والعتاق بالملك، وجوز تعجيل النذر المعلَّق، وجوز تعجيل كفارة اليمين. وقال في قول الله تعالى: ﴿ وَمَن لَّمْ يَسْتَطِعْ مِنكُمْ طَوْلًا أَن يَنكِحَ الْمُحْصَنَتِ الْمُؤْمِنَاتِ النساء /٢٥ : إن تعليق الجواز بعدم طول الحرة: يوجب الفساد عند وجوده.
And as for the third: example is a man saying to a man: 'Have lunch with me,' and the other says: 'If I have lunch, my slave is free' – it is attached to it.
Similarly, if it is said: 'You are bathing tonight in this house from janaba,' and he says: 'If I bathe, my slave is free' – this comes as a response, so it implies repeating the preceding question, and it may admit being a beginning.
And if he says: 'If I bathe tonight, or in this house, my slave is free' – it becomes a beginning, to avoid nullifying the addition.
If he intended it as a response, he is believed between him and Allah, and the addition becomes a confirmation, and its examples are many. And from that: al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) held that suspension by a condition necessitates non-existence. And according to us, non-existence is not established by it; rather the suspended remains on its original non-existence. In essence: the suspended by condition according to us does not become a cause; rather the condition prevents causation.
And al-Shafi'i said: it is a deferrer, and therefore he invalidated suspending divorce and emancipation on ownership, and permitted hastening a suspended vow, and permitted hastening the expiation of an oath. And he said regarding the saying of Allah: {And whoever among you does not have the means to marry believing free women} (an-Nisa 4:25): that suspending permission on the non-existence of a free woman's means necessitates its opposite (corruption) when it exists.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage explains the third and fourth types of sentences. The third type is a sentence that comes as an answer to a previous statement, like 'If I have lunch, my slave is free' in response to an invitation. It implies the question and is attached to it. The fourth type occurs when extra words are added beyond the answer, making it an independent start, e.g., adding 'tonight' or 'in this house' to a conditional; if intended as a response, the addition is considered a confirming repetition. The passage then introduces a major usul dispute with Shafi'i: Shafi'i holds that a conditional suspension (ta'liq bi al-shart) creates non-existence ('adam) until the condition is met. For Hanafis, the suspended matter remains in its original non-existence; the condition does not establish non-existence but rather prevents causation. Thus, for Shafi'i, the condition is a deferrer (mu'akhkhir), so he invalidates suspending divorce or emancipation on ownership (since ownership is not yet present), but allows hastening a conditional vow or expiation. He cites Quran 4:25 to argue that if permission is suspended on the absence of ability, then when ability exists, the opposite (prohibition) should apply. The Hanafi response is implied in the earlier assertion that the condition does not cause non-existence but prevents.

Key Terms
التعليق بالشرط (al-ta'liq bi al-shart): Suspending a legal consequence on the occurrence of a future condition.
العدم (al-'adam): Non-existence of the legal effect before the condition is met.
السبب (al-sabab): Cause that brings about a legal effect. The suspended matter does not become a cause in Hanafi view.
الانعقاد (al-in'iqaad): Formation or validity of a legal act. The condition prevents formation in Hanafi view.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view in the passage, what is the legal status of a matter suspended on a condition?
A) It is considered non-existent due to the condition.
B) It remains on its original non-existence.
C) It becomes a cause immediately.
D) It is deferred to when the condition is fulfilled.

Key Takeaway
For Hanafis, a condition does not establish non-existence; it only prevents the suspended matter from becoming a cause until the condition is met.

The Requirement of a Subject in Suspended Obligations (Ta'liq)
Analyzing ta'liq and its shubha with proof and dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that a suspended obligation (ta'liq) requires the existence of its subject (mahall) due to its shubha with iwab, and evaluate Imam Zufar's counter-argument.

Core Idea
Suspended talaq requires a wife; the shubha of obligation binds the ruling to a subject.

This lesson explores a foundational usul principle: why the Hanafi school insists that a suspended divorce (ta'liq) requires the existence of the wife (mahall) at the time the condition is set. The reasoning rests on a subtle analogy with stolen goods and the concept of shubha (semblance) to obligation. Imam Zufar's objection challenges this, and resolving the dispute reveals the deeper structure of Hanafi istidlal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The passage addresses whether a suspended obligation (e.g., 'If I enter, my wife is divorced') requires the existence of its subject (the wife) for validity, even when the original obligation is nullified.
This is a dispute within the Hanafi school between the majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) and Imam Zufar.
2
Understand the Hanafi position and proof
The majority holds that the subject is required because ta'liq has a shubha (semblance) to iwab (obligation). Just as a stolen item must be returned or its value guaranteed—both requiring a subject—so too the obligation of divorce requires a wife.
This is a qiyas jaliy (clear analogy).
3
Examine Imam Zufar's objection
Zufar argues that when the original iwab is nullified, the stipulation for the subject's existence is dropped. He cites the case: if one swears by triple divorce and then actually divorces three times, the oath is not broken, showing the divorce counts without continuing ownership.
Zufar's proof is based on a different example.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response distinguishes between the nullification of the oath and the nature of ta'liq. The existence of ownership at the time of the condition weights the ruling, even if ownership later ends. The analogy of ta'liq upon marriage after triple divorce shows that the subject (marriage) is required, not its continuation.
This counterexample is explicitly cited from the Book of Divorce.
5
Evaluate the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
The dispute affects rulings like hastening a suspended vow: the Hanafi school prohibits early performance because the obligation is not yet due; Zufar's view might allow it under certain conditions.
Understanding this helps apply the principle in fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يصير غير لازم بأدنى الخطرين فكان أولى، وأما هذا فيحتمل الخطر، فوجب القولُ بكمال التعليق في هذا الباب. وأما الحكم فإن من حلف لا يبيع فباع بشرط الخيار : حنث. ولو حلف لا يطلق، فحلف بالطلاق: لم يحنث. وإذا بطلت العُلْقة : صار ذلك الإيجاب علةً، كأنه ابتداء، ولهذا صح تعليق الطلاق والعتاق قبل الملك به. ولهذا لم يجز تعجيل النذر المعلّق، وتعجيل الكفارة، وهو كالكفارة بالصوم. وفَرْقُه : باطل؛ لأنا قد بينا أن حق الله في المال: فعل الأداء، لا عين المال، إنما يُقصد عين المال في حقوق العباد، وأما في حقوق الله تعالى: فلا فإنما العبادة فعل لا مال وإنما المال آلتُه. وقال زفر رحمه الله : ولما بطل الإيجاب: لم يُشترط قيام المحل لبقائه، فإذا حلف بالطلاق الثلاث، ثم طلقها ثلاثاً: لم تبطل اليمين، وكذلك العتق. وإنما شرط قيام الملك ؛ لأن حال وجود الشرط متردد، فوجب الترجيح بالحال، فإذا وقع الترجيح بالملك في الحال: صار زوال الحل في المستقبل، من حيث إنه لا ينافي وجوده عند وجود الشرط لا محالة، وزوال الملك في المستقبل سواء. ألا يُرى أن التعليق بالنكاح يجوز وإن كان الحل للحال معدوماً، فلو كان التعليق يتصل بالمحل : لما صح تعليق الطلاق في حق المطلقة ثلاثاً بنكاحها. وطريق أصحابنا لا يصح إلا أن يثبت للمعلق ضَرْبُ اتصال بمحله؛ ليُشترط قيام محله. وأما قيام هذا الملك : فلم يتعين؛ لما بينا أنه ليس بتصرف في الطلاق ليصح باعتبار الملك، والطريق في ذلك أن تعليق الطلاق له شُبهةٌ : بالإيجاب. وبيانه: أن اليمين تُعقد للبر، ولا بد من كون البر مضموناً؛ ليصير واجب الرعاية، فإذا حلف بالطلاق كان البرَّ هو الأصل، وهو مضمون بالطلاق كالمغصوب يلزمه ردُّه ، ويكون مضموناً بالقيمة، فتثبت شبهة وجوب القيمة، فكذلك ها هنا تثبت شبهةً وجوب الطلاق، وقدر ما يجب: لا يُستغنى عن محله. فأما تعليق الطلاق بالنكاح : فتعليق بما هو عله ملك الطلاق، فيصير قدر ما ادعينا من الشبهة مستحقاً به فتسقط هذه الشبهة بهذه المعارضة ومسألة تعليق الطلاق بالنكاح بعد الثلاث: منصوصةٌ في كتاب الطلاق. وفي «الجامع» أيضاً نص في نظيره، وهو العتاق.
It becomes non-binding with the slightest of the two risks, so it is more appropriate. As for this, it may involve risk, so it is necessary to say that the suspension is complete in this chapter. As for the ruling: whoever swore not to sell and sold with a conditional option, he breaks his oath. If he swore not to divorce, then [swore] by divorce, he does not break his oath. When the connection is nullified, that obligation becomes a cause, as if it were a beginning, and therefore the suspension of divorce and manumission before ownership is valid. Therefore it is not permissible to hasten the suspended vow, nor to hasten the expiation, and it is like expiation by fasting. His distinction is invalid; because we have clarified that the right of Allah in wealth is the act of payment, not the wealth itself; wealth is intended only in the rights of people. As for the rights of Allah Almighty, worship is an act, not wealth; wealth is merely its instrument. Zufar said: When the obligation is nullified, the existence of the subject is not stipulated for its continuation. If he swears by triple divorce and then divorces her three times, the oath is not nullified, and similarly for manumission. The stipulation of the existence of ownership is because the state of the condition's existence is uncertain, so a preponderance is required by the current state. When preponderance is given by current ownership, the future dissolution becomes such that it does not negate its existence at the time of the condition's occurrence inevitably, and the future loss of ownership is equivalent. Do you not see that suspension upon marriage is permissible even though dissolution is currently nonexistent? If the suspension were connected to the subject, it would not be valid to suspend divorce regarding a woman divorced three times upon her marriage. The way of our companions is not valid unless the suspended thing is proven to have a type of connection to its subject, so that the existence of its subject is stipulated. As for the existence of this ownership, it is not determined; because it is not a disposition in divorce to be valid by virtue of ownership. The method in this is that suspending divorce has a similarity to obligation. Its clarification: The oath is made for righteousness, and righteousness must be guaranteed to become obligatory to observe. If he swears by divorce, righteousness is the original, and it is guaranteed by divorce, like a stolen object that must be returned and is guaranteed by its value. Thus a similarity to the obligation of value is established. Similarly here, a similarity to the obligation of divorce is established, and the amount that is obligatory cannot do without its subject. As for suspending divorce upon marriage, it is a suspension upon what is the cause of the power of divorce, so the amount of similarity we claimed becomes due by it, and this similarity is cancelled by this counterargument. The issue of suspending divorce upon marriage after triple divorce is explicitly stated in the Book of Divorce. In Al-Jami', there is also a text on its analog, which is manumission.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on the nature of suspended obligations (ta'liq) in Hanafi usul, particularly regarding divorce and manumission. The author begins by stating that ta'liq is complete and binding even with minimal risk, unlike certain other types of obligations. He provides examples: if someone swears not to sell but sells with an option, the oath is broken because the sale is valid; if someone swears not to divorce but then uses the words of divorce as an oath, the oath is not broken because the divorce is not actually initiated. When the connection (al-ulqa) is nullified, the original obligation becomes a new cause. This explains why ta'liq of divorce or manumission before ownership is valid. He then explains a distinction: it is not permissible to hasten a suspended vow or its expiation, similar to expiation by fasting, because the obligation is not yet due. The author refutes the distinction by stating that rights of Allah in matters of wealth are about the act of payment, not the wealth itself, whereas in rights of people, the wealth itself is intended. Zufar's view is presented: he argues that if the obligation is nullified, the existence of its subject (mahall) is not required. For example, if someone swears by triple divorce and then actually performs triple divorce, the oath is not broken because the divorce counts. The Hanafi response is that the existence of ownership is required because the condition's occurrence is uncertain, and current ownership weighs in, even if future ownership disappears. They support this with the example of ta'liq upon marriage being valid even though dissolution is not currently possible. The author then explains the method: The suspended thing (mutallaq) must have a connection to its subject; otherwise the existence of the subject is not stipulated. However, ownership is not a direct disposition in divorce, so the approach is different: suspended divorce has a shubha (similarity) to obligation (iwab). The analogy: an oath is made for righteousness (birr), and righteousness is guaranteed; when sworn by divorce, righteousness is guaranteed by divorce, similar to how a stolen item is guaranteed by its value. Thus a shubha of the obligation of divorce is established, and this obligation requires a subject (i.e., the wife). When divorce is suspended upon marriage, which is the cause of the power of divorce, this shubha is satisfied, and the Shubha cancels out. This is why it is valid to suspend divorce upon marriage after triple divorce, as stated in the classical texts.

Key Terms
تعليق (ta‘līq): A suspension of a legal obligation (like divorce or manumission) upon a future condition. It has a shubha to iwab (obligation).
شبهة (shubha): A similarity or semblance that causes a legal rule to apply. Here, the shubha is between the obligation of divorce and the guarantee of value in a stolen item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it not permissible to hasten a suspended vow (نذر معلق)?
A) Because the vow is nullified if performed early
B) Because the obligation is not yet due until the condition occurs
C) Because the vow involves a right of Allah that must be performed at a specific time
D) Because the suspended vow is like an oath that cannot be fulfilled before its time

Key Takeaway
Suspended obligations in the Hanafi school require the existence of their subject because of a shubha (semblance) to a guarantee obligation, which is proven by analogy with stolen goods.

Binding Nature of Commenced Nafil
Defines nafil and argues it becomes binding once started

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on commenced nafil, analyze the Shafi'i counterargument, and apply the Hanafi qiyas to a vow (nadhr) to derive the binding ruling.

Core Idea
Once a supererogatory act is started, it becomes a right of Allah that must be completed.

Supererogatory (nafl) acts are generally optional, but once you start one, do you have to finish it? The Hanafi school says yes, and this passage defends that view against Imam al-Shafi'i, who says no. The argument hinges on the idea that the performed portion becomes a 'right of Allah' that cannot be thrown away, and that this is analogous to a vow (nadhr). Let's work through the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the two positions
The core debate: Is a commenced nafil act binding to complete? State the Hanafi view (binding, wajib) and the Shafi'i view (remains non-binding, can stop anytime).
The Hanafi position is based on ihtiyat (precaution) in worship.
2
Parse al-Shafi'i's argument
Al-Shafi'i says: nafl is defined as non-obligatory, and starting does not change its nature. The portion performed becomes void in ruling (like conjecture). Identify the key premise here.
Opposing dalil: nafl's definition precludes bindingness.
3
Follow the Hanafi response
The Hanafis argue that the performed act becomes a surrendered right of Allah, which is inviolable and cannot be destroyed. Therefore, the remaining part must be completed to protect that right.
Two conflicting matters: performed vs. unperformed. Preference given by ihtiyat.
4
Understand the analogy to vow (nadhr)
The text compares commenced nafl to a nadhr: just as uttering 'for Allah' creates an obligation, commencing an act of worship creates a binding commitment. Trace how the analogy works.
The analogy strengthens the Hanafi claim that commencement creates obligation.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafi says if you start a voluntary prayer and break it, you must make it up (or perform sajdah al-sahw). Shafi'i says you may leave without penalty.
This illustrates the real-world impact of the dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النفل : فما يُثاب المرء على فعله، ولا يعاقب على تركه. ولذلك قلنا: إن ما زاد على القصر من صلاة السفر: نفل، والنفل شرع دائماً ، فلذلك جعلناه من العزائم، ولذلك صح قاعداً وراكباً؛ لأنه على ما شُرع يلازم العجز ، لا محالة، فلازَمَ اليُسر، وهذا القدر من جنس الرخص. وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : لمَّا شُرعُ النفل على هذا الوصف: وجب أن يبقى كذلك غير لازم وقد غيّرتُم أنتم، حيث قلتُم باللزوم، وقلتُ أنا : إن ما لم يفعل بعد فهو فيه، فبطل المؤدى حكماً له، كالمظنون. وقلنا نحن إن ما أداه فقد صار لغيره مسلّما إليه، وحق غيره محترم، مضمون عليه إتلافه، ولا سبيل إليه إلا بإلزام الباقي، وهما أمران متعارضان، أعني : المؤدَّى، وغير المؤدَّى، فوجب الترجيح لما قلنا بالاحتياط في العبادات. وهو كالنذر: صار الله تعالى تسميةً لا فعلاً، ثم وجب؛ لصيانته ابتداء الفعل، فلأن يجب لصيانة ابتداء الفعل : بقاؤه أولى. والسنن كثيرةٌ في باب الصلاة والحج وغير ذلك.
As for supererogatory (nafl): it is that for which a person is rewarded for doing it, and not punished for leaving it. Therefore we said: whatever exceeds the shortening (qasr) of the travel prayer is nafl, and nafl is legislated perpetually, so we made it among the 'aza'im (unshakable determinations), and therefore it is valid while sitting and riding; because it was legislated on the basis of accompanying inability inevitably, so it accompanies ease, and this amount is of the kind of concessions (rukhas). And al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, said: Since nafl was legislated on this description, it must remain as such, non-binding, but you have changed it by asserting bindingness. I say: what has not been performed remains in its original state, so the performed portion becomes void in its ruling, like conjecture (maznun). And we said: what he has performed has become surrendered to another (i.e., a right of Allah), and the right of another is inviolable, and its destruction is guaranteed, and there is no way to it except by obligating the remaining portion. These are two conflicting matters – the performed and the unperformed – so preference (tarjih) becomes necessary for what we said, based on precaution (ihtiyat) in acts of worship. And it is like a vow (nadhr): the name (of Allah) is uttered, not the action, then it becomes obligatory to safeguard the commencement of the action. So if it becomes obligatory to safeguard the commencement, its continuation is even more due. And the sunan (supererogatory acts) are many in the chapters of prayer, hajj, and elsewhere.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i concerning the legal nature of supererogatory acts (nafl) once commenced. The author first defines nafl as an act rewarded but not punished for omission, then explains that Hanafis consider nafl a type of 'azimah (determination) because it is legislated perpetually and remains valid even when performed with concessions (like riding in travel). Al-Shafi'i argues that since nafl is not originally obligatory, it should remain so; starting it does not make completion obligatory, and the performed part becomes void in its rule. The Hanafi response invokes the concept of rights: the act once performed becomes a right of Allah that cannot be destroyed, so the remaining part must be completed to preserve it. They analogize it to a vow (nadhr): just as uttering a vow creates an obligation, commencing an act of worship creates a binding commitment. This reasoning uses the principle of precaution (ihtiyat) in worship. The passage concludes by noting that many sunan exist in prayer, hajj, and other acts.

Key Terms
النفل (al-nafl): Supererogatory act: rewarded for doing, not punished for leaving.
العزائم (al-'aza'im): Acts of determination that become binding despite original concession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what makes a supererogatory act binding?
A) The intention before starting
B) The commencement of the act itself
C) A vow (nadhr) uttered beforehand
D) The absence of a concession

Key Takeaway
Commencing a supererogatory worship creates a right of Allah that requires completion, analogous to a vow.

Categories of Rukhsah (Legal Concession) in Hanafi Usul
Classifying the four types of rukhsah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four categories of rukhsah in Hanafi usul, distinguish between genuine and metaphorical rukhsah, and apply the Hanafi preference for 'azimah over rukhsah in the second category.

Core Idea
Rukhsah is a concession from the original rule; some concessions are genuine, others metaphorical, and the Hanafi school prefers resolution over deferment.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul presents a systematic classification of rukhsah (legal concession) into four categories. The classification hinges on whether the original ruling remains operative, is deferred, or is abrogated. Understanding this taxonomy is essential for applying concessions correctly in Hanafi jurisprudence, especially when different schools disagree on the preference between 'azimah and rukhsah, as with the case of the fasting traveler.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories
Read the passage and note the four distinct types of rukhsah: (1) necessity-based concessions where the cause is immediate, (2) concessions with a deferred cause (e.g., traveler's fast), (3) metaphorical concessions involving complete abrogation (naskh), and (4) mixed concessions where a ruling is dropped in practice but remains legislated in principle (e.g., salam).
Categories 1 and 2 are 'true' rukhsah; 3 and 4 are 'metaphorical'.
2
Examine the second category and the ikhtilaf
Focus on the traveler's fast: the cause (travel) exists but its ruling is deferred. The Hanafi position holds that 'azimah (fasting) is superior, while al-Shafi'i holds that rukhsah (breaking) is superior. Analyze the reasoning: Hanafis point to the perfection of the cause for 'azimah and hesitation in the license; Shafi'i relies on the apparent deferment.
The thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafi mufti advises a strong traveler to fast; Shafi'i advises to break.
3
Understand metaphorical rukhsah (third category)
The third category is pure naskh—rulings like 'isr and aghlal (heavy burdens from previous nations) are completely removed. This is called rukhsah only metaphorically because no original ruling remains.
Example: the lifting of the obligation to fast continuously or avoid certain foods.
4
Analyze the mixed fourth category
The fourth category includes rulings like salam (advance sale). The general rule of sale requiring immediate exchange remains legislated, but it is dropped for salam due to ease. Thus, it is partly metaphorical (dropped) and partly genuine (general rule remains).
This is below the third category in metaphorical strength.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك هذا فيمن أُكره على إتلاف مال غيره : رخص له؛ لرجحان حقه في النفس، فإذا صبر حتى قتل : كان شهيداً؛ لقيام الحرمة، وهو حق العبد. - وكذلك إذا أصابته مَخمَصَةٌ ، فصَبَر عن مال غيره حتى مات. - وكذلك صائم أكره على الفطر. ومُحرِمٌ أكره على جناية، وما أشبه ذلك من العبادات، والحقوق المحترمة، وأمثلته كثيرة. ٢- وأما القسم الثاني : فما يُستباحُ بعُذر مع قيام السبب موجباً لحكمه، غير أن الحكم متراخ. مثل المسافر رُخص له أن يُفطر ؛ بناءً على سبب متراخ حكمه، فكان دون ما اعترض على سببٍ حَلّ حكمه. وإنما تكمل الرخصة بكمال العزيمة لكن السبب لما تراخى حكمه من غير تعليق: كان القول بالتراخي بعد تمام السبب رخصةً، فأبيح له الفطر، وكانت العزيمة أولى عندنا؛ لكمال سببه، ولتردُّد في الرخصة، حتى صارت العزيمة تؤدي معنى الرخصة من وجه، فلذلك تمت العزيمة، على ما نبين في آخر هذا الفصل إن شاء الله تعالى. وقد أعرض الشافعي رحمه الله عن ذلك، فجعل الرخصة أولى؛ اعتباراً لظاهر تراخي العزيمة، إلا أن يُضعفَه الصوم: فليس له أن يبذل نفسه لإقامة الصوم ؛ لأنه قتيلاً بالصوم، فيصير قاتلاً نفسه بما صار يصير به مجاهداً، وفي ذلك تغيير المشروع، فلم يكن نظير من بذل نفسه لقتل الظالم حتى أقام الصوم حقاً الله تعالى؛ لأن القتل مضاف إلى الظالم، فلم يصر الصابر مغيراً للمشروع، فصار به مجاهداً. وأما أتم نوعي المجاز: فما وضع عنا من الإصر والأغلال، فإن ذلك يسمى رخصةً مجازاً؛ لأن الأصل ساقط لم يبق مشروعاً، فلم يكن رخصةً إلا مجازاً، من حيث هو نسخ تمحض تخفيفاً. -٤- وأما القسم الرابع: فما سقط عن العباد مع كونه مشروعاً في الجملة. فمن حيث سقط أصلاً كان مجازاً، ومن حيث بقي مشروعاً في الجملة : كان شبيهاً بحقيقة الرخصة، فكان دون القسم الثالث. مثاله : ما روي أن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام رخص في السلم، وذلك لأن أصل البيع أن يُلاقي عيناً موجودةً، وهذا حكم باق مشروع، لكنه سقط في باب السلم أصلاً؛ تخفيفاً، حتى لم يبق تعيينه في السلم مشروعاً، ولا عزيمة، وهذا لأن دليل اليُسر متعيّن؛ لوقوع العجز عن التعيين، فوضع عنه أصلاً. (١) حديث: أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم رخص في السلم : قال القرطبي شرح مسلم : ومما يدل على اشتراط الأجل في السلم الحديث الذي قال فيه : نهى : رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عن بيع ما ليس عندك ، وأرخص في السلم». انتهى. ولم أقف عليه هكذا، وعندي أنه مركب، فحديث النهي: هو حديث حكيم بن حزام، وحديث الرخصة: هو حديث ابن عباس قال: قَدِمَ رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم المدينة وهم يُسلفون في الثمار السَّنَةَ ،والسنتين، فقال: من أسلف في تمر: فليسلف في كيل معلوم، ووزن معلوم إلى أجل معلوم». رواه الجماعة.
And likewise this in the case of one who is coerced to destroy the property of another: a license is given to him due to the preponderance of his right in his own life, so if he endures until he is killed, he is a martyr, due to the inviolability [of life] and it is a right of the servant. And likewise when a severe famine befalls him, and he refrains from [taking] another's property until he dies. And likewise a fasting person coerced to break his fast, and a person in ihram coerced to commit an offence, and what resembles that among acts of worship and inviolable rights; and their examples are many. 2- As for the second category: it is what is rendered permissible by an excuse while the cause is operative as a legal effective factor for its ruling, except that the ruling is deferred. For example, the traveler is given license to break his fast, based on a cause whose ruling is deferred, so it is less than what intervenes on a cause whose ruling is immediate. And the license is only perfected with the perfection of the resolution, but since the cause's ruling is deferred without being suspended, the statement of deferment after the completion of the cause is a license; thus he is permitted to break his fast, and resolution is superior according to us, due to the perfection of its cause and due to the hesitation in the license, so much so that the resolution comes to convey the meaning of license in one respect, therefore the resolution is perfected, as we shall explain at the end of this chapter, God willing. And al-Shafi'i, may God have mercy on him, turned away from that, and made the license superior, considering the apparent deferment of the resolution, except if fasting weakens him: then he should not devote himself to performing the fast because he would be killed by fasting, thus becoming a killer of himself through that by which he would become a mujahid, and in that is a change of what is legislated, so it is not analogous to one who devotes himself to killing the oppressor until he performs the fast as a right of God Almighty; because the killing is attributed to the oppressor, so the patient one does not become a changer of the legislated, and thereby becomes a mujahid. And as for the most complete of the two types of metaphorical [license]: it is what was removed from us of burdens and shackles, for that is called a metaphorical license, because the original [ruling] is abrogated and no longer legislated, so it is not a license except metaphorically, in that it is an abrogation that is purely a lightening. 4- As for the fourth category: it is what is dropped from the servants while it is legislated in general. Insofar as it is dropped entirely it is metaphorical, and insofar as it remains legislated in general it resembles a true license, so it is below the third category. Its example is what is narrated that the Prophet, peace be upon him, granted a license in salam [advance sale], and that is because the original rule of sale is that it meets an existing specific object, and this ruling remains legislated, but it is dropped entirely in the chapter of salam as a lightening, so much so that its specification in salam no longer remains legislated, nor is there a resolution, and this is because the evidence of ease is determined, due to the inability to specify, so it was dropped entirely. (1) Hadith: The Prophet, peace be upon him, granted a license in salam: al-Qurtubi in Sharh Muslim said: Among that which indicates the condition of the term in salam is the hadith in which he said: 'The Messenger of God, peace be upon him, forbade selling what is not with you, and he granted a license in salam.' End. I have not come across it like this, and in my view it is composite: the hadith of prohibition is the hadith of Hakim ibn Hizam, and the hadith of license is the hadith of Ibn Abbas who said: The Messenger of God, peace be upon him, came to Medina and they were advancing payment for fruits for one year and two years, so he said: 'Whoever advances payment for dates, let him advance payment for a known measure, a known weight, and a known term.' Narrated by the group.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul presents the continuation of the discussion on rukhsah (legal concession) after covering the first category. The author first gives examples of the first category (not explicitly named here): cases where a person is coerced to destroy property, or faces extreme hunger, or is forced to break a fast, etc. In these, the license is given due to necessity. Then he moves to the second category: situations where the cause for a ruling exists but the ruling is deferred (mutaraakhi). The example is the traveler's license to break his fast: the cause (travel as a hardship) exists, but its ruling (permission to break) is not immediate; it is a deferred permission. The Hanafi position is that in such cases, the 'azimah (the original ruling of fasting) is superior to taking the license, because the cause is not fully operative. Al-Shafi'i disagrees and prefers the license, considering the deferment as a sign of weakness. However, the Hanafis argue that if fasting would cause serious harm, it is not allowed to persist, as that would be self-destruction. Then the third category: metaphorical rukhsah, which is the complete removal of burdens like the 'isr and aghlal (heavy obligations from previous nations). This is called rukhsah only metaphorically because the original ruling is completely abrogated (naskh), not merely a concession within a continuing obligation. The fourth category: rulings that are dropped in practice while remaining legislated in principle. The example is salam (advance sale), where the normal requirement of immediate exchange of goods is dropped due to ease, but the general validity of sale remains. This is between true and metaphorical rukhsah. The footnote discusses the hadith evidence for salam.

Key Terms
عزيمة (ʻazīmah): The original, unexempted ruling; the full obligation or prohibition without concession.
رخصة (rukhsah): A legal concession granted due to an excuse, either genuine (haqiqi) or metaphorical (majazi), where the original ruling is either deferred, modified, or abrogated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, in the case of a traveler who is able to fast without harm, which is preferable: to fast (ʻazīmah) or to break the fast (rukhsah)?
A) To fast (ʻazīmah)
B) To break the fast (rukhsah)
C) Both are equal
D) It depends on the traveler's intention

Key Takeaway
Rukhsah in Hanafi usul is not a single category; it ranges from true concessions where the original rule remains to metaphorical concessions where the original is abrogated, and the Hanafi school generally prefers 'azimah when the cause is not immediate.
